Summary (English)
Smart materials have the capability of changing form and properties, similar to
your muscles, dependent on the electric voltage, magnetic ﬁeld or induced temperature
changes (signal processing and control inputs). Smart materials have, so far, not
been used in any industrial applications relative to bearing technology, but they have
great potential. The main research goal is to investigate, theoretically as well as
experimentally, the feasibility of applying smart materials to gas bearings (clean,
environment-friendly and sustainable technology) with the aim of improving the
bearing characteristics, i.e. higher load capacity, less energy losses, capability of running
faster (increase in production) without vibration instabilities. Smart gas bearing
technology can directly be applied to small turbines, turbochargers, compressors (air
conditioning), heat pumping systems in their compact forms, leading to more efﬁcient
machinery which will reduce energy consumption in production processes.
Generally, smart materials can be used as sensors or actuators. While sensors
are used to provide system response, then are actuators used to improve bearing
characteristics through input control. This thesis focuses on the use of piezoelectric
ceramics, which is well established in many industries and have equally large potential
in bearing technology due to its fast acting dynamics. By using piezoelectric ceramics
as a sensor the full advantage is not utilized. Moreover rotor system is most often ﬁtted
with proximity sensors due to its favourable characteristics. The aim of this thesis is
consequently to use piezoelectric ceramic as an actuator to enabling control strategy
which could either be passive or active.
This thesis focuses on the active use to control gas bearing dynamics. There are
many aspects in this ﬁeld among many to increase the understanding of the physical
principles in an active gas bearings. Here the inherited dynamics consists of many sub-
systems which characterize the response, and the insight is initially gained on a partial
contribution to the research and development of the ﬁnal application. Consequently
two main aspects are pursued. The ﬁrst one involves modelling of the electromech-
anical coupling between the top foil of the gas bearing and the piezoelectric actuator.
This is done in a holistic framework with respect to physical aspects and geometrical
limitations of the system. A phenomenological damping model is employed to provide
high model accuracy, which bas been experimentally veriﬁed. The mechanical model
is successfully established for a subsystem known as a ﬂexible structure.
The second aspect involves design of a control system for the integrated piezoelec-
tric actuator. Experimental and numerical results reveal how the designed control
system can reduce modal vibrational and increase damping in the system signiﬁcantly.
Irrespectively of the occurrence of non-linearities it performs robust and desirable,
which enables ﬂuid ﬁlm interaction by means of a compliant top foil.

Preface
This thesis is submitted as partial fulﬁlment of the requirements for obtaining the
Danish M.Sc degree. It is in accordance with the deﬁned Honour Programme, enrolled
from February 2014 to April 2016. The thesis consists mainly of a summary written and
an experimental contribution of the appended publication performed during the Master
project from August 2015 to April 2016. The work is comprised of multidisciplinary
projects, involving Fluid-Structure Interactions, Dynamics, Vibrations, Piezoelectric
Actuation, Mathematical Modelling, Numerical Methods, and ﬁnally Experimental
Mechanics where i have my passion. It is carried out primarily at the Section of Solid
Mechanics (FAM), Department of Mechanical Engineering (MEK), Technical University
of Denmark (DTU).
Professor Ilmar Ferreira Santos has been my personal mentor during the two years
of my Master, and has supervised this thesis. I am sincerely grateful to Ilmar, for an
educational collaboration where i have gained advanced insight in both experimental
and analytical practice of electromechanical machine elements. I believe we have
gained mutual respect, and we have shared many frustrations and emotional per-
spectives in our privacy. His profound kindness has been crucial for me as a human
being, and in the future he will be a huge inspiration in my professional career as
well as in privacy. This overall friendly and supportive behaviour is reﬂected in the
research group, which was accounted lately when i had to prolonged my master thesis
by 3 months due to the disease of Guillain-Barré syndrome. I am sincerely grateful to
my supervisor Ilmar, co-supervisor PhD student Bo Bjerregaard Nielsen and my PhD
colleagues in this regard, Sebastian von Osmanski, Jonas Lauridsen, Cesar A. L. L.
da Fonseca, Geraldo F. de S. Rebouças, Andreas J. Voigt, Christian Mandrup-Poulsen,

Jorge A. G. Salazar, and Fabián G. P. Vásquez.

==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Rotor systems require bearings to support and enable transmission of power with a
low cost of energy. Bearings can moreover increase stability margins of rotor systems.
This is most optimal obtained by low friction between the shaft and the bearing, by
high damping and high load carrying capacity, Enemark 2015. Generally, various types
of bearing provide advantages and disadvantages in the subjected area. Among many
Active Magnetic Bearing, Active Lubrication Bearing and Gas Bearing types possesses
some of these capabilities. This thesis focuses on Gas bearings which have immense
advantage due to low mechanical friction, high speed application and abilities to
operate in clean-tech environment. Unfortunately they suffer from low damping and
are prone to instability if not properly balanced.
In the past, gas bearings have been improved passively in the design phase by
adjusting geometry and improving dissipation of energy through use of bump foils.
Adding smart materials will thus provide a new dimension to optimization of gas
bearing technology where the potential of signiﬁcant improvements is large due to the
feasibility of integrating a control system. A multi-physic model will be built with the
aim of describing the gas bearing. In continuation, ﬂuid ﬁlm forces (also represented
by damping and stiffness coefﬁcients) as a function of the bearing surface compliance
(elasticity) will be established which will permit control using smart materials. The
bearing load capacity and the ﬂuid ﬁlm forces are strongly dependent on the bearing
geometry and rotor angular velocity. By controlling the geometry of the bearing gap
via smart materials and optimal design of feedback control gains, signiﬁcant changes
in the bearing properties are expected. This gives an opportunity of compensating
unbalanced mass. However this thesis only focuses on the initial investigation of a
partial subsystem, leaving implementation in the bearing application for further work.
Piezoelectric ceramics is one type of the categorized smart materials known for
fast acting dynamics crucial in high speed application. The objective is to model
and analyse the dynamics of the mechatronic structures, referred to as the top foil,
composed by a curvilinear plate with a piezoelectric actuator as presented in Figure
1.1. The aim is to motivate further work, where three mechatronic top foils are built
into a gas bearing for ﬂuid ﬁlm interaction. The bearing system in its simplest form
characterized by the clamped top foil with piezoelectric also involves many aspects. As
seen it consist of one displacement sensor which provides dynamic free response by
contactless measurement. One actuator is moreover attached to the top foil, which
makes the system a single input single output (SISO) system.
==PAGE:18==
2
1 Introduction
Figure 1.1: Test rig of the top foil showing the proximity sensor, the wires for the piezo-
electric actuator attached on the rear side of the shell, and the clamping mechanism.
To apply control theory to gas bearings, and to understand and utilize the full
potential of a piezoelectric actuator coupled to the top foil, the system must be stud-
ied in details and a mathematical model must be establish based here on. Such a
mathematical model is proposed as a Finite Element Models which generally is to
large for implementation in control purpose. Reduction by modal decomposition is
expected to give the desired behaviour. Finally, validation must be obtained based on
a proposed phenomenological damping model and analytical and experimental results
are expected to correlate. This is done with respect to the scientiﬁc literature in this
ﬁeld. Moreover adequate precaution must be given to the operational principles of
sensor and actuators to ensure an optimal integration.
Finally, the experimental tests should aim at clarifying or rejecting assumptions
made in the numerical model. Here especially focus will be on piezoelectric ceramics
known for non-linear response. This is done to initiate that the physical model correlates
with theory and to substantiate the ﬁnal robustness. The single top foil can in the end
be coupled to a rotor whereby experimental validation for the ﬂuid ﬁlm forces can be
assessed. Furthermore, a study must be made to assess how the compliant structure
effectuates a change in the rotor system. Here is referred to the equivalent damping
and stiffness coefﬁcients of the air ﬁlm forces. The single top foil can thus be fused
into a more generic model including the complete active gas bearing beneﬁting from
engineering control with safe and reliable operation.
1.1
Prior Work and Literature Review
The master project is strongly multi-disciplinary and will link the main disciplines
carefully chosen for the candidate: Machinery Dynamics, Mechanical Vibrations, Finite
Element, Smart Materials, Control Techniques, Fluid Mechanics, Special Course I
(Dynamics & Control of Flexible Structures) and Special Course II (Nonlinear Dynamics
of Jeffcott-Rotor Coupled to Piezoelectric System). The theoretical part of the master
thesis is carried out in a collaboration with the PhD project studied by Bo Bjerregaard

1.1 Prior Work and Literature Review

Nielsen. The experimental part is carried out in special test rig which is designed and
built at DTU Department of Mechanical Engineering, which is illustrated in ﬁgure 1.2.
The test rig is designed on a modular concept by the named PhD student, enabling
partial execution of various experiments with and without coupling of the rotor. The
test rig is studied through previous work presented by Grathwol 2015a, where a small
explanation of the test rig can be found. Grathwol 2015b also presented a tutorial
describing the preliminary use of the test rig which is signiﬁcant improved in terms of
electrical instrumentation and mechanical durability carried out during this master
project.

==PAGE:20==
4
1 Introduction
1.2
Objective
Previous research and development of gas bearing technology has exclusively been
assessed on passive methodologies where geometry and various design aspects has
been treated. The purpose of the thesis is to provide an academic contribution to
substantiate optimization of gas bearings through active regulation. This is established
through the following objectives:
• Understand the working principle of a piezoelectric actuator coupled to a ﬂexible
structure (single top foil) and establish a dynamic mathematical model.
• Based on an optimal control law, design a SISO control system to reduce modal
response and increase damping in the compliant top foil by means of feedback
gains.
• Describe different actuation principles and enable dynamic positioning of top
foil curvature through control.
• Implement the control system experimentally and characterize its performance
and robustness.
• Based on an experimental approach, describe the linking of air ﬁlm forces and
angular velocity dependence between a ﬁrm rotor and the top foil with its
inherited dynamics.
1.3
Outline
This thesis consist of one appended publication describing the electromechanical
ﬁnite element model of the ﬂexible structure coupled to the piezoelectric actuator. The
main working principle is outlined based on theoretical and experimental method-
ologies while results verify the model. Based on the summary it is highlighted the
optimization procedure of developing state of the art smart gas bearing technology.
The aim at using active control, based on the derived model. The introduction cat-
egorizes various aspects needed for the technology which is systematically elaborated
throughout the chapters in this thesis.
Chapter 2 brieﬂy summarizes derivation of the model, and present a reduced single
input single output (SISO) model. A complex separation is carried out, and aspects
of controllability and observability are assessed. System parameters are determined
experimentally. Finally the model is compared with experimental tests to determine
the accuracy.
Chapter 3 focuses on non-linear phenomena inherited in the top foil dynamics.
It raises the right insight under physical actuation, to prevent system drift from the
linearized operating point, deﬁned by the proposed model.
Chapter 4 deals with the design of two different control system that are employed
to the reduced model. A Linear Quadratic Gaussian regulator is obtained with and
without integral action. Estimations of modal response provides feedback for the
control system. Analytical and experimental results shows that the mathematical
model is well established in terms of control. Signiﬁcant damping is actively added to
==PAGE:21==
1.3 Outline
5
the system. Sensitivity and uncertainty aspects are ﬁnally related to the robustness
and performance of the controller.
Chapter 5 treats the dynamic coupling between a rotor and the single top foil.
Based on an experimental test the inﬂuence of air ﬁlm forces is related to the change
in system’s natural frequencies. Angular velocity dependence is established experi-
mentally, and mass unbalanced and residual misalignment of rotor are discovered as
process disturbance.
Chapter 6 ﬁnalizes with the conclusion and future aspects.
==PAGE:22==
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2
Finite Element Model of Top Foil
In order to deal with the dynamic modelling and control of active air bearings,
the system’s inherited dynamic must be modelled correctly. It consists of piezoelectric
actuators which are the inputs in the system, while displacement sensors are the
outputs in the system. The modelling is done by separation into subsystems while
experimental validation is attained before the complexity is increased towards the
ﬁnal application. The main model on which the additional subsystems are imposed
is a linear time invariant Finite Element Model (FEM) of the top foil which is the
main focus in this thesis. The additional subsystems, are the bump foil model and the
air ﬁlm forces which is out of scope toward a control implementation in this thesis.
However, the air ﬁlm forces is revised in chapter 5, where a coupling between the
top foil and the rotor system assessed experimentally. Before this is established a
Single Input Single Output (SISO) system of the dynamic model must be obtained,
and reduced to 1st order state space form. In this procedure it is discovered how well
Experimental Modal Analysis (EMA) can be used to provide crucial information of
system properties to model the correct level of damping into the system. Modern
control theory with optimal control can be applied, which among many control systems
is the most efﬁcient. A reduction of the state space model is required to enable real
time signal processing of the control system. This is necessary sine Electronic Control
Units (ECU) have limited memory and processor capacity, where the processing time
of the ECU is somehow inversely proportional to the size, e.i. the numbers of degree of
freedom (d.o.f.), of the control model. As revealed in this chapter, the reduced model
has complex states, why the system must be complex separated. This is attained as
integration of complex states is not possible in a ECU. Finally, forced response will be
dealt with experimentally as well as numerically to see how well the model predicts
the physical system. This is essential as the proposed control theory requires optimal
model prediction. Another essential aspect of this is controllability and observability of
the states in the system. Controllability is associated with the modelled piezoelectric
actuator imposing energy into the system, whereas the observability is referred to
the output sensor which provide the response of the system. It should be noted that
chapter is a new and improved revision of a report made in the special couse Dynamic
& Control of Flexible Structures, presented by Nielsen 2015.
==PAGE:24==
8
2 Finite Element Model of Top Foil
2.1
Dynamic Modelling
The FEM model of the top foil dealt with in this thesis is presented by Nielsen
et al. 2015. It consists of the mechatronic double curved shell with a piezoelectric
actuator mounted on the top foil’s backside. A schematic of the physical system is
shown in Figure 2.1, where a ﬁxed proximity sensor provide the local time dependent
output displacement y(t) given by the unstressed gap size y0, and the dynamical
response yr(t). A dynamic response can be obtained if the piezoelectric input voltage
u is time varying which will induce excitation of the structure. The displacement can
alternatively be expressed with an areal dependency
y(t, bt,ε) = y0(bt,ε) + yr(t, bt,ε)
(2.1)
here bt deﬁne the width of the top foil, while ε the circumference angle. It is illustrated
how the top foil is clamped in a wrench, which constrain the motion to zero along this
line. This must be accounted for by proper boundary conditions in the FEM model.
To express the displacement y(t, bt,ε), the shell is discretized and a ﬁnite element for-
mulation is performed. The formulation is based on general linear elasticity described
by double curved isoparametric shell elements with ﬁrst order shear deformation theory
throughout the thickness of the shell, as proposed by Cook et al. 2002, page 578.
y0
u
(a) Right view
(b) Isometric view
Figure 2.1: Schematic of the experimental set up of the electromechanical top foil.
Note the sensor and actuator location with the corresponding unstressed gap size y0
and input signal u of the piezoelectric actuator.
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Dynamic Modelling
9
(a) Element with local coordinate
systems in each node
(b) Local and global coordinate
system and nodal kinematic variables
Figure 2.2: 8-node double curved shell element with local coordinates given in each
node and the relationship between the local and global coordinates and the kinematic
nodal variables. The origin of the ﬁgure is found in Nielsen et al. 2015.
The discretization is based on 8 node elements, with 5. d.o.f for each node. These
are presented by three translational in the global coordinate system, due to the three
dimensional shape of the top foil, while only two rotational d.o.f, tangential to the
surface is assumed due to thin shell assumption which gives ue = [x1, x2, x3,α,β]⊤,
see Figure 2.2. Additional d.o.f. arise on the nodes where the piezoelectric actuator is
overlayed the shell. These d.o.f are characterized by the electrical potential difference
over the piezoelectric actuator as well as the electric ﬁeld in the piezoelectric material
denoted by ∆φe. The study show how coupling can be established between the ﬂexible
top foil and the piezoelectric actuator relative to the deﬁned d.o.f.. Here, the effect of
piezoelectric actuation is coupled to structural displacement by mean of the applied
electrical potential across the electrodes of the actuator. However, the effect can also
be reversal as mechanical displacement of the structure will impose an equipotential
voltage on the piezoelectric actuator. Generally, this means that mechanical deﬂection
of the shell induces a voltage in the piezoelectric actuator, and vice versa an applied
electrical load on the piezoelectric actuator will enforce deﬂection of the top foil. As a
consequence the two problems cannot be solved independently which is known as cross
coupling between the piezoelectric effect and the mechanical stiffness. The coupling is
used to describe the dynamics of the structure as well as the inﬂuence of the control
signal. Finally, the semidiscrete form of the problem can be deﬁned on element basis
according to the vector qe, containing mechanical and electrical degree of freedom
qe = [ue,∆φe]⊤
Me¨qe + Keqe = Fe
(2.2)
the element system matrix Me and Ke corresponds to the mass and stiffness matrices
according to:

An assumption is here that the electric potential between the top and the bottom of
the actuator, cause an electric ﬁeld, which is constant through the thickness. By this

means no shape function are required to approximate the electric ﬁeld in the ﬁnite

expressed in an external force vector composed with electric ﬁelds and mechanical
forces. For more details please ﬁnd the appended publication Nielsen et al. 2015.
Returning to the objective of this section that deals with vibration analysis of the
top foil, the coupled electromechanical system must be reduced only to contain the
mechanical d.o.f. as they are of main interest. This is assumed because the control
action described in Chapter 4 is independent of the actuation of the electrical d.o.f
caused by use of an ampliﬁer. The reduction is proposed by GUYAN 1965, where
all mechanical d.o.f. remain in the reduced system as masters denoted η, while the
discarded d.o.f. is the electrical deﬁned as slaves respectively. The Guyan reduction of
the system ﬁnally takes the well known equation of motion:
M¨η(t) + D˙η(t) + Kη(t) = ¯bu(t) = f(t)
(2.4)
It is based on Newton’s second law stating that all internal forces are balanced by
external forces as seen in equation (2.4). The matrices M, K and D represents the
consistent mass, stiffness and proportional damping of the top foil. η denotes the
displacement vector of the n d.o.f. in generalized coordinates, why the (˙) indicate
it’s time derivatives. By representing the mentioned cross coupling proportional to a
constant u(t) deﬁned by the time dependent input voltage on the piezoelectric actuator,
¯b represents how the actuator perturb the top foil. Multiplying ¯b with u(t) result in
the surface force acting on the compliant structure. Alternatively, the surface force can
be written as a vector f, representing a point load vector. The result of the applied
surface force caused by the electric potential u are summarized:
• An increasing in the electric potential u result in an expansion of the piezoelectric
actuator which makes the top foil bend inward by means of decreasing the
curvature radius.
• A decrease in the electric potential u causes a contraction of the piezoelectric
actuator which result in an outward motion increasing the curvature radius of
the top foil.
The reader must be aware, that the signal u is the magnitude of the voltage across
the piezoelectric actuator. As the ampliﬁer only accepts signals in the range of ˜u ∈
[−2.5;7.5] V, which correlates to a physical magnitude u ∈[−500;1500] V through
a gain κ = 200 V. The scaling must be build into the input vector ¯b for a correct
implementation. Although, it is assumed that the piezoelectric actuator operates
linearly in this range u ∈[−500;1500], it is not physically true. This particular
problem is resumed in the later part of the thesis, such that correct precaution can be
established toward the non-linear phenomena of piezoelectric actuators.
Aiming at modern control design based on ﬁrst order linear time-invariant systems,
the second order system (2.4) needs to be rewritten:
˙z(t) = Az(t) + Bu(t)
w(t) = Cz(t)
(2.5)
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Dynamic Modelling
11
This is the well known state space model, where z is the generalized state vector
containing state variables of the matrix A containing system dynamics. B is the input
matrix containing actuator dynamics, and C the output matrix containing inputs in
terms of measured states in the system, which deﬁnes the output w. As will be later
emphasized in the thesis, the matrices A, B and C should be treated properly, for
efﬁcient control to be applied.

However, also here problems arise, when the matrix E is inverted since an equivalent

There is however another advantage of the restated equation of motion, since the
damped eigenvalue problem can be assessed. This approach can be used to decompose
the system into modal coordinate denoted by x enabling a modal reduction. This is
caused by the orthogonality properties of eigenvectors, which is a powerful mathemat-
ical tool in reduction techniques. It should be emphasized the importance of reducing
the model, since the full FEM model is computational to heavy for implementation
in a real controller of the electromechanical bearing system. Consequently, reduction
techniques which is based on the properties of eigenvalue assessment is dealt with in
the following.
Modal order Reduction
Various methods of model reduction has been studied and there exist several
examples in the litterature efﬁcient for reduction of ﬂexible structures. Among all
of them there exist static reduction proposed by GUYAN 1965. This is characterized
as a set of master d.o.f in the reduced system while discarding residual dynamics
deﬁned by the slaves. This is evident under the assumptions that less important d.o.f.
which has signiﬁcant lower magnitude of vibration can be deﬁned as slaves. To further
eliminate redundant degree of freedom, an additional approach is to include only a
sufﬁcient amount of nodes with its corresponding d.o.f.. To approximate the inherited
mode shape requiring at least 2 nodes per half sine function of the shape. This is
however cumbersome and difﬁcult to determine and a substantial amount of d.o.f.
can still be left in the reduced system to sufﬁcient approximate e.g. the amplitude
of the ﬁrst 6 modes. The result of inadequate number of d.o.f. in this technique is a
reduction in amplitude of vibration in the frequency domain, which will degrade the
control performance. Hence a reductiontechnique known as Pseudo Modal Truncation
is proposed.
The Pseudo Modal Truncation is more precise and less trivial, relative to the remain-
ing d.o.f in the system. Moreover, it provides the ability to get around the singularity
problem with the mass matrix described in the later section. This technique is studied,
among many Christensen and Santos 2005, Trindade et al. 2000 and Inman 2008, page
601, where a decomposition to modal coordinates is obtained due to orthogonality
properties of eigenvectors.
Pseudo Modal Truncation
The solution to the corresponding eigenvalue problem of the homogeneous part of
equation (2.12) is assumed to be complex of the form:
z = Φeλt
(2.14)
==PAGE:29==
2.1 Dynamic Modelling
13
The advantage of the restated 1’st order system (2.12) containing the M,D and K
matrices, is a linear solution to the eigenvalue problem according to:
(E −λjF)Φj = 0
(2.15)
E and F is real and symmetric matrices with size 2n × 2n. λj = δj + iγj denotes
the complex eigenvalue separated into its real and imaginary part. Other than the
trivial solution Φ = 0, the full eigenvalue problem will have 2n solutions linearly
independent. All the eigenvalues λj and the eigenvectors Φj will appear in complex
conjugate pairs. The solution will be under-damped, which are typical for structural
mechanical problems.
By inspection of the solution to the eigenvalue problem in equation (2.14), one can
assess the meaning of an under-damped solution characterized by a negative real part.
The solution will be oscillating, and the negative real part δ will correspond to the
decay in vibration amplitude, caused by the dissipation of internal energy. In this report
the damping and the dissipation of energy is modelled with proportional damping,
and a detailed review of this topic is found in section 2.2. Lastly, the imaginary part
γ of the eigenvalue will correspond to the frequency of vibration, which will be the
damped natural frequency as seen in equation (2.16) containing Euler’s formula.
Φe(δ+iγ)t = Φ eδt eiγt = Φ eδt (cos(γt) + i sin(γt))
(2.16)
The eigenvectors Φj corresponds to the complex modes of vibration. Collecting all the
eigenvectors in terms of the right eigenvectors one obtain the right modal matrix Ur.
Ur = [Φ1
Φ2
···
Φ2n]
(2.17)
Since the Finite Element Problem is non-defective i.e. that no eigenvalues are repeated,
then there exist no linearly dependent eigenvectors. In other words, are none repeated
eigenvalues. This is a very important property, since the product of the inverse of
the right modal matrix with itself gives a diagonal identity matrix. In case of correct
normalization, and the eigenvalues appear in ascending order, the result is shown
below:
U−1
r Ur = I
(2.18)
In general, this tells that the eigenvectors are orthogonal to each other which indicate
that all off diagonal elements are zero, Cook et al. 2002, Appendix C.

i ̸= j
(2.19)

value problem through transforming the generalizes coordinates z to modal coordinates

The reason why it is written as a summation is to illustrate that the transformation
only needs to be done for the modes that matters. This means that the motion of the
complicated Finite Element system with n d.o.f., can be approximated by only a couple
of modal contributions, within the bandwidth of the system deﬁned by ωb.
In order to determine which modes are important, one have to perform a more detailed
study of the system. This is done with frequency response functions later in the thesis.
However, the designer must take into account the frequency range of interest as well as
considerations regarding controllability and observability of the independent modes.
Solving the full eigenvalue problem (2.15) is very time consuming, and compu-
tational expensive. Moreover, including all modes will not reduce the system as just
emphasized. Hence the interest is to ﬁnd a subset of eigenvalues, within the range
of interest. Here N deﬁnes the eigenvalues and corresponding eigenvectors sought
of in ascending order, where N denotes the number of modes to be included in the
reduced model. Then the modal matrix Ur can deﬁned by a subset of eigenvectors in
the transformation matrix Tr. This transformation matrix will just contain the N modes
of interest. However, ﬁnding the inverse of the modal matrix Tr, used to decouple
the system become impossible since Tr is non symmetric. For the problem stated in
this report, the system matrices M, D and K is symmetric and must consequently be
decomposed to modal coordinates by including the left eigenvectors in the left trans-
formation matrix Tl. This implies the same orthogonality properties to Tr. However,
as the problem was non-defective, the left transformation matrix can be simply stated
as Tl = Tr.
Tr = [Φ1
Φ2
···
Φ2N]
Tl = [Φ1
Φ2
···
Φ2N]
(2.22)
Hereby
T⊤
l Tr = I
(2.23)
An important thing to remember in the above, when transposing a complex eigenvectors,
is to make the transpose of the complex conjugate. Moreover, the eigenvalues and
eigenvectors comes in complex conjugate pairs why one have to ﬁnd the 2N numbers
of eigenvalues in order to include N modes in the reduction.
Remarks on Left Eigenvectors

made to perform the decomposing through the eigenvectors. One would have to solve
the left eigenvector stated by the eigenvalue problem:
(E⊤−λjF⊤)Ψ j = 0
(2.24)
where the left eigenvectors can be collected in the left modal matrix Ul:
Ul = [Ψ1

Hence one can change the 2n × 2n state system in generalized coordinates into a new

of N selected modes. Here with a similarity transformation Tr used to map the state
variables in and out of generalized- and modal-coordinates. Based on the selection
of the input matrix C the transformed matrix Cr tells how much of the generalized
response is represented in terms of modal contributions being present in the structure.
Caused by the diagonalization of the reduced system, the system matrix Ar contains
the eigenvalues Λ of the system along the diagonal, while the off-diagonal elements

Arv = λv,
→

(2.32)
stating that the system is completely diagonalized, and the eigenvalues is not different
from the diagonal terms in Ar.
The trade-off when reducing the system this way, is the system become complex. In
general, this is not a problem since imaginary numbers and linear algebra works ﬁne.
The problem arises when performing integration of the implemented state space model
in MATLAB - SIMULINK, and when implementing real time control on the Control Unit
(CU) which in this case is a dSPACE card. Such applications can not handle complex
matrices, why it must be attained by complex separation, which will be the topic of
the next section.
Complex Separation
Since all three system matrices, here stated in the reduced form Ar, Br and Cr, are
complex, separation is attained. The concept is to respect the complex relation within
the multiplication of the state space model in equation (2.31). Splitting the complex
state into a real and a imaginary part is hence proposed, which initially was proposed
by Christensen and Santos 2005, and later elaborated by Greve and Warburg 2010.
This section is a revision of the procedure.
In terms of reduced state space model in equation (2.31), the system can be handled
as three independent equations as seen in (2.33)-(2.35).
˙x(t)
= Arx(t)
(2.33)
˙x(t)
= Bru(t)
(2.34)
q(t)
= Crx(t)
(2.35)
Initially, the modal state variables x and ˙x, need to be separated into a real and a
imaginary part. As a consequence it doubles the number of states in the separated
model which will now be 4N. This is associated with a trade-off, since the reduced
model become computational more heavy. However, in the end of this section it is
shown a mathematical trick to work around this problem aiming at maintaining the
size of 2N states. For the convenience in this section, the separated matrices is denoted
with the indices s, while Re, Im represents respectively the Real and Imaginary part.
The separated modal state variables is:
xs = [Re(x1) Im(x1) Re(x2) Im(x2) ··· Re(x2N) Im(x2N)]⊤
˙xs = [Re(˙x1) Im(˙x1) Re(˙x2) Im(˙x2) ··· Re(˙x2N) Im(˙x2N)]⊤
(2.36)

Reduction of Separated State Space Model
The Pseudo Modal Truncated Complex Separated Reduced state space model can
now be written with the subscript rs:
˙xs(t) = Arsxs(t) + Brsu(t)
y(t) = Crsxs(t)
(2.57)
The number of states included in the system is 4N, with N indicating the number
of modal coordinates in the reduction. It has increased 4 times in size. However,
it is possible to downscale it to the size of 2N, thus maximizing performance in a
control objective. This is acquired by a simply elimination as the complex state appear
redundant with it’s complex conjugate.
Recall that the reduced model is obtained by rewriting the generalized coordinates
z to modal coordinates x by a linear combination of the modes that matters (2.21).
Then the output matrix Crs is mapping the modal coordinates back to the generalized
coordinates through:
z =
N
X
i=1
Crs,ixs,i
Therefore it can be utilized that the complex separated linear time invariant system
(2.57) has some symmetric properties due to the complex separation and complex
conjugate pairs. The output being measured is a linear combination of the included
modes, which is mapped from modal coordinates to generalized coordinates by the
output matrix. Multiplying the modal amplitude xrs,i with the modal contribution of the
output matrix Crs,i, result in a measure of how much each generalized coordinate get
of the motion of each mode. The mode shapes distributes the amplitude proportional
with the correct amount of the measured output.
Since the reduction is based on the eigenvectors and its complex conjugate, symmetry
occurs when the separation is performed of the output matrix, with an conversion of
the signs. Inspection of the modal coordinate and its complex conjugate reveal the
following equality:
Re(x1) + i Im(x1) = Re(x2) −i Im(x2)
(2.58)
[−Re(Cr,1) −Im(Cr,1)] = [−Re(Cr,2) Im(Cr,2)]
(2.59)
Assuming that the reduced state vector include only one mode:
y = Crsxs
Crs = [−Re(Cr,1),−Im(Cr,1),−Re(Cr,2),+Im(Cr,2)]
xs = [Re(x1),
Im(x1),
Re(x2), −Im(x2)]⊤
(2.60)
Then a multiplication of the modal state vector with the output matrix Crs given by
equation (2.60) result in:
y = −Re(Cr,1)Re(x1) −Im(Cr,1)Im(x1) −Re(Cr,2)Re(x2) + Im(Cr,2)(−Im(x2))
= −Re(Cr,1)Re(x1) −Im(Cr,1)Im(x1) −Re(Cr,1)Re(x1) −Im(Cr,1)Im(x1)
= 2 (−Re(Cr,1)Re(x1) + Im(Cr,1)Im(x1))
(2.61)
==PAGE:37==
2.1 Dynamic Modelling
21
The above calculations are only performed for the ﬁrst modal contribution and its
complex conjugate. However, the tendency apply equally for all modes. The reduced
complex separated system (2.57) can be reduced by multiplying the output matrix Crs
with a factor 2 due to the redundancy. While the complex conjugate solution from the
output must be discarded the input and the dynamic state matrices. The selection is
determined by the vector κ indicating the indices to be selected from the matrices.
˙xrrs(t) = Ars(κ,κ)xs(κ, t) + Brs(κ,:)u(t)
y(t) = 2Crs(:,κ)xs(κ, t)
(2.62)
κ is found by selecting the modal representing vector m, stating the modes that mattes
less that or equal to N in ascending order, while Nm states the number of included
modes in the system:
κ(2i −1 : 2i) =
Nm
X
i=1
(4m(i) −3 : 4m(i) −2)
(2.63)
As an example if m = [1, 2, 3, 4]⊤, then:
κ = [1, 2, 5, 6, 9, 10 13, 14],
Please note that as the reduced complex separated system containing N states with the
N lowest eigenvalues, then m can be chosen to include particular modes by complying
inclusion of modes described with integers lower than or equal to N. An example with
N = 5, solving for the 2N lowest eigenvalues and selecting m = [1, 3, 5]⊤, will result
in a reduced model which will inﬂict response only on the 1st the 3rd and the 5th mode.
κ will here be equated to:
κ = [1, 2, 9, 10, 17, 18],
m = [1,3,5]
(2.64)
Modal Reduction techniques involving complex separation should capture the
dynamic in the range of interest. To check for correct implementation in the top foil
application, forced response is used to evaluate how well the full- and reduced-model
correlates with each other. In this way comparison of experimental obtained results
with the mathematical model can beobtained. For a more generic expression in the
thesis the complex separated reduced order system (2.62) is written without indices
equivalent to equation (2.5). However, now represented in terms of modal coordinates.
From this point in the thesis and onwards, when talking about state space model, is
referred to the reduced linear time invariant model:
˙x(t) = Ax(t) + Bu(t)
y(t) = Cx(t)
(2.65)
Forced response
Evaluation of forced response, can be stated both in terms of the full and reduced
model. For the generality in this thesis this section covers an elaborate derivation.

The forced response must be evaluated both for the full and reduced model at the

Having deﬁned the boundaries for assessing the reduced state space model the
dynamic of the top foil is to be dealt with. This is done in the next section, using
experimental modal analysis. Frequency response functions between the input and
the output is used to predict system properties. Investigations of the system dynamics
are carried out in an iterative procedure aiming at a better implementation of the
mathematical model. It deals exclusively with obtaining correct eigenvalues, mode
shapes as well as structural properties i.e. damping and physical constants. Hereby
the ﬁnite element model can be updated with respect to these parameters to rely on
the numerical response which should be almost equivalent with the physical response.
This is crucial for a control application that is based on optimal control theory assuming
optimal model prediction. It must be emphasized the importance of a experimental
validation. Subsequently, the quality of the reduction techniques dealt with in this
section can be assessed.
==PAGE:40==
24
2 Finite Element Model of Top Foil
2.2
Model Validation of Dynamic system
Modal analysis as well as experimental modal analysis can directly be used to
validate the mathematical model in terms of natural frequencies and mode shapes.
The section distinguish between an analytical and an experimental prediction. The
analytical prediction was previously dealt with in section 2.1. It is obtained by rewriting
the second order system into a ﬁrst order system without inclusion of damping in
the model. This will make the the solution of the eigenvalue problem complex. The
eigenvalues will consequently represent the undamped natural frequencies ω while
the eigenvectors will correspond to the mode shapes of the structure. By assessing
correct eigenvalues from the mathematical model result in correct implementation of
the physical uncertainties in the system. Initially these must be studied and predicted
from experimental results revealing modal parameters as damping ratios, eigenvalues
and physic constants. This will subsequently lead to a more accurate mathematical
model.
Natural Frequencies and Mode shapes
The result of the iterative procedure reveal damping ratios as well as systems
uncertainties. However, before damping is considered the ﬁrst 8 undamped natural
frequencies from the mathematical model is obtained, see Table 2.1. These frequencies
are the results of the mathematical model being optimized iteratively relative to actuator
location, piezoelectric constants and top foil geometries. The following sections will
outline how the remaining parameters such damping is modelled into the system.
Until now the FEM model has been presented with a damping matrix D without stating
how is has been determined. It is based on a proportional damping model, which are
to be conﬁrmed or disproved. It is stated according to:
D = αM + βK
(2.80)
Here α and β is the stiffness and mass proportional relation that couple to the modal
damping ratios ζ with the natural frequency ω:
ζi =
α
2ωi
+
βωj
2
(2.81)
Here i indicates the corresponding mode. The coefﬁcient is found through least square
ﬁtting towards the experimental obtained modal damping ratios. Such damping ratio
is generally difﬁcult to predict theoretically for any systems, why an experimental
approach is used. Consequently, experimental modal analysis is carried out using a state
of the art laser scanning vibrometer. It has the advantage of obtaining experimental
Table 2.1: Undamped natural frequencies in Hz of the validated top foil obtained by

mode shapes based on frequency response functions. By using a mode ﬁtting software.
All the FRF functions can be used to estimates the damping ratios through least square
ﬁtting of single d.o.f. system to the resonance peaks.
Experimental Modal Analysis - Damping
Utilizing state of the art technology in obtaining experimental mode shapes one
can predict modal damping ratios. This is performed with a laser scanning vibrometer
using the doppler effect to measure velocity change of the vibrating top foil, due to an
delay in the frequency of the laser light.
Measurements is carried out as illustrated in the test set up shown in Figure 2.3.
It illustrates that a distance h must be ensured between the scanner and the top foil,
determined according to hardware descriptions. Moreover, it is assumed that the
laser light is projected normal to the top foil, assuming that the angle θ spanned
by the scanner is very small. Therefore the velocity is represented in terms of the
x component deﬁned by the global coordinate system as depicted in the ﬁgure. It
provides the measurement in a 2-dimensional space (x −y) which is an assumption as
the curvilinear top foil is 3-dimensional. Nonetheless the grid deﬁning the scan points
are presented in Figure 2.4(a) illustrating the camera view of the hardware, while
2.4(b) illustrates the assumed 2-dimensional projection. Here, the line with partially
numbed points indicate orthogonality relative to the scanner. Moreover, the top foil
will be clamped in the test-rig, why the amplitude of the shell tend to zero resulting in
a low coherence of the measurements which is more governed by noise. As a result of
this the measurements are based on an average of 15 measurement series to maximize
coherence, and cancel noise out of the measurements. The total scan time is close to
30 min.
Exporting the result from the laser scanning software reveals the frequency response
functions, and coherence for three different points in Figure 2.5. The phase plot is not
presented. A difference can be seen in the amplitude of the three different responses.
By ﬁtting a single degree of freedom system to each resonance peak, the amplitude at
every point for all natural frequencies can be evaluated. By including information of
the phase angle the experimental mode shapes can be obtained.

By inspection of the corresponding natural frequencies of the mode shapes, it is
seen to deviate by as much as 20% from the theoretical. This indicate an error in the
mathematical model as good results is obtained due to a high coherence in Figure 2.5.
It has incidental been discovered that measurements with the same vibrometer result in
different natural frequencies. This mean that the natural frequency change according
to a change in a system parameter, causing a slightly better or worth comparison with
the analytical results. The parameter that inﬂicts this change is the difference in the
amplitude of the excitation signal send to the piezoelectric actuator. This topic is dealt
with in a thorough research in section 3.1.
Recall that the objective of this section was to assess the modal damping ratios from
the experimental modal analysis. It is carried out by a polynomial ﬁt with ME-scope to
the measured frequency responses, shown for one example in Figure 2.7. Table 2.2
contain the resulting estimate of damping ratios. By ﬁtting the α and β parameters one
obtain the correlation according to equation (2.81) with the experiments as presented
in ﬁgure 2.8. It is believed that the 5 % damping for the ﬁrst mode is a combination of
the damping imposed by the surrounding cavity of air as well as structural dissipation
of energy.

2.3
Forced Response - Results
As just emphasized, large beneﬁts can be obtained when adjoining experimental
information with the mathematical modelling. In section 2.2 Experimental Modal
Analysis, and frequency response functions was used to obtain a correct damping
model. Moreover it provides evidence for small adjustments with respect to physical
uncertainties in piezoelectric and geometrical constants. The results are higher ac-
curacy which is presented in this section. This has been done on an agreed level of
accuracy with respect to non-linearities present in the system.
Recall that the the forced response provide the amplitude of vibration in the frequency
domain. The mathematical model including the piezoelectric actuator can thus be
validated. Observations of how the piezoelectric actuator couples to the Finite Element
Model of the top foil is pursued. The aim is to reconstruct the solution of the theoretical
Forced Response to the experimental FRF in terms of H1. In order for a fair compar-
ison, the displacement sensor, which provides the response of the structure has been
calibrated, see Apendix A1. Moreover as brieﬂy discussed in the previous section, the
non-linear effects that caused a change in the natural frequencies correlated with the
amplitude in the excitation signal. The mathematical model is consequently adjusted
with an amplitude level
¯u = 300V
By calculating the forced response of the full model according to equation (2.69), and
the response of the reduced model, according to equation (2.77), comparison with
==PAGE:46==
30
2 Finite Element Model of Top Foil
experimental results are possible. The results are acquired in the frequency range
0-500 Hz determined by the controllable gas bearing application. Within this range,
the model includes the ﬁrst ﬁve modes.
The response is obtained in d.o.f.=2072 which corresponds to the 415’th node in the
ﬁnite element discretization of the top foil, see appendix A7. This location corresponds
to the experimental measurement point. Moreover the measured displacement will be
a component with multiple d.o.f., mainly in the direction normal to the shell but also
the direction tangential to the surface i.e. the x- and y-direction.
The compliance of the model including the ﬁrst 5 modes are found in Figure 2.9.
Experimentally as well as numerically it is seen that the piezoelectric actuator is less
likely to excite modes other than the bending modes i.e. the 1’st, 3’rd and 5’th mode.
This is evident since the actuator apply force symmetric along the width of the top
foil, thus not creating any signiﬁcant torsional effect on the shell. Very little energy
can hence be transferred to the torsional modes. The effect is more signiﬁcant in the
model compared to the experiments as imperfections exist in practice. This arise e.g
when joining of the actuator to the top foil is performed see appendix A2.

2.3 Forced Response - Results
31
concept of modal observability. This topic is dealt with in the next section, which
eliminate this concern. The discussion of the repressed response in the 2’nd, 4’th
and the 6’th torsional mode, can thus be referred to as reduced controllability of the
inherited modes. This topic is also dealt with in the next section, and revised in chapter
4 when control actions are implemented. It might be evident to neglect torsional modes
in the control model. This is an advantage since it reduced the number of states with
6 variables.
When equating the forced response with exclusion of the discussed torsion modes,
reduced controllability is conﬁrmed as the the exact same response is obtained in the
reduced model. The trade-off is unfortunately that the model become less accurate,
which emphasize the importance of improving the model.
In general the responses corresponds very well with the experimental results, and
shows the beauty of ﬁnite element modelling. However, the reduced as well as the full
model can be improved slightly to include the torsional mode hereby replicating the
experimental results.
Recall that a force component placed asymmetric on the top foil will cause an
ampliﬁcation in the torsional modes. In theory it can be placed anywhere except
from on a nodal lines of the respective mode shapes. By replacing the equivalent
piezoelectric surface force of the model with small point loads represented by the
vector ¯f, the resulting response can be investigated. It is not straight forward to place
point loads to accommodate the desired response. The act of placing force components
with regard to the mode shapes, and the phase of vibration. After some cumbersome
work a satisfactory response is obtained. This is assessed by placing two point loads
with opposite sign, in the left half of the top foil as seen in Figure 2.10. Hence mainly
inﬂicting response on the 2’nd the 4’th and the 6’th mode, which can be correlated
with node 481 and 278 in the d.o.f. normal to the top foil. Although it will also cause
an ampliﬁcation in the remaining modes, it gives a satisfactory response, which is seen
in Figure 2.11, in terms of amplitude and phase.

If the two force models, including the equivalent piezoelectric force and the point

˜b = ¯bu +¯f
(2.83)
The result of the superimposed FEM model with the inherited imperfection is now
obtained, see Figure 2.12. Ampliﬁcation occur mainly at the 2’nd and 4’th mode
relative to the symmetric model without force imperfections which gives a more
accurate response. The magnitude of the point loads was found by a trial and error
approach, given by f1 = 0.000019 N in node 481, and f2 = −0.000013 N in node
278. The ﬁnal model is thus validated, whereby the reduced model is concluded
to correlate well with the ﬁrst 5 natural frequencies of the model. It is convincing
that the experimental response given by H1 has a well deﬁned response within the
bandwidth of the model. The coherence is close to one except at anti resonances
where the amplitude is in the order or the measurement noise. Regarding the phase
angle, it deviates from the experiment as the frequency exceed 185 Hz. As previously
emphasized this unsatisfying response may be caused by an experimental inclusion
of many d.o.f. relative to the mathematical model which unfortunately only consider
one d.o.f.. An average of the frequency response functions of all measured degree
of freedom can reveal if this is the correct assumption. Generally, this approach is
not obtained, as the system dynamics is extremely dependent on the experimental set
up. Adjustments must be made relative to each top foil. In terms of robustness of a
controller it is seen how well the model perform in stabilizing the dynamics. Among
this discussion the complex eigenvalues is expressed in Table 2.3. It is seen that all
modes are asymptotically stable with damping ratios in the order of the experimental
obtained.

2.4
Controllability and Observability
There are multiple ways of assessing controllability and observability for a linear
control system. In general the controllability refer to as the systems ability to respond
on actuation of the input, while observability the degree of sensing dynamic of the
system from the output. It usually apply to linear control system where the input
and output matrices are governing. Based on the now experimental validated and
corrected model in terms of damping, the measure of controllability and observability
are studied. This is done in the thesis according to two criterias. The ﬁrst state explicit
if the system is fully controllable and or observable Hendricks et al. 2008, page 127 &
143. While the last gives a quantitative measure of controllability and observability
of the different modes in the model Choi et al. 2000 and Hamdan and Nayfeh 1989.
Both observability and controllability of a system can be improved by including more
sensors and actuators. This can enhance system observability and controllability such
that control action can be used to stabilize the system. However if the uncontrollable
and unobservable states is stabilizable and or detectable placing additional sensors
in the system is less important. If the sensors and actuators are located properly one
utilize the beneﬁts of a more simple and efﬁcient control system. This is studied in
terms of ﬂexible structures with piezoelectric actuators by Gupta et al. 2010. Here an
optimization procedure for optimal placement is presented, whereas remarks towards
the experimental response in the previous section is assessed.
Controllability - explicit
The state space system (2.65) is controllable if and only if the controllability matrix
Mc = [B
AB
A2B ··· AN−1B]
(2.84)
has rank N, i.e., full rank. The controllability matrix can similar be obtained for discrete
time systems.
Observability - explicit
The state space system (2.65) is observable if and only if the observability matrix
Mo = [C
CA
CA2 ··· CAN−1]⊤
(2.85)
has rank N, i.e., full rank. The controllability matrix can similar be obtained for discrete
time systems.
Quantitative measure
Recall that the system has been reduced by the modal decomposition through the
diagonalization, caused by orthogonality properties of the left and right eigenvalues.
T⊤
l Tr = I
Here the control input matrix B represents the coupling from control input to the
system’s modes, while the matrix C represents the coupling from the system’s modes to
==PAGE:51==
2.4 Controllability and Observability
35
the output. This can be evaluated as a measure of the controllability and observability
for each of the modes included in the reduced model. The measure is presented
in terms of an amplitude and phase for the system in generalized coordinates. The
Controllability is evaluated according to
T⊤
l,i · bj = ∥Tl,i∥∥bj∥cos(θi j),
i = 1,2..n, j = 1,2..Ni
(2.86)
Here i represents the mode of interest in the transformation matrix. Ni denotes the
number of inputs associated with the actuators acting on the structure which for the
matter of this thesis is one. However, in a full controllable gas bearing, the outline
are three actuators why three inputs will be govern. Moreover, bj represent the input
force vector in generalized coordinates. So the measure is an indication for the degree
of controllability of the i’th mode to the j’th input. While the angle θi j gives the angle
between the input vector and the left eigenvalue. If the measure is close to zero it
means that the mode cannot be controlled very well.
A measure of the observability is similar deﬁned as
ck · Tr,i = ∥ck∥∥Tr,i∥cos(γki),
i = 1,2..n, k = 1,2..No
(2.87)
here No denotes the number of outputs associated with the number of sensors installed
in the control application. ck the input vector in generalized coordinates containing the
degree of freedom the sensor is attached, whereby the magnitude gives an indication
for the degree of observability for the i’th mode to the k’th output. While γ the angle
between the output vector and the right eigenvector.
Based on these formulations it is clearly seen that the reduced input matrix B and the
output matrix C is deﬁned similar, why these can conclude something about the level
of controllability and observability in the reduced system. Since the transformation
matrices are complex, one use the non-separated matrices to evaluate the magnitude
and phase. The magnitude is normalized so that the most controllable and observable
state is equal 1. The phase angle is illustrated according to equation (2.88) and Figure
2.13.
cos(θ) = Re(a · b)

Optimization for Controllability and Observability - Results
So far only theory of the controllability and observability has been considered.
However, guidelines for placing sensors and actuators in an optimal sense is here
outlined. As to what criteria are optimal, is questioned in terms of control application
dealt with in chapter 4. The guideline presented in this section is thus used to maximize
the observability and controllability of the top foil. It apply directly to other ﬂexible
structures reduced by modal order reduction techniques. Placing sensors and actuators
without assessing the quantitative measure require many years of expertise and can
result in undesired response, why the quantitative analysis must be obtained.
Observability
Optimal placement of the output sensor is made relative to a proximity sensor used
to measure the displacement. A proximity sensor restrain higher order modes from
being measured, as the amplitude become in the range of the measurement noise.
This apply also if the sensor is placed closed to a nodal line. To avoid this phenomena,
concept of the mode shapes and corresponding response must be evaluated. Given
the mode shape of the top foil in Figure 2.6, which tells the form of vibration, forced
response with random excitation will result in vibrations in a linear combination of
the inherited modes. Optimal sensor placement is thus obtained according to the d.o.f.
where amplitude of the mode shapes are largest. As emphasized the proximity sensor
measures an area of d.o.f. with respect to displacement. This is of cause relative to
the ﬁnite element discretization of the top foil. However it can be assumed that the
sensor only measure one variable, i.e. d.o.f. which in this case is the displacement
normal to the surface of the top foil see 2.1. Usually, it is of interest to place sensors to
measure as much dynamic as possible with as few sensors as possible, hereby reducing
application cost. However, it can come with the trade off in having noise to govern the
response. This can fortunately be circumvented in the control application, obtained
by an intelligent observer design. Deﬁnitely the rule of thump is to place the sensor
where the amplitude of the mode shape is largest to maximize noise to amplitude of
vibration ratio.
Based on this outline the know validated reduced model can be evaluated with
respect to the quantitative measure. Four different sensor locations is pursued in order
to fully investigate the output capabilities which provide information to state feedback.
The four different conﬁgurations can be found in Figure 2.14. It is deﬁned by the angle
of sensor inclination given by ε, and the placement according to the width of the top
foil deﬁned by bt, or bt
2 . The quantitative measure of the observability is normalized
with respect to the largest magnitude, for a better comparison. 1 will thus indicate the
most observable mode.
The reduced output matrix C including the ﬁrst 5 modes with the different sensor
conﬁgurations result in the measures in Table 2.4. Although no remarks are made on
the angle assessment, it is seen that the 1st mode is the most observable regardless
of the conﬁguration. This is due to the response of the top foil’s ﬁrst mode given by
ω1 which has the largest amplitude of vibration. The results reveal, in terms of the
ﬁrst mode, that conﬁguration (a) and (b) are better compared to (c) and (d). This
is caused as the amplitude of the ﬁrst mode increase proportional to the inclination

obtained experimentally. This indicates that nodal lines and points with zero motion,

the largest observability of the ﬁrst 5 modes, which are all observable. The respective
locations are in one of the two top corners of the top foil. The worst conﬁguration is
(c) as the observability of the 2’nd and 4’th mode is zero. Moreover it cause signiﬁcant
lower observability for the 1’st, 3’rd a 5’th mode.
Due to this ﬁnding the experimental test rig is designed to allow sensor position
along the left and right edge of the top foil as presented in appendix D3, containing the
work shop drawings of the test module. Unfortunately an oversight of the possibility to
reduce observability of torsional modes was not proposed. As will be shown in chapter
4, the consequence of the fault tolerant decision is a major downgrade of the control
performance and its associated robustness, due to spillover effects.
Controllability
The actuator has been ﬁrmly joined to the top foil before this design study was
performed, see appendix A2. Remarks will henceforth only apply for future revisions of
the active air bearing. The two design parameters which are dealt with in this analysis
is size of the rectangular piezoelectric actuator and the joined location on the top foil.
Generally, optimal actuator location should be considered towards the bandwidth of
the system with the governing modes.
The piezoelectric patch covers most of the backside of the top foil, controllability will
consequently exist in some way on all modes. If this should be avoided, allowing
only the most predominant mode to be controlled then the study in terms of optimal
controllability become more complex relative to shaping the actuator which must
be optimized selectively to the particular mode of interest. The result is a complex
geometry relative to the top foil’s mode shapes. However this is not the objective but
literature exist on this topic.
Within the design parameters a compromise must be attained due to practical
limitations and complex matters. If all modes are considered with equally importance
the actuator must be placed to maximize the respective magnitude of all modes thus
utilizing most power across the frequency range of interest. It was shortly discussed in
the section with forced response that torsional modes had a lower response compared
to the bending modes. This indicated that the controllability was lower for the torsional
modes as the system responded much better to symmetric bending modes, than to
torsional modes. This conclusion are to be conﬁrmed by the quantitative assessment
of the controllability. Hereby, remarks on the preliminary location of the actuator can
be made, and revised in experimental implementation where control spillover become
an inherited phenomena. Nonetheless the physical meaning of placing the actuator as
seen on Figure 2.15 indicate a symmetric coupling to the bending bending modes of
the top foil. This is caused by the electrode direction. In an controllable gas bearing
application these will also be the desired behaviour due to the laminar ﬂow between
the rotor and the top foil. Moreover, the rotor will primarily cause excitation in terms
of the bending modes. The quantitative measure of the controllability are normalized
with respect to the largest magnitude, for a better comparison. 1 will thus indicate the
most controllable mode.
The reduced input matrix B including the ﬁrst 5 modes with the actuator conﬁguration,
result in the measures in Table 2.5. It is seen that the most controllable mode is the 5th
mode. Moreover as previously stated the patch cannot control the torsional mode i.e.

that if the 1st mode should be penalized with more importance and the actuator must
be moved closer to the ﬁxed boundary as the strain density will be relatively larger.
The modal strain density is thus the controlling factor for the controllability, which
must be studied if suboptimal controllability are to be assessed.

Comparison
Based on the quantitative measures, the following statements are obtained
• Observability = 5
• Controllability = 3
This mean that the top foil can be fully observable, while only a subset of the modes
can be controlled. Based on the eigenvalue assessment of the reduced order model,
the system is stabilizable, which mean that the uncontrollable modes is asymptotically
stable due to a negative real part. A similar explanation can be used to describe
unobservable states, which become detectable as the states are asymptotically stable.
Therefore it is possible to design both a controller and an observer.
Finally the observability and controllability is evaluated explicit, by means of calculating
the controllability matrix and observability matrix. This is done including the the ﬁrst
N = 5 modes, and evaluating the rank of the separated reduced model. Recall that full
rank is equivalent with 2N = 10 as the system appear in terms of complex numbers.
The results are
rank(Mo) = 4
rank(Mc) = 4
(2.89)
By inspection of the calculated rank, disagreement exist toward to quantitative measure.
This is caused by the MATLAB function rank() that evaluate the rank toward a tolerance
criteria which is inﬂicted by the low observability and controllability as found in the
quantitative measure. It is thus believed that the quantitative measure is more accurate
for evaluating controllability and observability for large ﬁnite element structures toward
the reduced order model. Henceforth as the system is detectable and stabilizable,
feedback control with an observer can be designed. Before any results is shown with
respect to this matter the non-linear phenomena are shortly dealt with to give a full
transparency of the response. Hereby, providing detailed information that must be
accounted in the control properties.
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 3
Non-Linear System Response
In the previous chapter it was discovered that non-linearities existed in the system.
As the control theory is based on linear system control it is important to understand
how the non-linear effect propagates in the system. This is essential to establish the
model around a linearized operating point, while adequate precaution can be given to
the control implementation dealt wit in Chapter 4. If the system is highly non-linear
the controller can potentially cause instability of the system as the eigenvalues of the
system shift, which at all times must be avoided. The topic of this chapter is based
exclusively on experiments to reveal all the non-linear effects in the system. It is seen
that some non-linearities can be interpreted as disturbances while others is affecting
the discrepancy between the mathematical model and the physical system.
3.1
Non-Linear Dynamic Behaviour
It was found during the experimental work with the top foil that the natural
frequencies of the structure change depending on the amplitude of the excitation
signal. This can either be posed by a non-linear effect of the piezoelectric actuator, or
alternatively to a geometrical non-linearity of the top foil. Two different approaches
is used to study this. The analysis is based on excitation with white Gaussian noise,
with the excitation procedure found in Appendix A4. Moreover, the displacement
sensor is calibrated according to Appendix A1. To grasp the analysis correctly, there
is a distinction between two different parameters in the excitation signal denoted ˜u.
The ﬁrst is the change in amplitude deﬁned by three times the standard deviation
±3σ, while the latter is the DC-bias corresponding to the mean value µ. A distinction
between the two, can only be obtained if one parameters constant while the other is
changed. Finally, additional non-linear phenomena is sought of to assess the necessary
precaution. This makes it possible to conclude on the non-linear effect, and characterize
the important factors for the equilibrium the mathematical model is establish around.
Change in Amplitude
The experimental set up used to evaluate on the non-linear behaviour that correlates
with the change in amplitude is equivalent with the previously set up. A distinction is
a ﬁxing-aggregate as presented in ﬁgure 3.1.
==PAGE:58==
42
3 Non-Linear System Response
Figure 3.1: Schematic illustration of the electromechanical top foil, with ﬁxed boundary.
The sensor is placed according to Figure 2.14(d) on the left side of the top foil,
orthogonal to the surface, hereby all modes less than 500 Hz are observable. All
measurements are based on a 32 minute time interval with a sample frequency of
2000 Hz. The sensor is ﬁxed at the same location during all measurements, whereas
experimental frequency response functions are obtained based on the input voltage
and the output displacement. The excitation signal has a constant DC-bias at µ = 0 V
in all cased. The effect of different mean values cause a change in the electrical ﬁeld
of the actuator which is highly non-linear, this explanation is revised with more details
in section 4.1.
Regarding the results, the coherence is expected to decrease around antiresonance
where the ratio between the response and the magnitude of the measurement noise
will be large. The same argument is used for the FRF functions where a decrease in the
amplitude level of the excitation signal results in a lower response and the signiﬁcance
of measurement noise increases. To circumvent this undesired behaviour and avoid bad
coherence, the FRF is based on a large numbers of samples, why the large experimental
time is requested.
The results of the experiments is presented Figure 3.2, where the general response
in the bandwidth of the system ωb ≤500 Hz is presented in Figure 3.2(a). It is seen
that the natural frequency decrease with increasing level of excitation amplitude. A
zoomed section view of the response and the phase angle is shown to magnify the
non-linear behaviour with natural frequency for two different resonance peaks, ω1
and ω3, see Figure 3.2(b).

3 Non-Linear System Response
It is seen that the ﬁrst natural frequency ω1 change 6.5 %, while ω3 change 5.6%
proportional to the change in the amplitude of the excitation signal. There is also a
slightly indication that the stiffness of the top foil increased when the amplitude of ˜u
increase. This is seen as the overall magnitude of response decrease. Possible causes
for this non-linear dynamic is sought of in different explanations however before this
is pursued, the change in DC-bias of ˜u is attained.
Change in DC-Bias
A change in the DC-bias is characterized by a change in the mean value µ of the
excitation signal ˜u. If one consider a constant signal ˜u = µ, then the displacement y
of the top foil results in a curvature dependency where the radius of the top foil will
decrease with increasing DC-bias. If the random excitation is imposed on the input
signal forced response can be obtained with the curvature dependency. The amplitude
of the excitation signal is constant at ±3σ ≈121V. The experimental procedure is
almost equivalent, as the sensor position has changed. The non-linear dependency of
the top foil’s natural frequencies is obtained in Figure 3.3.

Figure 3.3: Frequency response function of the top foil showing non-linear dynamic
dependency of shell curvature radius which change the natural frequency. The amp-
litude of excitation signal is constant at ±3σ ≈121V, while the bias voltage change
according to µ ∈[−300 : 1300] V for the piezoelectric actuator.
==PAGE:61==
3.1 Non-Linear Dynamic Behaviour
45
It is seen that the change in DC-bias has the largest effect on the 3’rd natural
frequency deﬁned by ω3. Here, an increase of 11.3 % is discovered such that the
natural frequency change from 125 Hz to 141 Hz as the DC-bias increase from -300
V to 1300 V. It apply for all natural frequencies that the frequency increase with
increased DC-bias. This tells that the smaller the shell curvature the higher the natural
frequencies become, not necessarily correlated with the equivalent stiffness of the top
foil. The amplitude of vibration is moreover seen to drop with increase DC-bias. This
can be caused by two different reasons. Either the structural stiffness increase, or vice
versa the electrical coupling become weaker with increasing DC-bias. It is in general
very difﬁcult to conclude on this behaviour based on these simple empirical results.
Nonetheless the structure has both geometrical and electrical non-linearities. To what
extent is the most dominant is not explicitly stated, but it must be heavily accounted
for in the control system as it can potentially cause instabilities and degrade the active
performance. This is dealt with in chapter 4 where optimal model prediction with a
non deviating model is key to success.
The two previous examples shows that no correlation can be established between
the change in amplitude and the DC-bias of the excitation signal. While one effect
make the natural frequencies decrease the other caused an increase. This mean that
the electromechanical system is non-linear between the coupling of the piezoelectric
actuator and the structure. This is not accounted for in the mathematical model, why it
is proposed that the model must be tweaked according to shell curvature irrespectively
of pre-tensioned strain with the piezoelectric actuator. Moreover the piezoelectric
constants must be adjusted to obtain the desired response. This adjustment is possible
to perform within engineering assumptions of a maximum change of 5-10 % in the
physical constants.
Non-Linear Spring Behaviour
In continuation to understand the non-linearities, the test rig is studied. The top
foil is clamped as shown in Figure 3.1. Due to the pre-curved shape the structure will
experience a non-linear spring in the clamped boundary. This is caused since the top
foil is more likely to bend in a inward curved motion compared to an outward curved
motion that will tend to unfold the top foil. This is experimentally depicted in Figure
3.4), where the output displacement is based on a chirp function in the range of 0-500
Hz in 9 seconds. Please note that the enlarged displacement in the inward motion,
corresponding to a positive displacement, is most evident around resonance where the
response is ampliﬁed.
Strange Occurrence of Resonance Peaks
Piezoelectric actuators are known for non-linear effects. In the following no ex-
planations will be considered according to non-linear behaviour of an experimental
investigation. It is seen in Figure 3.5 showing the frequency response function H2 , that
for low amplitude response in the low frequency domain an introduction of additional
resonance peaks occur. This indicates that the non-linear effect from the piezoelectric
actuator, may become more predominant for low amplitude excitation.

Non-Linear Periodic Response
Until now, the system has been studied with forced response with white Gaussian
Noise. This subsection deal with non-linear phenomena arising with steady state
response with constant sinusoidal signal. The sinusoidal signal has an amplitude of
160 V, which result in the experimental response of the single top foil as shown in Figure
3.6. An elaborate analysis within the bandwidth of the system is presented in Appendix
A8, where a comparison to numerical time series is performed. Nonetheless ﬁgure
3.6, show examples of how a sinusoidal excitation signal can cause a non-sinusoidal
response. This is obtained at 10 Hz and 134, where the structure respond with a
multi periodic response 1.5 to 2 times faster than the excitation frequency. No further
studies are made on this objective as it is out of scope of the thesis. Finally, the effect is
assumed to be negligible as it only occur at these two frequencies. Moreover, it cause
no instability or inﬂuence in the control system as which is described in Chapter 4.

Piezoelectric Creep
Finally piezoelectric actuators are known for creeping effects, when steady state
response are to be obtained. This is sought of under conditions where the ampliﬁer
for the actuator is cold i.e. just turned on, and when it is warm i.e. have operated
for some hours. Hereby, it can be clariﬁed if there exist a correlation with the rate of
creep.
A response with multiple steps is send to the actuator, while the displacement is being
measured. Irrespectively of warm or cold condition, the creep occur with equal rate,
see Figure 3.7. This is clearly seen in the zoomed views in the middle ﬁgures, where
the response creep to to the steady state response, which is barely achieved within the
desired step time of 8 seconds. In addition to the steady state response, there exists
several other response characteristics which is deﬁned by the settling time, the rise
time and the overshoot. A detailed explanation of these are presented in 4.
A distinction between two response phenomena is attained in the zoomed views,
one with overshoot and another without overshoot. It is seen that there exist no
overshoot when the step change is heavily inﬂuenced by creep, which cause tension
in the top foil. However, as the step change is initiated from a steady state condition,
where all creep effect is eliminated from the response or alternatively when the step
changes direction then overshoot occur. This strange behaviour can be accounted for as
an inherited system disturbance. It is difﬁcult to model, and it can be compensated for
under control. A remark on this topic with it’s corresponding response characteristics
is made in the next Chapter dealing with control. It is stresses the importance of
this disturbance toward an active air bearing application, where it is crucial to deﬁne
a constant gap size between the rotor and the top foil. A remark on the difference
between the cold and the warm conditions are made according to the difference
between the two signals. Deﬁning the difference in the response according to
ydi f f = ycold −ywarm
(3.1)
then a fast Fourier transformation can be obtained based on the signal ydi f f , to check
which frequencies that occur. This is presented in Figure 3.8 where frequency content
is observed below 15 Hz, and around the system’s natural frequencies. By close
inspection, not presented here, it is concluded that the piezoelectric actuator has a
