==PAGE:65==
3.2 Non-Linear System Remarks
49
3.2
Non-Linear System Remarks
The general tendency of the non-linear phenomena dealt with in this section is
that some non-linearities occur as system disturbances while others occur as system
deviations towards the mathematical model. Consequently, it is forward from this point
in the thesis believed that the piezoelectric actuator is best utilized with somewhat
large input signals with amplitudes that does not impose a drift in the system’s natural
frequencies relative to the physical piezoelectric constants. In this way one maximizing
the power in the top foil with respect to the control signal, while respecting the linear
mathematical model established in chapter 2. Hence, the mathematical model should
be ﬁtted for the natural frequencies of the top foil which are found based on an
experimental FRF. It must be obtained for every experimental set up, and for different
top foils as the system dynamics is extremely dependent here on (see Appendix A3) in
order to obtain the best results. This is proposed by adjusting the geometry, size of the
top foil and actuator, and ﬁnally physical parameters for the piezoelectric actuator and
its coupling to the structure.
Based on the ﬁnding in this chapter it is interesting to see how a designed controller
can stabilize the system and increase the structural damping. Based on the geometrical
and electrical non-linearities it is tested the level of robustness the controller is able to
attain with the proposed model.
==PAGE:66==
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 4
Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
The following chapter deals with numerical and experimental implementation
of discrete time optimal controllers. It is based on the derived model in chapter 2.
Precaution are given to the inherited non-linearities in the top foil, found in chapter
3. Based on the proposed model, design of controllers and observers are performed.
Both applications are necessary for functional control system. The chapter provides a
comprehensive examination of the closed-loop performance, that is ﬁnally expressed in
terms of simulations and physical response. Although it is possible to derive controllers
and observers irrespectively of each other, referred to as the separation theorem, results
are presented with the two working in conjunction with each other.
The numerical implementation covers the design procedure of the controller while
the experimental implementation provides a validation. Moreover the experiments will
contribute with an evaluation of physical constraints and a measure of the performance
and robustness towards the mathematical model. In this way, proper insight of the
system can be obtained and future design considerations can be given the necessary
attention to improve the system.
The content of this chapter is based on design of two different optimal control
systems. It consists of a combined Linear Quadratic Regulator, with a optimal Kalman
observer. Such a control system is very robust and is known as the Linear Quadratic
Gaussian regulator (LQG). In this framework the two different optimal controllers is
obtained. The ﬁrst one is a standard LQG regulator while the latter is a LQG regulator
with integral action. Both regulators is designed for the SISO system corresponding
the single electromechanical top foil. The chapter borrows excerpts from a compulsory
assignment done in conjunction with the course Linear Control Systems 2, presented
by Nielsen and Michelsen 2014.
The assessment of the experimental implementation are immediately of greater
importance with stability issues compared to the ideal conditions in simulations and
must be treated accordingly. Moreover erroneous effects is less likely in simulations,
as long as a simple rules are ensured. Moreover the equations of modern control
theory must be treated with precaution towards the stability limits. Finally a remark on
control- and observer-spillover effects is included, to evaluate on sensor and actuator
position. This is based on performance investigations of the results, numerically as well
as experimentally. Attention should be given to the experimentally implementation to
ensure that the system attenuates the response, compared to the open-loop performance.
It is expected that improvements can be obtained.
==PAGE:68==
52
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
4.1
Design Speciﬁcations
The response of the Linear Quadratic regulator is proposed to fulﬁl a set of design
speciﬁcation within the bandwidth of ωb = 500 Hz. These are mainly deﬁned according
to the fast acting dynamics of a rigid rotor supported by air foil bearing, presented
by Larsen et al. 2015. Here the predominant time constant result in the following
requirements:
• a 10% step change in r0 of the LQR controller without integral action, should
produce a response in x(node 467) with a maximum of 5% overshoot and a
settling time between 0.01-0.02 seconds.
• a 10% step change in r0 of the LQR controller with integral action, should
produce a response in x(node 467) with maximum 5% overshoot, a settling time
between 0.01-0.02 seconds, and zero steady state error.
Moreover, the control signal must be bounded by a limit of u = [−500;1500] V, in order
to avoid the control signal from saturation caused by the ampliﬁer. With this regard it is
proposed by Damjanovic 1998, that the electric ﬁeld induced on piezoelectric ceramics
operates in a linear range if the applied potential is below a certain limit. This idea is
used by Nielsen et al. 2015 in the ﬁnal application of the electromechanical system
worked with in this thesis. It assumes that the mathematical model of the piezoelectric
patch has a linear response when the control signal remain within an interval of u ±300
V. Consequently the idea is that the control effort leaves the signal bounded strictly
within this range to avoid any of the previously discussed non-linear phenomena to be
present. This is important non-linearities potentially can cause physical instability of
the control system, arrived due to the discrepancy in the mathematical model.
Before the design speciﬁcations can be obtained in the response the next objective
is to enable digital processing of the continuous time state space model.
4.2
Discrete Time Model
The physical response phenomena in the electromechanical top foil are of continu-
ous nature. Consequently the state space representation in equation (2.65) has been
formulated in continuous time. However a discrete time representation is necessary to
comply with experimental implementation on a digital control unit, and a reformula-
tion of the continuous time model to discrete time may be performed. This is obtained
with a sampling period of Ts = 1 × 10−5 s, indicating the time period between adjacent
instances where the continuous model is valid. Here a notation k is used to indicate
the current instantaneous sample number. The discrete time model neglecting process
and measurement noise is presented as
x(k + 1) = Fx(k) + Gu(k)
(4.1)
y(k) = Cx(k)
(4.2)
The immediate assessment of the sampling period, indicates a sampling frequency
ωs = 100kHz that is ≈200 times higher than the smallest time constant of the system
bounded by the upper limit ωm = 500 Hz. In general simulations can be performed
==PAGE:69==
4.2 Discrete Time Model
53
Table 4.1: Sampling period Ts and sampling frequency ωs of the control system for
the electromechanical top foil.
Ts = 1 × 10−5 [s]
ωs = 100 [kHz]
with a sampling frequency ωs > 2ωm, sufﬁcient to reconstruct a sinusoidal motion
of the system response, and no information will be lost between adjacent instances.
Hereby aliasing is avoided and the discrete system is fully represented; in practice, this
is different. It is outlined in the following some of the limiting factors, causing the
choice of the sampling period found in Table 4.1. It is presented by Gopal 2009, page
80-82, which gives inspiration for the content and concluded on an optimal sampling
period for the top foil system. Special attention is given because the limiting factors
can cause system instability even though the system is closed-loop stable, which must
be kept in mind throughout this chapter. The selection of ωs is based on a trial and
error approach. Some conclusions are based also on empirical rules.
Generally a long sampling period reduces computational load and hence reduce the
hardware cost. However, as the sampling period is increased, a number of potentially
degrading effects start to become signiﬁcant, and cause instability of the closed-loop
performance of the control system typically with high frequency content. While the
lower limit of the sampling period is dictated by the hardware, the upper limit is
deﬁned by the degrading effects present as a results of the system dynamics, type of
control algorithm, control requirements and the characteristics of input and output
noise signals.
Limiting Factors
Information Loss due to Sampling
The frequency content of the system dynamics is in practice not strictly deﬁned by
ωm. Frequency components always exists outside the system bandwidth deﬁned by ωb.
Choosing a sampling frequency ωs > 2ωm = 2ωb is consequently associated with some
risks. In most cases frequency components outside ωb are present and will appear

Figure 4.1: Lowpass ﬁlter response
==PAGE:70==
54
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
as low frequency signals between 0 and ωs/2. This is known as aliasing which leads
to loss of information. It is thus of great importance to include an antialiasing ﬁlter
or use a relative high sampling frequency compared to the bandiwidth. However it
can be difﬁcult to interpret if disturbing frequencies occur in the signal due to aliasing
effect. Including a ﬁlter is considered as best practice.
Such a ﬁlter can both be analogue hardware based or digital. A digital ﬁlter such as a
FIR lowpass ﬁlter is not ideal with the cut-off frequencies, and introduces attenuation
and a phase shift of the signal, see ﬁgure 4.1. This leads to reconstruction errors, and
cause a high computational load in case of a optimal ﬁlter response. The digital lowpass
ﬁlter is tried implemented without success in order to obtain a stable system with
as large sampling period as possible. The ﬁltering of the high frequency component
caused computational overﬂow and prevented this solution. In contrast an analogue
hardware based lowpass ﬁlter will be more appropriate. It is almost always a part of
control systems, Hendricks et al. 2008 ,Chapra 2012. The trade off is immense costs to
allow this ﬁltering within the desired bandwidth of the system.
Information Loss due to Measurement Noise
Irrespectively of the observer design high frequency noise components above 2ωb
will be included in the signals. This arrive from measurement noise and will appear
as low frequency signals between 0 and ωs/2 due to the aliasing effect which cause
a loss of information. Again the aliasing can be avoided by use of an analogue anti
aliasing ﬁlter with a cut-off frequency much higher than the system bandwidth. Hereby
avoiding a signiﬁcant inﬂuence of the sampled data.
Destabilizing Effects
A phase shift may cause destabilizing effect of the closed loop performance. This
can occur by the absence of control action between computation of adjacent sampling
instances. The processing time of the control signal for the discrete time model cause
a dead time with the adjacent calculation. If the sampling period is too large the dead
time between the computations introduces a signiﬁcant phase shift. One approach to
avoid such an effect is obtained experimentally by selecting the sampling frequency
manually. The adjustment must be assessed by investigating the stability limits of the
closed loop response as the sampling period is increased. Hereby one ensure that no
signiﬁcant dead time occur.
Discretization Accuracy
This effect is caused by the discrepancy between the continuous time model, and
the discrete time model. The discretization method used in the project assumes that
the control input are constant over the sampling period Ts. A ﬁnite sampling period
will therefore result in lack of state information, available to the controller. The effect
is equivalent to the consequence of applying a change in the input signal. This causing
a step change of the output which excites a linear combination of the modes in the
system, and the controller will respond insufﬁcient to the continuous time system.
Only as the sampling period approaches zero the response can become fast enough
and free of residual dynamics, equivalent with the continuous time system.
==PAGE:71==
4.2 Discrete Time Model
55
Word Length Effect
As the sampling period Ts →0 the measured signal become ill conditioned due to
adjacent samples with similar magnitude. Digital control units have a magnitude and
precision limits on their ability to represent numbers, Chapra 2012.
Spillover Effects
Control spillover refers to the control action exciting vibration modes that are not
included in the model. Vice versa, observation spillover refers to the control action
being affected by part of the state not included in the model. When both control and
observation spillovers are present, they can destabilize a system which appears to be
stable based on the chosen model. However, if either one of these spillovers is zero,
then stability of the control on the reduced-order model implies stability of the control
on the full-order system, Dartmouth 2016.
Sampling Period and its Practical Aspects
With respect to the limiting effects described above, a conclusion can be made
towards the sampling period of electromechanical systems, here in terms of the piezo-
electric top foil. Electromechanical systems are generally governed by response with
high frequency content why the sampling frequency must be relatively high. Empir-
ical rules presented by Gopal 2009, page 80-82 deﬁnes the sampling period as one
tenth of the smallest time constant in the system. This give a sampling frequency
ωs = 10ωm = 5000 Hz. Unfortunately this is not sufﬁcient to make the system experi-
mentally stable due to the destabilizing effects, even though the designed controller is
stable. The topic of closed-loop stability are given attention in the next section.
No general rule of thumb can be applied to the sampling frequency. It should be
based on a rational decision on the understanding of its inﬂuence with the control
requirements for the closed-loop system, while the stability measure of the open-loop
system is held as a reference.
The top foil is a slender low damped structure, which response is signiﬁcantly attenu-
ated when active damping is damping added in terms of a controller. Consequently
considerations of selecting the sampling frequency should be treated accordingly. It
could for instance be imagined that an open-loop unstable system needs a very high
sampling rate to make the system closed-loop stable. Reasonable sampling rates that
accounts for this effect is as a rule of thump in the order of 10-30 the bandwidth of
the system. While a sampling frequency of ωs ≈12 kHz makes the system stable, the
processor in the control unit allows a sampling frequency of 100 kHz. This provides a
greater reliability measure to compensate for the missing anti aliasing ﬁlter. However
with an increasing model complexity of the top foil, the optimal sampling period may
be revised. Moreover it is strongly encouraged to include an anti aliasing ﬁlter for the
future application. The general conﬁguration of the digital control scheme is presented
in Figure 4.2.

Spillover effects has not been commented on in this regard. The importance is
emphasized later in the thesis where the practical implementation of the designed
controller raise concern to address this topic. As will be shown the implementation
involves aspects of observability and controllability. This is necessary to prevent
excitation of non-controllable modes, and to prevent the presence of residual modes in
the measured outputs. Here is referred to the residual modes as states which are not
included in the reduced model.
Discretization of the State Space Model
Throughout the remaining part of the thesis the sampling period obtained previ-
ously Ts = 1 × 105 s is used unless otherwise stated.
The continuous time model 2.65 is discretized using the MATLAB function
c2d(A,B,Ts), here A and B characterize the continuous complex separated reduced
order system, while Ts the sampling period. The output matrix remain unchanged, in
the form of (4.2), which gives the response of the system matrices F and G. Initially the
control system is designed to include the response of mode 1, mode 3 and mode 5.
This is based on previous ﬁndings that revealed a higher controllability of the bending
modes compared to the torsional modes. Moreover due to negligible response of the
torsional modes.

circle in the complex plane, |λ| ≤1, deﬁning the stability limit of discrete time system,
Hendricks et al. 2008, page 112. The magnitude of the eigenvalues for the discrete

the placement occur close to the extremity of the unit circle in the complex plane.
The imaginary part is small compared to the real part, indicating that little damping
occur. Though all eigenvalues are strictly inside the unit circle and non repeated,
the system is asymptotically stable. A conversion from discrete time eigenvalues to
continuous time λAj of the system matrix A can be performed. By comparison with the
eigenvalue assessment of the continues time system matrix, no signiﬁcant discrepancy

The observability and controllability of the system is evaluated with the explicit
measure given by the rank of the controllability- and observability-matrices. The rank
of the observability- and controllability-matrix is full, and the discrete time system is
fully stabilizable and detectable equivalent with the continuous time system. Moreover
the discretization does not change the structural property of the system matrix F, and
the modes is still uncoupled similar to the continuous time system.
Based on the now asymptotically stable discretized model 4.1, a threshold can be
established for the system, which provides the response characteristics given by steady
state value, settling time, and overshoot. This response is known as the open-loop
response.
4.3
Threshold of Open-Loop Response

response of the system. Hereby a illustrative comparison of the control performance
can be made. The steady state conditions that characterise the open-loop response
of the discrete time system is obtained by setting x(k + 1) = x(k) in equation (4.1),
which gives
x(k) = Fx(k) + Gu(k)
(4.6)

to u(k) = r0, where r0 characterize the reference value in voltage that keeps the output
y at the desired position. This deﬁnes the reference value strictly with a SI unit of
voltage V. By relating the output to the modal states characterized by the reduced order

However the general system response is very similar with respect to the transient
decay and settling time which is speciﬁed according to a 2% limit of the steady state
state value. Here the settling time is ≈0.6 seconds which is approximately 30 to 60
times slower than the design speciﬁcations. In relation to the overshoot of the response
it is found to be > 6.3 % which exceeds the threshold of 5%. There is thus many
indications that stress the importance of adding a controller to the SISO system in order
to substantiate compliance with the design speciﬁcations. However as the proposed
controller is based on full state feedback, information of all included modal coordinates
must be provided in discrete time. This is the topic of the next section.
4.4
Discrete Time Observer
Implementing a control system based on modern control theory and optimal control
design includes full state feedback. As previously stated the response of the discrete
time system is obtained in modal coordinated. No sensors can physically provide
measure of modal coordinates, and the mathematical model must be used to obtain
estimates that originates from a measured output with noise of the physical system. This
action is performed by an observer of the given system. In general no systems are free
from noise propagating in the system dynamics. Measurement noise from displacement
sensors (see appendix A1) and process noise characterized e.g. as a unbalanced mass in
a rotating system is the faced challenge of the observer. Irrespectively of what control
==PAGE:76==
60
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
purpose the LQG regulator fulﬁls, the discrete time system with this characteristic is
deﬁned as
x(k + 1) = Fx(k) + Gu(k) + Gvv1(k)
(4.10)
y(k) = Cx(k) + v2(k)
(4.11)
Here v1 indicate process noise, and v2 measurement noise. It is important to note
that process disturbances in terms of a unbalanced response is rejected, if the system
remain in this form. Alternatively it can be suppressed from the control action by
intelligent augmenting the system with the disturbance modelled. This is proposed
by Belleter et al. 2013, where rejection of unbalanced response is modelled into the
system according to:
y(t) = σ sin(ωet + φ)
(4.12)
here σ is the unknown amplitude, ωe the unknown frequency, and φ the unknown
phase. It can be presented by the differential equation
¨y = −ω2
e y = θ y
(4.13)
For the estimation of the frequency of the signal (4.12), an auxiliary ﬁlter is used
˙ζ1 = ζ2
˙ζ2 = −2ζ2 −ζ1 + y
(4.14)
ζ represents the cut off frequency of the signal. Using the ﬁlter (4.14) and the model
(4.13) one can ﬁnally obtained the disturbance model
y = −2˙ζ + ζ + θζ
(4.15)
Proceeding from this step will be to augment the system matrices describing the
top foil dynamic with the inclusion of the disturbance states which takes the form
xk(k + 1) = Fkxk(k) + Gku(k) + Gv,kv1(k)
yk(k) = Ckx(k) + v2(k)
Estimates of this system can be weighted by the probability measures of the process
noise, such that the estimate gives the state mean value of the disturbance, e.g. the
unbalanced response. The state vector xk of such a system is representing by the
initial top foil dynamics x and the additional disturbance states characterized by the
unbalanced response xu which has to be modelled accordingly. Rewriting the state
vector including the disturbance one get
xk =

x xu
T
This augmentation is crucial if the working principle of the active air bearing is based
on adding damping to the top foil dynamic, rather than controlling the unbalanced
response. Controlling the unbalanced response can be important but may cause too
high control effort to reject and must be kept in mind for the continued research
project.
Nonetheless the design in this chapter is based on response of a single top foil with no
process disturbance except from a stochastic excitation disturbance, characterized by
inﬂuence only on the inherited states why the system remain unchanged.

4.4 Discrete Time Observer
61
Designing Discrete Optimal Observer: Kalman Filter
The Kalman ﬁlter is the most optimal observer among all, under the assumption
that the measurement noise v1(k) and the process noise v2(k) is uncorrelated white
Gaussian noise with covariance matrices V1(k) and V2(k). It is build on the concept of
the state mean value ˆx(k) that propagate according to to a observer design given by
ˆx(k + 1) = Fˆx(k) + Gu(k) −L(k)[y(k) −Cˆx(k)]
|
{z
}
in(k)
(4.16)
Here L(k) has a minimum value that satisfy the response with respect to the uncertain-
ties build into the covariance matrices V1(k) and V2(k). This is done by minimizing
the error e between the true state and the estimated state.
e = x(k) −ˆx(k)
(4.17)
The principle is deﬁned by the state noise propagation with the Lyapunow equation
and the Riccati equation, that modify the states accounting for the noise intensity in
the system. Hereby the observer can be designed for any noise scenarios resulting
in optimal performance. Although the Kalman ﬁlter in general is time dependent a
suboptimal solution exist. This is under the assumption that the state and measurement
noise sources remain constant, while solving the Riccati equation. As will be later
emphasized this assumption is rough due to variations in the process noise, but the
ﬁlter remain optimal as long operation is performed around the equilibrium. To
emphasize the importance as noise change e.g. when the Gaussian excitation signal
with amplitude of ±3σ, disturb the structure the observer is not optimal. Although it
is robust and keep the system stable, it can potentially degrade the performance of the
LQG controller. Changing noise sources can alternatively arrive from a unbalanced
response of a rotor when the angular velocity is changed. Finally precise positioning
of the top foil may be acquired when process noise is non existing. In this case the
measurement noise become the most signiﬁcant and is governing the state estimation.
However this perspective is out of scope of this thesis, but must be revised when the
ﬁnal design of the active air bearing is assessed. Consequently the Riccati equation
is solved for the suboptimal steady state solution where Q(k + 1) = Q(k) = Q. A set
of coupled quadratic equations (4.18)-(4.19) which yields the error variance can be
found. Finally the suboptimal Kalman gain is obtained by evaluating equation (4.19),
which gives the closed form of the Kalman ﬁlter, Hendricks et al. 2008, page 457.
Q = FQF⊤+ GvV1G⊤
v −LCQF⊤
(4.18)
L = FQC⊤+

CQC⊤V2
−1
(4.19)
The MATLAB function dlqe(F,Gv,C,V1,V2) is used to perform the calculations,
which is somewhat based on a trial and error approach to achieve the optimal solution
for an experimental implementation. Initially the matrix Gv must be chosen, which
deﬁne how the state noise input affect the modal coordinates. It could be unity for all
discrete time modal coordinates, however it is assumed that the excitation signal used

Performance Assessment of Observer Gain
It is seen that the element in V1 is larger (corresponding to a large process noise),
and the elements in V2 is small (small measurement noise). This means that the
measurement can be trusted and which causes the large observer gain. This correlates
with the observer equation (4.16) where the residuals of the output, known as the
innovation
in(k) = y12B1(k) −Cˆx(k) = y12B1(k) −ˆy12B1(k)
(4.23)
will give large corrections in the state estimates of the discrete modal coordinates.
The ﬁlters output will thus be less dependent on the process model. Consequently the
==PAGE:80==
64
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
innovation will have a great inﬂuence on estimating the discrete modal coordinates,
indicating an observer with large measurement sensitivity due to the large observer
gains. Under ideal circumstances the probability measures of the innovation process
would be white noise equivalent with the size of the measurement noise, and the
process noise will not contaminate the innovation.
Initial Conditions and Reference Settings
To further establish the normative performance the observer response must be
obtained. It is important that the observer is working ideal without disturbances from
its inherited dynamics, emphasized with improper settings of the initial conditions. The
initial conditions in the integrators for the continuous time system or in the unit delay
for the discrete time system, must be chosen to eliminated zero steady state response.
Ideally the reconstruction of the states should be assessed immediately if correct initial
conditions is obtained. In case of wrong settings disturbing effects coming from the
observer will impose false transient vibrations in the state response with frequency
content equal to the natural frequencies of the observer. These vibrations will hence
decay (if the observer is stable) and the decay time before the observer keep track of the
system dynamics is solely deﬁned by the slowest time constant and its corresponding
damping of the observer. Consequently an eigenvalue assessment of the observer is
carried out in the next section. Mean while the transient vibrations will affect the
estimation of the true system and the result will be reduced controller speed in the
start-up phase. However, it can easily be avoided by correct settings of initial conditions
for the unit delay such that the states and the output is reached satisfactory from the
beginning.
The Steady state conditions characterising this response of the discrete time Kalman
ﬁlter is obtained by setting ˆx(k + 1) = ˆx(k) in equation (4.16).
ˆx(k) = Fˆx(k) + Gu(k) −L(k)[y(k) −Cˆx(k)]
(4.24)
by deﬁning the control signal equal the reference value, of the discrete time system, as
u(k) = r0, evaluated at the initial output y(k) = y0, the initial state conditions ˆxi0 can
be solved in equation (4.24). The set of initial conditions is thus deﬁned:
xi0 = (I −F −LC)−1 (Gr0 −Ly0)
(4.25)
the corresponding reference value r0 evaluated at the initial output is deﬁned according
to the open loop discrete time system derived in equation (4.7) as
r0 =
 C[I −F]−1 G

(4.26)

tip to the top foil can be deﬁned
y0 = 1.55mm

Eigenvalue Assessment of Kalman Filter
The eigenvalues associated with the Kalman ﬁlter provide information on the
stability as well as the speed of the observer. It is obtained by assessing the eigenvalues
of the observer system matrix which is based on Fkf = F - LC, and the characteristic
polynomial can be stated:
det(λFk f I −(F −LC)) = 0
(4.28)
The discrete time eigenvalues is presented in Table 4.4 and in continuous time deﬁnes
as the natural frequency in Table 4.5. It is seen that the discrete eigenvalues remain
strictly inside the unit circle of the complex plane as presented earlier, this will over
time eliminate the false state vibrations coming from improper initial conditions. The
natural frequencies of the observer is somewhat close to the natural frequencies of the
top foil system. This indicates that the Kalman ﬁlter exciting frequencies equal to the
observer poles, which results in a response contaminated with reconstruction error.
Although the observe does not provide the characteristics a rule of thumb that cause the
most optimal observer are eigenvalues theoretically 2-10 times faster than the smallest
time constant in the system. Hereby problem relating to observer spillover or aliasing

Numerical and Experimental Observer Response
A SIMULINK block diagram of the discrete time (Kalman Filter) is presented in
Figure 4.7. Both, simulations and experiments is carried out to obtain the estimated
output, and its compliance. It is based on two different response scenarios, providing
a detailed analysis. The former is a step change (case 1) in the input signal u(k),
resulting in transient vibrations with a linear combination of all modes of the top foil
and the observer dynamics. The later is equivalent to a forced response by excitation
with white Gaussian noise (case 2). Details regarding the excitation procedure can be
further studied in appendix A4.
Case 1 - Step Change
The results of the step change is presented in Figure 4.8. In the simulations, ideal
conditions is obtained with no degrading effects. It is evident that the estimate of
the Kalman ﬁlter keeps track of the output while ﬁltering the measurement noise, see
Figure 4.8(a). As the high frequency components of the transient vibration dies out,
the amplitude of the measurement noise become signiﬁcantly relative to the amplitude
of the structural response. Consequently the ﬁlter reduces the measurement noise on
the estimated output to provide the mean value. The performance is evaluated due
to the variance of the innovation. It reveals an innovation govern by white Gaussian

However the amplitude of vibration and phase of the estimate remain approximately
equivalent with the measured output. The Kalman ﬁlter keep track of the state in the
transient decay of all modes, while ﬁltering the measurement noise. In conclusion the
variance of the innovation deviates by 7.85% from the measurement variance which is
close to optimal from an engineering point of view.

Numerical Results
The next test scenario (case 2) is the forced response with white Gaussian Noise
where the purpose is to investigate the whiteness of the noise in the innovation.
Previously it was difﬁcult to see if the observer dynamics was subjected as disturbance
in the response, which is now the main focus. In this scenario, ideal conditions
is provided for the observer which assumes noise both in the process and in the
measurements. Generally the Kalman ﬁlter fails to operate optimal if the proposed
mathematical model is not perfect, or if the proposed observer is not fast enough. The
result is deterministic excitations in the innovation which in this case is analysed by

the FFT it is clear that a contaminant frequency occur at approximately 54 Hz in the
innovation. This frequency is equal to the slowest natural frequency of the observer.
The observer is consequently not fast enough which cause small deterministic excitation
in the response due to observer dynamics. It is stressed that great importance must be
added to this detail in experiments as it may cause ampliﬁcation around this frequency.

The experiments reveal the suspicion about degraded observer performance when
a non perfect mathematical model is used. It is conﬁrmed by deterministic occurrence
of frequency content in the innovation equivalent with the natural frequencies of the
top foil and the observer, which is obtained in the FFT Figure 4.10(b). The small
discrepancies in the amplitude of the frequency response function and the location of
resonances and anti resonances presented in section 2.3, is the second cause of the
degraded Kalman ﬁlter quality. The amplitude at ω1 = 28Hz (for the top foil) in the
FFT is two orders of magnitude higher compared to the contaminant from the observer
poles as well as the higher order modal response. By possessing the autocorrelation
function in Figure 4.10(c), the signal correlation is revealed. This conﬁrm that the
innovation is not white. In fact the variance of the innovation deviates as much as
10500% from the variance of the measurement noise.
In conclusion to the above stated performance characteristics, the observer design
in terms of the Kalman ﬁlter, is considered sufﬁcient for the control application. This is
somewhat surprising conclusion as the observer can cause relatively large deviations
in the innovation. Although it provide a steady state error in the reconstruction, a
solution exists by inclusion of a controller with integral action. By inspection of the
magnitude of the output and its estimate, a quantitative measure results in a deviation
of up to 12 µm in the estimate, when the amplitude of the true output is in the order of
220 µm. Due to this relative precision, the observer is though fast and viable enough to
reconstruct the output signal from the measurement both in terms of a step change and
forced response by white Gaussian noise within the desired bandwidth. However great
details to the observer speed must be considered for further analysis, as frequency
content from the observer is imposed in the response, known as observer spillover. A
summation of the results emphasized in this section is listed in Table 4.6.

4.5 Discrete Time LQG

4.5
Discrete Time LQG
In this section the discretized state space model is used to design the LQR controller.
The design procedure of the LQR controller is based on a trial and error approach until
the response remain within the design speciﬁcations. Finally the overall response of
the LQG system is evaluated, where the Kalman ﬁlter and the state feedback work in
conjunction with each other.
Designing Optimal LQR Controller
Since the system was found to be stabilizable and detectable, an optimal LQR
controller, that minimize the discrete performance index (4.29), can be designed. It is
assumed that the state vector x is fully accessible based on the Kalman ﬁlter designed
in the previous section.
J = 1
2
∞
X
k=0

x⊤(k)Qx(k) + u⊤(k)Ru(k)

(4.29)
Note that the requirements for a lower bound is avoided due to the selection of the
weighting matrices Q ≥0 and R > 0.
Since the discrete system with Kalman ﬁlter was proven to be controllable, the optimum
value of the performance index (4.29) Jmin has an upper bound with a limiting value
for k →∞, and the suboptimal control gain KDLQR can be found by solving the steady
state discrete-time algebraic Riccati equation:
Pd∞= Q + F⊤[Pd∞−Pd∞G
 R + G⊤Pd∞G
−1 G⊤Pd∞]F
(4.30)
Note that Pd∞=Pd(k+1)=Pd(k) which deﬁnes the steady state solution. The control
gain KDLQR is found according to:
KDLQR =
 R + G⊤Pd∞G
−1 G⊤Pd∞F
(4.31)
Weighting Matrices
To explicitly determine the control gains, the weight matrices Q and R must be
chosen such that it accounts for the control signal which is limited in size and bandwidth.
Here the bandwidth also refer to the difference in controllability of the different modes
of the top foil. It is important to remember that it is the relative size of the weights in
Q and R that determines the speed in controlling the state and the associated size of
the control signal. While R is associated with the control input Q is referred to modal
coordinates in the reduced system.
Due to the complex separated reduced order system, no cross coupling occur between
the modes of vibrations presented by the decoupled system matrix F. An exception is
here from each complex separated state which introduce a cross coupling between the
imaginary and the real part. This is however of insigniﬁcant importance, and is not
being given special attention. The immediately assessment of the diagonal elements
Qii in the state weighting matrix are being given particular importance, in controlling
==PAGE:88==
72
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
the individual mode. This is further emphasized since no modes need to affect one
another, why the selection is restricted only to the diagonal elements.
The result of choosing an element size is a penalization relatively to the size of the
adjacent elements in the matrix. Thus if one element corresponding to a particular
mode is large compared to the others, the cost function and the optimal control gain will
emphasize making this mode less dominant in the system response. The penalization
can also be used to give particular importance in the control effort to the modes less
controllable, hereby resulting in an equivalent mode performance.
The weights in Q are found through a trial and error approach, that ensures
fulﬁlment of the deﬁned speciﬁcations. The controller is implemented in a SIMULINK
system to enable ongoing simulation while the weightings in Q are modiﬁed. Values in
R, are determined based on knowledge of the piezoelectric actuator. It is limited by
the piezoelectric actuator and its ampliﬁer with a control signal within the range of
u = [−500;1500] V. Moreover it must fulﬁl the design speciﬁcations, that ensures a
linear response. An approximate initial value is proposed by Hendricks et al. 2008,
page 307 where:
Rj j =

The eigenvalues of the closed-loop system must be assessed to evaluate the stability.
The same stability criterion must be obtained for the system to be asymptotically stable,
and is found by evaluation of:

accounted compared to the open loop performance. The length of the poles is approx-

the complex eigenvalues. Generally it can be difﬁcult to make any conclusions on the
system performance based on discrete time eigenvalues. Consequently a conversion
to continuous time eigenvalues is proposed using equation (4.5). Hereby physical
system properties can be linked to the modal response. Eigenvalues in continuous
time includes characteristic knowledge of the natural frequency and the damping ratio.

increased by 71 % of mode 1, 7 % of mode 3 and ﬁnally 0.6 % of mode 5.
Initial Conditions and Reference Settings
Similar as for the Kalman ﬁlter, one must ensure correct initial conditions for the
unit delay in the discrete time system. Moreover a correct reference value that satisfy
the corresponding output must be calculated, hereby ensuring that the LQR controller
does not perform any control action in the start-up phase. In case this is not obtained
the controller will immediately drive the output to the corresponding reference value
to satisfy the speciﬁed reference. This will degrade the regulator which enquire a
settling time to eliminate the preliminary dynamics. Nonetheless it will be difﬁcult to
keep physical track of the steady state response of the output as no reference control to
the output is made. This topic is dealt with in the next section where the inclusion of
integral action performed this behaviour. An interaction between the reference value
and the initial conditions for the LQR controller is thus proposed. Hereby a reference
value can be calculated which ensure a steady state error that keeps the output at the
desired position.

Closed-loop Performance
The fulﬁlment of the design speciﬁcation is associated with a minor mistake. Origin-
ally the results should be based on a 10 % step change in the reference value, however
it ended up being 7.7% step change deﬁned by an experimental mistake. Disregarding
this error the design speciﬁcations for controlling the top foil displacement is presented
and proven in Figure 4.12(a) for the numerical response, and 4.12(b) for the experi-
mental response respectively. The corresponding control signal and the reference can
be investigated in Figure 4.12(c).
It is seen that the calculated reference signal r0 keeps the output of the top foil at
the desired position y0 = 1.55mm. After 1 second a step change in the reference signal
is imposed. The response evaluation of the top foil is based on a 2% settling time, and
the overshoot is deﬁned relative to steady state value x f inal.
The response of the LQR controller introduce an experimental overshoot less than 3 %
caused by the fast speciﬁcation for the settling time. However the linear response of the
inherited system modes (mode 1, 3 and 5) excited by the step change is well established
in the mathematical model, and the controller attenuates the response signiﬁcantly
encountered by the almost immediately cancellation of the transient vibrations.
By comparison of the experimental and the numerical response, it is seen that the
settling time is 0.016 s for the experiments, and 5 times faster in the simulations, res-
ulting in somehow good correlation between experiment and the simulation. However
the output does not scale equal, and the relative change of the steady state response
imposed by the step change in the numerical model is 28% smaller compared to the
relative change in the experiments. The change in 7.7% of the reference is hence not
equal to a similar change in the experimental output, which emphasize the need of
integral action to fulﬁl such a requirement.

Spillover Effects
A more generic view of the experimental response and the control signal in Figure
4.12 reveal another distinctive difference. The amplitude of the steady state response is
inﬂicted by the occurrence of a ampliﬁed frequency at 70 Hz. This is seen in the control
signal, and further in the FFT in Figure 4.13. Previous analysis tell that the presence of
this frequency is caused by the 2nd mode of vibration. This mode is a torsional mode
which is not included in the controller design for the controlled states denoted xc as the
controllability and the observability of this mode was found to be very poor. The mode
0
70
150
Frequency [Hz]
0
0.5
1
|Y(f)|
×10-5
Figure 4.13: Fast Fourier Transformation of the experimental output signal under LQR
control action
==PAGE:93==
4.5 Discrete Time LQG
77
ended up being a residual modes denoted xr not included in the model. However
as previous experiments showed, the response of the 2nd as well as the 4th mode can
not be neglected in the output due to the sensor location and the actuator location.
Even hough the problem does not arise in the simulations due to perfect symmetry
in the mathematical model, small imperfections occur in practice which explains why
an ampliﬁcation in the steady state response is present in the experiments and not
the simulation. It relates to the difﬁculties in joining the piezoelectric actuators with
high tolerances, see appendix A2. As the controller try to reject the response at 70
Hz the actuator causes excitation of the present mode, which the controller does not
encounter for. A solution to this undesired behaviour is given particular importance
in the next section. However the most obvious way to circumvent the problem is to
eliminate the response in the output, and in the control signal. This has been proposed
by Gupta et al. 2010 in dealing with smart piezoelectric structures. It is stated that
the results of a sensor output being contaminated by residual modes via the term Crxr
cause observer spillover, while the actuator in terms of the feedback control excites
the residual modes via the term Gru known as control spillover. This phenomena must
be avoided. Potentially these two effects may cause the eigenvalues of the closed-loop
system to shift away from their decoupled location which is assumed in the control
law. As the stability margin of all modes in the discrete system is close to the unit
circle, a small shift in the eigenvalues can cause the residual modes to become unstable,
which is known as spillover instability. See Figure 4.15 for a schematic overview of the
spillover effects.
In this framework the result is less dramatic, and the observer spillover as well as the
control spillover causes a natural ampliﬁcation of the top foil at this mode. The reason
why spillover instability does not occur may be the result of the very small sampling
period chosen for the system, hereby allowing the controller to stabilize the system.
Recall the experimental mode shapes obtained in the experimental modal analysis.
The best way to avoid the spillover phenomena is to place the sensor such that the

observability of this mode is minimized. This can be obtained by locating the sensor
at a nodal line of the particular mode on the top foil, see Figure4.14. Hereby the
response of the 2nd mode shape with ω2 = 70 Hz is minimized in the response, and
the controller causes less ampliﬁcation when rejecting the amplitude of the response.
Unfortunately it is not possible to relocate the sensor in the experimental set up, and
an alternative method must be obtained. The same acquires for the actuator, that
however is impossible to remove due to the adhesive and the size of the patch. Control
spillover can thus not be avoided since the trade off in installing multiple actuators or
a reduced size actuator causes a more complicated system. The result of this strategy
will also be a lower power input on the more dominant modes of an air bearing system
mainly associated with the bending modes.
Discussion and Experimental Remarks
The generally conclusion of the LQR performance is overall a satisfying response
and the top foil dynamics can strongly be affected by the piezoelectric patch. Selection
of the sampling period was the most cumbersome task in the design of the controller
which often resulted in spillover instability. A trial and error approach was performed to
avoid destabilizing effects while the weighting matrices were modiﬁed. Moreover the
controller does not demand to high control effort which is emphasized by the magnitude
of the control signal, and the controller is satisfactory although the performance of
==PAGE:95==
4.5 Discrete Time LQG
79
the controller is faster in the simulations compared to the experiments see Table 4.9.
Regarding the spillover effects present in the response, twist modes is the governing
modes that cause trouble and must be accounted for if ampliﬁcation around these
frequencies should be avoided. This is believed since no higher order modes outside the
system bandwidth was present in the response. Residual modes either at is inherited
signal or downscaled due to aliasing outside the system bandwidth deﬁned by ωn = 500
Hz is consequently neglected.

Forced Response of LQG Controller
As the controller was found to fulﬁl the design speciﬁcations, it is now desired
to obtain the general response in the frequency domain. Similarly to the previous
section it provides information of how the observer and the state feedback will work
in conjunction with each other, when the system is exposed to a random excitation.
The experimental response is obtained by an excitation of the top foil with white
Gaussian noise, while the output is measured. These are used to obtain frequency
response functions. The SIMULINK block diagram presented in ﬁgure S1.2 in Appendix
S1.2 is the used set up.
According to the numerical prediction the frequency response is obtained based on
the continuous time system assuming that the reference is a time varying harmonic
oscillator with frequency ω. This will cause a linear response of the model which
will have an oscillating motion with equivalent frequency. The governing system
representing the LQG regulator is proposed by Hendricks et al. 2008, page 273, which
is obtained by augmenting the state vector with the estimation error ee.

The performance of the standard LQG regulator is evaluated according to the
experimental response. The response is equivalent with the open-loop response at 0
Hz, where the input is in phase with the output. From this point onwards the LQR
regulator impose immense damping on the 1st mode at 28 Hz, until the occurrence
of the 2nd natural frequency. Here the mathematical model deviate from the physical
system causing an ampliﬁcation in the response. Nonetheless the controller moreover
succeeds in controlling the 3rd mode, which response is signiﬁcantly attenuated. Finally
in the range after 170 Hz, the model deviates, and the absence of the 4th mode causes
a shifted in the 4th natural frequency of the electromechanical system, causing severe
ampliﬁcations in the response. Moreover the coherence is drop after 170 Hz which
is caused by a magnitude of vibration equivalent with the level of the measurement
noise. Fortunately is the overall response reduced considerably. This can be seen very
clearly in the physical response presented by the output in Figure 4.17. It is the result
of the system’s response obtained by excitation with a chirp function, in the frequency
range from 0-200 Hz in a duration of 9 seconds.
4.6
Discrete Time LQG with Integral Action
Active Air Bearing systems have their strength compared to passive systems, espe-
cially when the capability of maintaining a constant gap size between the rotor and
the top foil is obtained. This is in particular an advantage as the stiffness of the air ﬁlm
forces change depending on the gap size. The system dynamic was previously found
to change depending on geometrical constraints. Thus a constant gap size by means
of controller can stabilized the system’s geometry to operate around the linearized
set point. As proposed earlier the control will moreover work in conjunction with the
capability of imposing active damping to the top foil, while rejecting or minimizing
disturbing effects from an unbalanced response in the ﬁnal rotor system application.
In this framework a control system with the inclusion of integral action will permit
the emphasized performance. Hereby the output is directly involved in the control,
allowing a predetermined stationary relationship between the input and the output in
==PAGE:98==
82
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
the system. However the inclusion of an integral state is known from Hendricks et al.
2008, page 239 to give give rise for dynamical problems due to a negative phase shift
of the integrator. This concern is dealt with through numerical as well as experimental
validation to provide the overview of the LQG performance with integral action. The
result are expected to attenuate the response, and dissipate energy in the system which
are presented based on forced response, giving the performance. The section covers
both design of a controller and a new Kalman ﬁlter.
Augmented Discrete Time State Space system
Integral control action is implemented by augmenting the state vector and the
discrete time system matrices denoted with the subscript ()a. Hereby the design
procedure for the state feedback and the observer design, as elaborated in the previous
section can be reused. A maximum of one integrator is added to the system strictly
deﬁned by the number of outputs in the system. This cause an increase of 1 new
eigenvalue in the system.
Previously spillover effects was present in the output which caused ampliﬁcations in
the response with frequencies equal the natural frequencies of the twist modes. Special
attention is now given to eliminate this, why the reduced order complex separated
system in modal coordinates contain 4 modes instead of 3. These are:
• Mode 1 (28 Hz)
• Mode 2 (70 Hz)
• Mode 3 (126 Hz)
• Mode 5 (416 Hz)
It is important to note that the reduced system is restricted by the inclusion of
a force asymmetry imposed in the input vector of the full state system in physical
coordinates. This was previously dealt with in section 2.3. The force asymmetry is
chosen mainly to inﬂict the 2nd torsional mode, which was the dominant frequency
in the spillover. The result is a state space model which is associated with a more
realistic response that minimize the discrepancy between the physical and and the
mathematical model.
The augmented state vector is
xa = [

Figure 4.18: Implementation of the discrete time Linear Quadratic Regulator - KDLQR
presented with the Kalman Filter.
Including the state estimation through the Kalman ﬁlter, and the state feedback
with integral action the closed-loop LQG response is shown in the block diagram in
Figure 4.18. It is seen that the structure of the LQR controller is somewhat different
from the previous set up, although the same non-augmented system matrices is used.
The integral state provides an outer loop feedback which is the new primary summation
point where the system error vector ee is being driven to zero as the direct consequence
of the integrator (in this case a unit delay). The result is a representation of the
reference signal r which is now representing the gap size with SI units in meter instead
of the previous case with voltage.
By equating the discrete time system matrices the observability and controllability
can be assessed. The controllability is once again assessed with the build in MATLAB
function ctrb(Fi,Gi). The rank of the controllability matrix is 6, which is equal to
the non-augmented system with the exclusion of the 2nd mode and the integral state.
Thus the system appear not to be fully controllable. By evaluating the eigenvalues
of the system matrix all modes are stable, and the eigenvalue of the integrator is
equal 1. The system is consequently stabilizable as the uncontrollable modes is stable.
The eigenvalues for the controllable modes has not changed compared to the non-
augmented system, which mean that the same values can be imposed in the weighting
matrices Q and R, while additional weights must be applied for the integral and the
new states. A similar assessment is carried out for the observability with the function
obsv(Fi,Ci) where the rank of the observability matrix is 8. The only non observable
state is the integral state.

Based on this evaluation it is possible to make a LQG regulator with integral action.

can be investigated in (4.51).

Keeping the weights from the non-augmented system as a reference it is seen that
the integral weight is relative large. This result is large control gains which is seen in
equation (4.52). Hereby the input voltage will be adjusted heavily to obtain the right
bias voltage of the piezoelectric actuator necessary for keeping the top foil positioned
at the desired position. This way the zero steady state error, with the fast settling
time as speciﬁed in the design requirements can be obtained. Another interesting
observation is the weighting for the 2nd modal coordinate. In this case the relative
size between Ri and Qi,33 is three orders of magnitude higher compared to the lowest
mode penalization. This result in control gains which is much larger compared to the
adjacent modes and the controller will spend a huge effort in controlling the second
mode. In this way the controller compensates from the reduced controllability of the
mode. The results of the particular attention is a rejection of the spillover effects.
Again the eigenvalues for the closed loop response can be assessed by evaluating
the discrete time dynamic state matrix FKi = Fi −GiKDLQRI. The eigenvalues are seen to
be asymptotically stable from Table 4.10. A conversion to continuous time eigenvalues
by means of the natural frequency and a ramping ratio is necessary to conclude on
the system performance. This is presented in Table 4.11, where the introduction of
the control gains again provide signiﬁcant damping in the system which is enhanced
by as much 50 times for mode 1. The ﬁnding is equivalent with the standard LQR
controller, although the inclusion of the integral state and the 2nd mode is represented.
The closed loop LQR system with integral action increase the natural frequencies by
72 % of mode 1, 0.4 % of mode 2, 7 % of mode 3 and ﬁnally 0.6 % of mode 5. This
mean that the dynamics of the higher modes remain somewhat unchanged, and that
the model is well established.

To ﬁnd the initial condition of the discrete integrators, that ensures zero regulations
error at the starting point. Similar procedure as described in the section with the

through the reference, and cause similarity with the open-loop initial conditions. A

==PAGE:104==
88
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
The inclusion of the 2nd mode and the integral action is now investigated through
the experimental response. Unfortunately the unﬁltered output is feed into the con-
trol system contaminated with measurement noise and observer spillover. This cause
spillover effects to be more likely, and a counteracting measure is proposed by using
the output estimate (obtained by the Kalman ﬁlter) as the state feedback for the in-
tegral part. Hereby the state mean value will provide information for the outer loop
control feedback which will be free of control action against measurement noise and
uncontrollable modes. The piezoelectric actuator will in this way not be overloaded
by means of fatigue excitation with high frequency content from the measurement
noise, and observer spillover will be minimized. However this was not carried out
due to an oversight of the inherited problem, moreover the observer was previously
found to predicted a steady state error in the innovation. The suboptimal solution
with the measured output has consequently been used, and the designed controller
is able to compensate for the spillover effects within the accepted design speciﬁca-
tions. The experimental response is distinguished between a control system with 3
modes including spillover effects (see Figure4.19(c)-4.19(d)), and the reﬁned 4 modal
control system, which is able to compensate for the spillover effect in the output (see
Figure 4.19(e)-4.19(f)). The adjustment of the control system removes the noteworthy
spillover frequency at 70 Hz corresponding to the now included mode.
The recurrent conclusion of an accepted settling time which exceeded the design spe-
ciﬁcations, is due to destabilizing effects probably from spillover instability of higher
order twist modes. This restrains the aggressiveness of integral control. The settling
time of the integral controller is dominated by the integral weight, which in too ex-
treme case caused these higher order twist modes to become unstable or ampliﬁed
in the response. Even with the inclusion of these unstable/ampliﬁed modes in the
system, the control spillover and the observer spillover become signiﬁcant compared
to the counteracting control measures that was tried implemented. Consequently, the
inclusion of additional modes did not provide the necessary robustness. This conclusion
again emphasize the need of a reduced observability in the system to avoid detection
of the non-controllable modes. The solution is a relocation of the displacement sensor.
Nonetheless by the compromise in the performance, extremely good coherence exists
between the numerical and the experimental results, see Table 4.13. The threshold of
5% overshoot is much higher than the actual overshoot present by 1 %. The response
provides a zero steady state error in the output that is scaled approximately linearly
with time. The change to a integral control system implies that the input become
somewhat proportional to the settling time deﬁned by the integral weight. Therefore a
more robust controller will be obtained by a decrease in the integral weight resulting

Due to the performance of the response a signiﬁcant improvement of the normal
LQR regulator is obtained. The corresponding reference change, regulation error and
control signal of the response can be investigated in Figure 4.20.
By evaluating the numerical control signal in Figure 4.20(b) it is seen to be overestim-
ated by 156 % of the experimental control signal. This gives rise to more elaborate
research about the electromechanical coupling, to correct this error in the mathematical
model. As has not been emphasized before, the LQR regulator is moreover able to
compensate for the creep effect of the piezoelectric actuator, which is seen in the slowly
decrease in the experimental control signal over time. Moreover the magnitude of the
signal is more realistic towards a ﬁnal application, where the magnitude of the signals
is in the right order such that the electric ﬁeld imposed by the applied voltage does
not change the linearised set point of the piezoelectric actuator that remains operated
in the linear range.
Finally the regulation error in seen in Figure 4.20(c). The optimal LQR controller
with integral action strives to minimize the error between the reference signal and the
output e = r −y. With the calculations of the initial conditions for the integrators in
SIMULINK, it is seen that the error is e = 0, at the time t = 0. The initial conditions
satisfy the start-phase without conﬂicting the states with transient vibrations due to
initial control. It is moreover seen that the error is largest when the step is applied,
and converge toward zero as expected.

fair comparison of the two.

The numerical response performed by the LQG regulator with integral action,
corresponds very well with the experimental results. It is seen that the amplitude of
the frequency response function is ampliﬁed when the mathematical model deviates
from the physical system. However as the scale is logarithmic, the damping of the
natural frequencies is still noteworthy, and the ampliﬁed response is signiﬁcant lower.
Above 170 Hz, the modes cause large deviations compared to the physical system, and
the result is a shift of the 4th natural frequency. This result in the 4th mode which has
the largest response, caused by the active attenuation of the 1st and the 3rd mode. The
general amplitude of the response is however not ampliﬁed compared to the open-loop
==PAGE:108==
92
4 Discrete Time Optimal Control Design
response which is surprising.
Unlike for the numerical results of the LQG regulator where the amplitude is signiﬁcant
larger at lower frequencies, the experimental response cancels low frequency vibrations.
By inspection of the coherence for the LQG regulator, the deviation can potentially be
caused by a bad coherence. Logically speaking the compromise of the somehow slow
settling time, may be the direct result of the ampliﬁcation.
The overall conclusion is a well functioning LQG regulator, which enhance the system
performance signiﬁcantly. In the presence of large vibrations illustrated in this section,
the measurement noise is less dominating in the output and a potential fatigue overload
of the piezoelectric actuator is less likely under such operational conditions.
4.7
Comparison of LQG & LQGi Controller
Performance
In conclusion to section 4.5 and 4.6 the response i now compared between the
standard LQG regulator and the one with integral action. Both regulators are concluded
eventually to fulﬁl the design speciﬁcations described in section 4.1. The systems is
able to observe the inherited dynamics based on a ﬁltered estimate from the Kalman
ﬁlter, derived in section 4.4. Furthermore the system was able to maintaining a stable
control action based on the Kalman ﬁlter, by feed forward on the displacement of the
top foil. This enabled a reduction in the overall magnitude of the system’s vibrations,
which was reduced 80% of the open-loop response. Moreover the system’s response
was attenuated and the modal damping of the most predominant mode #1, increased
50 times compared to the open-loop damping.
The faced challenges in the design phase was spillover instability, which was more
likely in the system with integral action. The general conception of robustness is thus
lower for the LQG regulator with integral action. Another trade off is the increased
level of complexity with the preventive action against a steady state error. A necessary
prevention against spillover instability was a complex inclusion of twist modes in
the reduced order system before the system’s response was acceptable. However a
relocation of the output at a nodal line of the twist modes is recommended to minimize
the observer spillover of the particular modes. A slightly better performance are
obtained with the LQG regulator with integral action, which is seen in Figure 4.22.
Conversely the standard LQR controller was the simplest and the fastest. As to what
criteria are the most optimal controller is determined by the ﬁnal application, the
active gas bearing.
In this framework it is stressed that particular importance should be given to tune
the system’s resonances (natural frequencies) and antiresonances in the mathematical
model. Hereby the system response can be even further optimized as the control
performance is based on optimal model prediction. Finally is concluded that the
controllers are able to stabilize the non-linear system based on the linear assumption.
The controllers enabled a compensation for the creep effect of the piezoelectric actuator
that drifted the steady state response of the top foil over time.

Roubustness of LQG Regulator
Finally is a remark on the particular useful characteristics of the LQG controller.
There exist an inherent guaranteed large gain margin, phase margin and tolerance
of modelling error Hendricks et al. 2008, page 324. 60 ◦deﬁnes the phase margins
for all inputs. Even though the design is based on optimal model prediction, and the
model may deviate signiﬁcantly, it is by far the best performing controller. Nonetheless
the optimal response is only ensured by proper tuning of the mathematical model
towards uncertainties. The main uncertainties is in this case manufacturing tolerances,
geometrical parameters, the joining characteristics of piezoelectric patch as well as its
precise location and ﬁnally the linearized set point of the piezoelectric constants.
==PAGE:110==
==PAGE:111==
Chapter 5
Coupling of Rotor System
In the previous chapters the model was validated against experimental test, and the
electromechanical top foil subjected to optimal control law was able to enhance modal
response signiﬁcantly. This was enabled by use of piezoelectric actuation whereby
active damping was found capable to be imposed on the structure. In continuation
of the gradual development of active gas bearings, coupling with air ﬁlm forces are
now established. The durability of the compliant structure with piezoelectric actuation
is the overall research objective in this chapter. It is investigated how the inﬂuence
of a change in the gap size between the rotor and the top foil change the equivalent
damping and stiffness coefﬁcients of the air ﬁlm. This is done with respect to a change
in angular velocity of the rotor such the rate of the coupling can be varied. The two
main properties to study are thus angular velocity and compliant gap size dependency.
A new rotor with diameter Ø85 has been manufactured, which is initially described.
A malfunction is caused by a misalignment of the rotor center under manufacturing
which prevent the test rig from optimal use according to the speciﬁed operating
principles. Notch ﬁlters and run-out compensating have been tested for a solution to
the inherited misalignment. The content of this chapter are primarily based on an
experimental investigation.
5.1
Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
The experimental apparatus dealt with in this section is shown in Figure 5.1. It has
a leading edge gap size between the rotor and the top foil deﬁned by h0. To position the
top foil and the radial gap size, two degrees of freedom has been added to the module
clamping the top foil. These are deﬁned by a translational and rotational according
to l and θ, while ω describes the angular velocity of the rotor. l and θ are ﬁxed
immediately after the desired positioning has been obtained. The speciﬁed locking
position is obtained under random excitation of the top foil. When just accurately no
mechanical contact occur between the dynamic response of the top foil and the rotor
at steady state condition ( ω = 0) the top foil is positioned satisfactory. Hereby one
ensure a study of increasing effect of air ﬁlm forces with increasing ω, with a starting
point equivalent to previous results. Please note that the top foil is clamped at the
leading edge and the motion of the rotor orbit and unbalanced response is assumed to
be zero.
==PAGE:112==
96
5 Coupling of Rotor System
θ
ω
h0
l
Figure 5.1: Experimental test module allowing coupling between the electromechanical
top foil and the rotor with angular velocity ω. Two degree of freedom allow the
clamping device to translate the top foil along the longitudinal direction deﬁned by l,
while θ denote the inclination angle deﬁning the radial gap size function, here shown
as a converging. The leading edge of the top foil has a prescribed gap size of h0.
Assuming a simpliﬁed dynamical system of the top foil with an equivalent mass
mt and stiffness kt, additional dynamics couples to the system by the air ﬁlm forces
as shown in the electromechanical system in Figure 5.2. A laminar ﬂow is build up
between the rotor with angular velocity ω and the top foil. The resulting force is a
distrubuted area force known for its highly non-linear dependency with ω. However, it
can be linearized by equivalent damping and stiffness coefﬁcients deﬁned by kr and br

previously studied by Larsen and Santos 2015. As depicted in ﬁgure 5.2 the radial gap

The test procedure is carried out by exciting the top foil with white Gaussian noise
according to appendix A4. Frequency response functions between the input voltage
and the output displacement are assessed to establish the dependency with increasing
ω. All results are obtained within the bandwidth of 500 Hz. The sensor is thus located
to providing full order observation of all modes within this range.
5.2
Misalignment of Rotor
Before any results are obtained, the dynamics of the rotor is studied. The rotor is
assumed to be dynamic free and rigid with no unbalanced mass. Moreover, it should
be ﬁxed at it’s center position. In this way only the inﬂuence of the laminar air ﬂow
between the rotor and the ﬂexible top foil can be studied without excess of other
dynamics. Of cause this requires very high speciﬁcation of the run-out tolerances and
balancing. To study this tolerance a calibration of the proximity sensor toward the
rotor material (Al 7075) is carried out, see appendix A1. It has been proposed by
Knapp et al. 2013 that the rotor should be made from aluminium to avoid electrical
run-out with the use of inductive displacement sensors. Two proximity sensors are
placed orthogonal to the rotor one in the top and one in the bottom as shown in Figure
5.3.

5.2 Misalignment of Rotor
99
misalignment of the rotor center is present. This residual misalignment is higher than
the allowed boundary, and a further distinction between top and bottom is clear as
the orbit is signiﬁcant larger in the bottom. Increasing the angular velocity moreover
causes an increase in the rotor orbit due to an unbalanced mass. This indicates that
the balancing of the rotor is not carried out properly. It occur that manufacturing
of the rotor has been associated with a malfunctioning misalignment. Consequently
the rotor center has an offset of ≈20µm, which result in a process disturbance when
coupling to the top foil is established. Hereby, the modal response of the top foil will
be contaminated by additional unbalanced response and residual misalignment, thus
the initial assumption of a dynamic free rotor is not valid. A short description of an
observer compensation can be found in section 4.4. It is based on the non-complete
mechanical model of the rotor as depicted in Figure 5.6. The ﬁgure shows the rotor
center Or with the misalignment and eccentricity toward the shaft center Os. While
εx and εy describes the eccentricity of the rotor toward the top foil. The unbalanced
mass is deﬁned according to this particular ﬁgure.
Another counteracting measure can be run-out compensating by means of subtracting
the rotor response from the top foil response. Unfortunately the spindle is not ﬁtted
with an encoder, which will allow the necessary correlation between angular velocity
and phase angle in case the run-out sensor is not aligned with the top foil’s sensor.
Moreover no sensor with a physical alignment occur in the experimental test module
why run-out compensating is not possible. This has though been accounted for in
the complete controllable gas bearing design. This is important in case control action
should avoid rejection toward the disturbance of the unbalanced and misaligned
response. Nonetheless in this section it is emphasize the need of applying digital
ﬁlters to the top foil response to obtain measurements that are not correlated with the
misaligned rotor.

5.3
Notch ﬁlter
In the previous section it is emphasized the need of a digital notch ﬁlter to attenuates
the response signals within a very narrow band of frequencies. This is supported by
the ﬁndings in appendix A9.1, where experiments are carried out with the top foil
coupled to the rotor at different angular velocities. Hereby the effect of the unbalanced
response and residual misalignment of the rotor center can be studied. In the appendix
it is seen that the top foil has a disturbance at frequencies equivalent to 1X synchronous
2X and 3X subsynchronous vibrations given by integers of the angular velocity. To
avoid this disturbance, notch ﬁlters has been designed with the MATLAB function
iirnotch(w0,bw), where w0 deﬁnes the location of the notch while bw the bandwidth
of the notch. The ﬁlters are applied to the output measurement.
By excinting the top foil with random Gaussian noise, while coupling to the rotor
with a angular velocity of 10000 rpm is maintained, the response of the top foil
with inclusion of noich ﬁlters can be obtained, see Figure 5.7. Similar approach has
been used in the literature of magnetic bearing systems, Lebron-Velilla 2003. It is
observed that the ﬁlter is fully capable of attenuate the 1X synchronous 2X and 3X
subsynchronous vibrations. However, it comes with the trade off by introducing a
phase shift around the notch frequency.

5.3 Notch ﬁlter
101
For the sake of curiosity the compliance of the notch ﬁlters toward a LQG controller
are studied. Irrespectively of a physical system that does not include the air ﬁlm forces,
the phase shift is expected to downgrade the performance of the closed-loop system
response. The additional states coming from the notch ﬁlters must be included in the
model to account for the phase shift and avoid the downgrade. Hereby the control
system will be able to handle the ﬁlter’s response around the notch frequency. The trade
off is however an increasing computational load. Coupling LQG control to the top foil
with the rotor and obtaining a frequency response function result in the performance
of the notch ﬁlters, see Figure 5.8.

An immense degrade of the top foil system is seen and the response is ampliﬁed in

deﬁnes a benchmark for a stable controller that is seen to amplify the top foil response. It
is thus emphasized the importance of including the air ﬁlm forces in the mathematical
model, to attenuate the electromechanical response. Moreover the initial studies
shows that use of notch ﬁlters in gas bearings technology is inappropriate and must
be avoided for future work. In stead run-out compensation or ﬁltering through an
advanced Kalman ﬁlter with an augmented state space model is preferably. This deﬁnes
the threshold and motivate future research.
5.4
Dynamic Dependency with Changing Angular
Velocity of the Rotor
With the experimental set up shown in Figure 5.1, one can obtain frequency
response functions for the electromechanical top foil coupled to the rotor. The sensor
and actuator location remain unchanged form previous experiments. Based on previous
ﬁndings, it is concluded that evaluation of the frequency response function H1 is more
accurate relative to the top foil’s dynamics. For the generality H1 is accurate for anti
resonance and H2 is preferable for resonance. Two descriptions can be made according
to Ewins 1984
• H1 assumes that the noise is in the excitation force. It is good for anti resonance
deﬁned when the amplitude of the vibration is small but the imposed excitation
force is large.
• H2 assumes that the noise arrives at the sensor signal. It is good for resonance
deﬁned when the amplitude of vibration is large but the excitation force is small.
In order to avoid faulty readings from the experimental FRF’s due to the unbalanced
mass and misaligned rotor, H1 is thus interpreted and conclusions are based here on.
The objective in this section is to study the dependency on the change in rotor
angular velocity ω. To obtain unique results the excitation signal of the piezoelectric
actuator is held constant in all experiments, such that only one parameter is changed.
The input signal is thus deﬁned with a mean value µ = 0 V and an amplitude ±3σ = 122
V, according to the excitation procedure with white Gaussian noise. Hereby one decrease
the electrical and geometrical non-linearities which can blur the results.
The angular velocity is varied in the range ω ∈[0 : 17000] rpm deﬁned by
12 different experiments with approximately same interval size. Measurements are
acquired for the input voltage, and the output displacement, obtained at steady state
conditions for the rotor. This mean that ω remain constant within the data acquisition
time performed for 800 seconds. Hereby, a good coherence is ensured between the
input and output. The results of the different frequency response functions with
corresponding phase angle are found in Figure 5.9. It is seen that the FRF function at
ω = 0 rpm is exactly the same as previous results which is expected as the response is
obtained in excess of any air ﬁlm forces.
