As ω is increased the air ﬁlm forces build in the gap size between the top foil and
the rotor, which forces the top foil to deﬂect away from the rotor. This is seen in
Figure 5.10, where the gap size between the sensor tip and the top foil decrease with
increasing ω. The top foil subjected to the air ﬁlm forces is thus extremely compliant,
and it undergoes a deﬂection which in somewhat proportional to the angular velocity
in the studied frequency range.
A distinction between the different response functions can be drawn relative to
equivalent damping and stiffness coefﬁcients of the air ﬁlm forces. It is not explicit
stated but rely only on visual inspection of ﬁgure 5.9. As seen, two dashed squares are
marked in the H1 response which provides the boundaries of the zoomed views found
in Figure 5.11.
The zoomed views illustrates the change in the top foil’s 1st and 3rd bending mode.
ω1 change ≈12.5 % by inspection of the natural frequency obtained at 90◦in the phase
plot 5.11(c). In continuation, the curves in the phase plot for ω1 reveal a decreasing
inclination around the natural frequency. This mean that the damping increases in
the system as ω increase, which are supported by the resonance peak in the H1 that
widens up, see ﬁgure 5.11(a). Moreover the magnitude of the response within the
bandwidth is seen to attenuate signiﬁcantly with increasing ω. This emphasize that
the equivalent stiffness of the air ﬁlm forces increases with increasing ω, which are
expected. As the laminar ﬂow between the top foil and the rotor is build, the pressure

5.5 Dynamic Dependence with Changing DC-Bias of Piezoelectric Actuation
105
It was found that the top foil were compliant and deﬂected proportional to the
angular velocity. It is now of interest to see how the effect of piezoelectric actuation
correlates with the air ﬁlm forces which are expressed as equivalent damping and
stiffness coefﬁcients. It is investigate if the piezoelectric actuator has a immense
endurance to withstand air ﬁlm forces in a ﬁnal application, enabling compliance in
the geometry toward the gap size between the rotor and the top foil.
5.5
Dynamic Dependence with Changing DC-Bias of
Piezoelectric Actuation
The dependency on a change in the DC-bias of the excitation signal to the piezoelec-
tric actuation is now established between the top foil and the rotor with a predetermined
angular velocity. The experimental set up is equivalent with the previous section. To
ensure a ﬁrm coupling between the air ﬁlm forces in the gap between the rotor and
the top foil, the angular velocity is determined to ω = 15.000 rpm = 250 Hz. This
gives a signiﬁcant level of equivalent damping and stiffness relative to the air ﬁlm
forces, which was discovered in the previous section. The changing parameter is the
mean value of the excitation signal to the piezoelectric actuator. It is deﬁned in the
range µ ∈[−3001300] V with different levels. The amplitude of the signal is equal to
±3σ = 122 V such that a fair comparison can be made relative to the previous section.
Nine different experiments are carried out at duration time of 800 seconds. The
results are shown in Figure 5.12. Please note that the response is plotted in linear scale
instead of logarithmic. It is not necessary to a provide zoom view of the response, as the
appearance of the piezoelectric effect is clear. The largest amplitude of vibration occur
around the 3rd bending mode. This is eventually not the true result of the physical
response as the coherence around the 1st mode is zero. Inspection of the change
in dynamical behaviour is thus based on ω3 ≈126 Hz where the coherence is high.
Hereby the correlation with a change in the gap size can be established.
By increasing the DC-bias of the electrical input signal the top foil deﬂects closer to
the rotor by decreasing the bearing gap size. This is seen in Figure 5.13, indicating that
the gap size between the top foil and the sensor tip increase with increasing voltage.
The direct result of a stronger coupling. It is evident that the piezoelectric actuator
is strong enough to interact with the air ﬁlm. The rate of the coupling can be varied,
which will change the equivalent damping and stiffness coefﬁcients.
By increasing the input voltage the system properties by means of the equivalent
damping and stiffness coefﬁcients increase due to the build up of a greater pressure.
The concision is similar as when the increase in angular velocity caused an increase in
the damping of the system. However, in this section it is based on electrical actuation
that changes the properties of the system’s response. It is discovered that ω3 change
24% from 126 Hz, to 166 Hz. Moreover signiﬁcant damping is introduced which
is supported by the increased inclination of the lines in the phase plot around 90 ◦
(resonance).
Recall that the change in DC-bias of the excitation signal of the top foil not subjected
to coupling with the rotor, caused a shift in the natural frequency, see section 3.1. Here
no change in damping was observed. The change in natural frequency in this section
==PAGE:122==
106
5 Coupling of Rotor System
is consequently expected due to two reasons. The ﬁrst one being the coupling with the
air ﬁlm forces, which change the damping and natural frequency and the latter the
electrical and geometrical non-linearities that caused a shift in the natural frequency.
This is why a signiﬁcant higher change of ω3 with respect to µ occur relative to the
results in section 3.1.

Another dependency occur with the amplitude of the system response relative to
µ. While the lowest amplitude of vibration in general was obtained for µ = −302 V
(weak coupling to the rotor), a maximum amplitude of vibration occur when µ = 504
V. This is seen in Figure 5.14. The ﬁgure contains the three dimensional dependency
between the frequency response functions and µ. Although µ = 1304 V provides the
highest level of damping it does not result in the lowest level of amplitude. Here, it
must be emphasized that the closer the top foil approach the rotor, the more likely it is
that the top foil will pick up the unbalanced response from the misaligned rotor center.
The system response may consequently be impaired by the means of the stronger
coupling. As to what criteria are optimal, in terms of the piezoelectric actuation is not
possible to conclude based on the ﬁndings and results in this thesis. However, it is
important to remember that the study in this thesis is created in the excess of bump
foils and a bearing house encapsulating the top foils. The future study is therefore
strongly motivated to ﬁnalize the research and development of controllable gas bearing
technology.

Chapter 6
Conclusions and Future Aspects
Integration of smart materials, more speciﬁcally piezoelectric ceramics, in gas
bearing technology is investigated in order to allow active control and increase damping
in rotor systems. This is established through a multiphysics approach where a single
top foil coupling to piezoelectric actuation has been modelled. The project is a partial
contribution to the ongoing research and development of controllable gas bearings. It
deﬁnes the boundaries of the project applied to optimal control design. Throughout
the thesis the mathematical model presented in the appended publication Nielsen
et al. 2015 has been dealt with. Predictions of the model are compared and validated
against experimental results obtained with the designed test modules during the master
project. This aim at linking piezoelectric actuation through a ﬁnite element model of a
curvilinear shell structure, was successfully established in the bandwidth from 0-500
Hz.
In order to carry out the experimental tests a comprehensive rebuild of the electrical
instrumentation has been performed. This reduced the possibility of faulty noise in
the test facilities toward sensors and actuators, and improved the layout of the test
area. Moreover several new initiatives have been made to upgrade the mechanical
performance of the ﬁnal rotor test facility. The spindle has been ﬁtted with a Ø85
rotor found in appendix D1. Moreover a safety shield in appendix D2 was installed to
eliminate hazardous incident of the operator. Finally several new test modules have
been designed and manufactured for the experiments in this thesis, which are found
in appendix D3-D5.
6.1
Main Conclusion
The large scale ﬁnite element model was modiﬁed to a reduced complex separated
state space representation. Using Modal Order Reduction techniques the system
reduced from n × n to 2N × 2N, where N indicates the number of modes included in
the reduced model. Hereby feasible control action with the proposed LQG regulator
can be enabled. Experimental solid mechanics successfully provided knowledge of
the system’s natural frequencies, mode shapes, modal damping and forced response
with corresponding phase angle. It was found that model parameters ware related
to uncertainties caused by geometrical and electrical non-linearities. A tuning of
geometrical factors and piezoelectric constants was necessary to obtain accurate results.
==PAGE:126==
110
6 Conclusions and Future Aspects
This is obtained by establishing a linearized operating point of the model based on
empirical results. Although, some robustness is inherited and the model can deviate
slightly, a tuning is crucial to acquire the perfect interrelation between a robust LQG
regulator and the proposed model. Based on the subjected tuning the control system
was able to reduce modal response by 80% of the open loop response. Moreover, modal
damping rations of the numerical model was increased ≈8-50 times. Additionally, two
different actuations principles was established. One focussing on reducing the modal
response, and one with the further inclusion of dynamic positioning of the top foil.
Hereby the curvature of the top foil geometry can be controlled, which substantiate
that the bearing gap in a complete set up can be changed via smart materials. Optimal
design of feedback control gains is thus obtained. This was although not possible to
obtain with coupling to the rotor as, the model deviated.
Robustness in optimal control system is deﬁned with a phase margin less than 60 ◦.
Eventually, non-linear effects were prone to cause instability of the top foil system as
natural frequencies and corresponding phase angle shifted with changing properties of
the control signal. The linear model can not account for this non-linear behaviour and
if the LQG controller is designed too aggressively the system become prone to instability.
This limits the model to work around the equilibrium, which must be established with
respect to the amplitude on the control signal for an optimal actuator utilization. In
this regard it is strongly recommended to study the dependency of the piezoelectric
constants and geometrical relations through basic research keen to accomplish a more
well functioning control system. This way a mathematical compensation can be inher-
ited in the model which should be able to account for a change of 20 % in the natural
frequencies which was found possible.
Although it is believed that the change in natural frequencies is less likely in a con-
trollable gas bearing relative to geometric non-linearities it must be emphasized the
importance of measures that increase the robustness of the controller. This conclusion
is based on the geometrical constraints that are physically provided by the gap size
between the rotor and the top foil in a complete bearing.
Another aspect of the performance of the control system was spillover effects. The
piezoelectric actuator was found mainly to control the bending modes of the top foil,
while the sensor was located to provide system info with the inclusion of torsional
modes. In this case, observer and control-spillover caused an ampliﬁcation in the
system around the torsional modes relative to open-loop response. The control system
suffered from model discrepancy which contributed to this ampliﬁcation. This could
fortunately be avoided by integration of the output sensor located on nodal lines with
reduces the modal amplitude. The response of torsional modes can be exterminated
from the measurement which is used as feedback in the system. Hereby observer
spillover is signiﬁcant reduced. An effective evaluation on sensor as well as actuator
location is found according to quantitative measures of controllability and observability.
In this study it was found that one sensor is sufﬁcient to make the systems modal
response fully observable. However as just emphasized only a subset of the modes
are of interest why it must be placed to observe the reduced subset of modes that are
governing in the system.
It is encouraged to expand the mathematical model to account for the air ﬁlm
forces when coupling to the rotor is established. The rate of the coupling was found to
correlating with dynamic change of the equivalent stiffness and damping coefﬁcients
==PAGE:127==
6.2 Future Aspects
111
relative to the physical system. This correlation has not yet been assessed numerically,
which is why the optimal control model designed for the single top foil was not able
to enhance the modal response and provide active damping on the structure coupled
to the rotor. Irrespectively on this assessment, uncertainties are related to the the
linearized equivalent damping and stiffness coefﬁcients of the air ﬁlm forces. This poses
the need of a controller which is robust toward model uncertainties. This approach can
be obtained either by H∞or µ synthesis. Hereby the beneﬁt of a uniform performance
capable of handling system variations will be obtained.
Finally serious aspects of unwanted dynamics are quantiﬁed with the rotor test facil-
ities. The test rig was initially designed under the assumption of no equivalent stiffness.
This is unfortunately not the case. Unbalanced response and residual misalignment of
the rotor center cause excitation of the top foil equivalent to 1X synchronous 2X and
3X subsynchronous vibrations with frequencies equivalent to integers of the angular
velocity. Notch ﬁlters ware applied to compensate for this response which should pre-
vent a control system from rejecting unbalanced and misaligned rotor center response.
The designed ﬁlters was able to remove the frequencies from the output. However
it introduced a phase shift around the 1X, 2X and 3X frequencies which causes an
ampliﬁcation in the closed loop response compared to the open-loop response. The
implementation is thus concluded to be unsuccessful, and if validation against experi-
ments are to be obtained a solution must be found. Consequently an integration of
intelligent observer design built into the Kalman ﬁlter or a run-out compensation is
suggested and believed to be a more unique and compatible solution to the problem.
Alternatively a refabrication of the rotor must be performed.
The angular velocity dependence on the top foil’s natural frequencies has also been
revealed. It is not a innovative interpretation, and has previously been elaborately
explained in the literature. Nonetheless it was found that increasing angular velocity
caused an increase in air ﬁlm forces. The result is an increased equivalent stiffness and
damping imposed on the top foils original dynamics. Hereby the natural frequencies
also increased. The greatest impact was found on the 1st and 3rd natural frequencies
(bending modes) of the top foil. Moreover, the proposed change in system properties
was successfully established by changing the gap size between the rotor and the top
foil by means of piezoelectric actuation.
A preliminary curvature dependency describing a working principle toward a
complete gas bearing is obtained. By increasing the actuation voltage top foil curvature
decreased and the coupling becomes stronger. The result is an increase of air ﬁlm
forces. Vice versa an increase of the curvature reduced the coupling, by lowering the
air ﬁlm forces. Parallels to this ﬁnding may be transferred to a controllable gas bearing
operating at a speciﬁc angular velocity, whereby attenuation of rotor response can be
individually adjusted and optimized. This is an incredible optimization opportunity
which deﬁnes the boundaries of state of the art gas bearing technology.
6.2
Future Aspects
It looks promising for the development of controllable gas bearings and there
exists evidence that piezoelectric actuators can induce signiﬁcant attenuation in the
response of a rotor system. It is expected that piezoelectric actuators are capable of
==PAGE:128==
112
6 Conclusions and Future Aspects
imposing considerably damping compared to passive gas bearings. The trade off is a
more complex operation, and increasing costs associated with sensors, actuators and
ampliﬁers. The beneﬁt is increased stability toward an increasing unbalanced mass
arise from wear and fatigue loading. The future development procedure is suggested
by ﬁnalizing the numerical development of the mathematical model accounting for
the air ﬁlm forces. This is to be validated against experimental results of a single top
foil, as previous dealt with in Chapter 5. The aim is to ﬁnalize with a successfully
implementation of a control system in a complete controllable gas bearing, which also
accounts for the bump model. An overview of the test module for the complete active
gas bearing is presented in Figure 6.1.

Figure 6.1: Schematic of a prototype of the controllable gas bearing. Note the inclusion
of electromagnetic shakers, force transducers, permanent magnets and ferromagnetic

==PAGE:129==
6.2 Future Aspects
113
It is intended that the rotor is rigid and ﬁxed at the center position, while the bearing
is able to translate in the two dimensional space. The rotor test rig is based on a vertical
concept which eliminates gravitational forces. The module has been manufactured and
prepared for experimental test when the ﬁnal mathematical model is fully developed.
Various aspects of run-out compensation are build into the module by inclusion of
multiple set of proximity sensors installed at the same angle, one measuring the top
foil and one measuring the rotor’s lateral vibration. To position the rotor with crucial
eccentricity in the bearing (as no static load occur due to the gravity), two permanent
magnets are installed which pulls toward discs made from ferromagnetic material.
Hereby, static load can be imposed on the bearing which are being monitored by force
transducers denoted fm1(ε1) and fm2(ε2). Here, ε characterizes the air gap between the
magnet and the ferromagnetic disc. Finally, are two electromagnetic shakers installed
with a 90◦phase shift to excite the bearing in two directions, these are monitored by
equivalent force transducers denoted fs1(t) and fs2(t).
The controllable gas bearing is a multiple input multiple output system (MIMO
system), where three proximity sensors provide the output response y of the top foils.
It is controlled by three independent piezoelectric actuators which is characterized by
the input control signal vector u. A look-up table with non-linear stiffness and damping
coefﬁcients correlating with angular velocity ω of the rotor must be numerically
determined. Subsequently it should be implemented in the control system to account
for the correct change in the system’s natural frequencies with changing ω. This
correlation must be established according to the eccentricity and the mechanical model
presented in Figure 6.3, which is left for future work.
In continuation, the sensor location must be changed to a symmetric position
excluding torsional modes from the response. This will reduce observer spillover
effects, which eliminate spillover instability. Recall that an anti aliasing ﬁlter is good
experimental practice, as it will low pass ﬁlter the sensor signal. This is strongly
recommended to be added to the feedback sensor in the ﬁnal application hereby
avoiding residual dynamics and subsynchronous vibration to be feed into the system.
Moreover, serious modiﬁcations must be made to the main rotor test rig. It was found at
very high angular velocity that the spindle creates a spray of oil mist in the surrounding
environment due to the injected lubrication. To prevent this hazardous incident causing
serious danger to the operator’s health care, it is strongly recommended to install an
industrial extractor on top of the safety shield. The ﬁnal rotor test facility is depicted
in 6.2. In addition precaution is emphasized toward the lubricating oil that suffer from
a potential risk of dripping down in the gas bearing. If this is not avoided, a resulting
multiphase ﬂow will be established in the intended single phase ﬂow gas bearing.

be properly validated. It will also be beneﬁcial to the installed electromagnetic shakers.
Refabrication with increased accuracy is approved by the supplier which is launched.

A layered shell containing patches of

The following journal paper is expected to be published in Intelligent Material
Systems and Structures, May 2016. The paper contains the mathematical modelling
and creates the foundation for this thesis.
==PAGE:138==
Original Article
Journal of Intelligent Material Systems
and Structures
1–19
! The Author(s) 2016
Reprints and permissions:
sagepub.co.uk/journalsPermissions.nav
DOI: 10.1177/1045389X16642537
jim.sagepub.com
A layered shell containing patches of
piezoelectric fibers and interdigitated
electrodes: Finite element modeling
and experimental validation
Bo B Nielsen, Martin S Nielsen and Ilmar F Santos
Abstract
The work gives a theoretical and experimental contribution to the problem of smart materials connected to double
curved flexible shells. In the theoretical part the finite element modeling of a double curved flexible shell with a piezo-
electric fiber patch with interdigitated electrodes (IDEs) is presented. The developed element is based on a purely
mechanical eight-node isoparametric layered element for a double curved shell, utilizing first-order shear deformation
theory. The electromechanical coupling of piezoelectric material is added to all elements, but can also be excluded by
setting the piezoelectric material properties to zero. The electrical field applied via the IDEs is aligned with the piezo-
electric fibers, and hence the direct d33 piezoelectric constant is utilized for the electromechanical coupling. The dynamic
performance of a shell with a microfiber composite (MFC) patch is investigated using frequency response functions
(FRFs) obtained via external impact test as well as internal random signal excitation using the MCF patch as an actuator.
The experiments are used to validate the numerical results. Good agreement between theory and experiments is
obtained in a large frequency range. Discrepancies and insights into optimal resembling frequency range and non-linear
behavior are discussed.
Keywords
Double curved shell, experimental validation, finite element method, interdigitated electrodes, microfiber composite,
active fiber composite, piezoelectric fiber composite, electromechanical system
Introduction
The past few decades have seen an intense development
and integration of smart materials into a variety of flex-
ible structures, with the goal of sensing, monitoring and
controlling the static and dynamic behavior of such
flexible structures. Piezoelectric material adds a new
dimension into the design of smart structures, since it
allows for adaptation and optimization of their static
and dynamic properties via electronics and software,
and with the development of piezoelectric patches these
properties are easily added to a flexible structure.
Sensing and actuating via piezoelectric structures
have been a topic of research for many years, dating
back to at least the 1950s; see for example Thurston
(1953). Over the years different types of sensors and
actuators have been developed, but the focus of this
article is on piezoelectric patches. Many of these
patches have been developed by NASA, and Bryant
(2007) gives a timeline for the development of different
types of piezoelectric patches, from the thin layer
unimorph driver (THUNDER) in the beginning of the
1990s to the microfiber composite (MFC) in 1996, and
so on. These two types of piezoelectric patches are
shown
in
Figure
1.
As
seen
in
the
figure
the
THUNDER patch consists of a thin piezoelectric sheet
covered by electrodes on top and bottom, while the
MFC consists of rectangular piezoelectric fibers iso-
lated from each other and covered by interdigitated
electrodes (IDEs). The idea for the piezoelectric fiber
and IDEs originates from the active fiber composite
(AFC) presented by Hagood et al. (1993) and Hagood
and Bent (1993). An overview of the MFC technology
and applications has been given by Williams et al.
DTU Mechanical Engineering, Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Corresponding author:
Ilmar F Santos, Technical University of Denmark, Department of
Mechanical Engineering, Niels Koppels Alle 404, 2900, Kongens Lyngby,
Denmark.
Email: ifs@mek.dtu.dk
122
Publication P1
==PAGE:139==
(2002) and Scho¨ necker et al. (2006). The top and bot-
tom electrode configurations exist for the MFC,
although this version cannot match the THUNDER
patch for actuation as shown by Bent et al. (1995). This
type of electrode for the MFC will not be discussed fur-
ther, and hence all references to MFCs or piezoelectric
fiber patches assume IDEs are used.
The literature within the topic of modeling piezo-
electric patches can be divided into three categories: (1)
analytical and finite element method (FEM) models of
beams, plates and shells of or with the THUNDER
patch, (2) analytical material models validated by full
three-dimensional (3D) FEM models of a so-called unit
cell or representative volume, and (3) analytical and
FEM models of beam-like structures utilizing MFC
patches in the design.
Among the first models of piezoelectric patches are
the dynamic equations presented by Tzou and Gadre
(1989) and the solid FEM model by Tzou and Tseng
(1991), which include the piezoelectric material in the
layers of a shell. These models utilize the extension
mode or d31 piezoelectric constant, where the electrical
field is applied to the shell normal direction, the same
direction as the polarization, but with the mechanical
reaction acting in the plane of the shell. The subsequent
FEM models presented in the literature are layered-
beam-, plate- or shell-based, utilizing different shear
deformation theory. Suleman and Venkayya (1995)
presented a four-node Mindlin element for plates with
the
possibility
of
including
multiple
piezoelectric
patches in the same layer, while Samanta et al. (1996)
presented a FEM model based on high-order deforma-
tion theory. Tzou and Ye (1996) utilized zigzag theory
with individual shear and electrical potential in each
layer for a FEM model with six by two node triangular
elements. A mixed formulation FEM model was pre-
sented by Saravanos (1997) combining first-order shear
deformation theory through the entire shell thickness
with a piecewise linear electrical potential in the layers.
Piefort and Henrioulle (2000) present a FEM model
based on Kirchhoff plate theory. A nine-node shell ele-
ment was presented by Balamurugan and Narayanan
(2001) based on first-order shear deformation theory.
Gabbert et al. (2002) described three different ways of
including the piezoelectric effect in an eight-node semi-
loof shell element ranging from an external force to the
inclusion of the electrical potential at each node.
Varelis and Saravanos (2006) introduced a double
curved layered shell finite element, which can handle
large deformations and is capable of modeling large
curved shells. A five-layer shallow shell finite element
was presented by Boudaoud et al. (2008), based on a
classical
three-node
flat
triangular
element,
while
Balamurugan and Narayanan (2009) utilize respectively
higher-order shear deformation theory on an eight-
node shell element. Vidal et al. (2011) presented an
eight-node shell element based on the Reissner–Mindlin
plate model with an additional quadratic thickness
stretching displacement term. The four-node shallow
element presented by Yasin and Kapuria (2013) utilizes
zigzag theory to describe the shear deformation.
In the same period FEM models have been pub-
lished for the THUNDER patch taking into account
the
thermal
effects
on
the
piezoelectric
material.
Chattopadhyay et al. (1998) presented an analytical
plate model including the thermal coupling to the elec-
tromechanical system. A FEM model of this was pre-
sented by Bansal and Ramaswamy (2002) in a four-
node plate element and in a nine-node shell element
version by Narayanan and Balamurugan (2003).
The piezoelectric layers in all the models presented
above are of the THUNDER type and utilize the exten-
sion mode, as opposed to the shear mode or d15 piezo-
electric constant, where the electrical field is applied in
the shell normal direction, but the polarization is in-
plane with the shell. The mechanical reaction acting on
the electrical field is in-plane shear. Benjeddou et al.
(1997, 1999) and Trindade et al. (2001) presented a
FEM model capable of including both extension and
shear mode for a sandwich beam. This was later
expanded by Kapuria and Hagedorn (2007) to include
multiple layers, and the internal electric mechanical
effects.
Figure 1. Two types of piezoelectric patches. The THUNDER
and the MFC P1. (1) Isolation layer, (2) piezoelectric sheet and
(3) electrodes.
2
Journal of Intelligent Material Systems and Structures
Publication P1
123
==PAGE:140==
In the second category of topics the main focus is
homogenization of the material properties of an exten-
sion mode piezoelectric fiber patch with IDEs due to
the complex structure and electrical field from the elec-
trodes. The first model presented by Bent and Hagood
(1997) is known as the uniform field method. The
method is developed for the AFC with IDEs, but due
to the close resemblance between the MFC and the
AFC, the method may also be used for homogenization
of the MFC material properties. Park and Kim (2005)
presented an alternative method to calculate the homo-
genized material properties. Results were presented for
single crystal MFC, standard MFC and AFC, showing
a better performance for the MFC. This can be attrib-
uted to the better contact between the MFC and IDEs
due to the squared fiber as opposed to the round fiber
used in AFC. Deraemaeker et al. (2009) used the mix-
ing rule for calculating the average material properties,
and validated the results via a 3D finite element model
of the unit cell. Biscani et al. (2011) presented the
asymptotic expansion homogenization for calculating
the material properties of the MFC with top and bot-
tom surface electrodes. The characterization of the
material properties in a shear mode MFC with top and
bottom electrodes has been presented by Trindade and
Benjeddou (2011), which shows the packaging lowers
the effective stiffness and inverse piezoelectric coupling
effect of the shear mode MFC.
The temperature dependency for the homogenized
material properties of the extension mode MFC is pre-
sented by Prasath and Arockiarajan (2015) as a func-
tion of the bonding layer thickness between the
piezoelectric fiber and the IDEs, since the bonding
layer obstructs the electrical field produced by the
IDEs. Nasser et al. (2008, 2011) focus on the electrical
field between two electrodes, making it possible to cre-
ate a correction for the dead zones in the electrical
field. This gives an insight into the distribution of the
electrical field associated with the IDEs used for piezo-
electric fiber patches.
Within the third topic Nguyen and Kornmann
(2006) used a 3D FEM model of a beam to investigate
the dynamic performance of the three collocated pairs
of piezoelectric patches, that is, THUNDER, AFC and
MFC, and compared results with experimental data.
An analytical investigation of a MFC patch mounted
on a beam was performed by Bilgen et al. (2010), which
agrees
with
experimentally
obtained
results.
Brockmann and Lammering (2006) developed a hollow
beam FEM model for modeling a helicopter rotor
blade. An air foil with 18 MFC patches has been mod-
eled by Guennam and Luccioni (2006, 2009) using two
different approaches. In the first one, the box beam of
the air foil is modeled by FEM in a piece of commercial
software, while in the second approach the box beam is
modeled by a four-node piezoelectric shell element. The
two models show very similar results. The latter model,
which is the model most comparable to the one pre-
sented in this article, has been expanded by Guennam
and Luccioni (2015) to take the non-linearity of the
piezoelectric coupling into account.
In this framework the main original contribution of
this work relies on the development and experimental
validation of a linear electromechanical finite element
for double curved layered shells with the capability of
including one or more piezoelectric fiber patches by
adding one single additional degree of freedom per elec-
trode pair, that is, the electrical potential difference over
the IDEs while taking the dead zones due to the IDEs
into account. The dynamic shell element model is based
on a static eight-node serendipity isoparametric layered
double curved finite element developed by Panda and
Natarajan (1981). Capabilities and limitations of the
new electromechanical finite element are shown by
comparison to experiment results performed with a sin-
gle curved shell with one MFC patch. The neutral bend-
ing axis in the electromechanical shell is not located at
the mid-plane of the shell due to the asymmetric layup.
It is important to highlight that, in most of the contri-
butions found in the literature, patches are symmetri-
cally distributed on both sides of the shell, leading to no
extension–bending coupling. In this work such a sym-
metric stacking of layers is not imposed due the practi-
cal application in mind.
Electromechanical model
A piezoelectric MFC patch consists of aligned rectangu-
lar piezoelectric fibers isolated from each other and with
IDEs on the top and bottom surfaces. The IDE creates
an electrical field due to the electrical potential difference
between the electrode fingers in the plane of the patch
and perpendicular to the electrodes, that is, in the same
direction as the fibers and the wanted action. This results
in an extension mode and utilizes the direct piezoelectric
constant d33, which has a larger magnitude than the d31
utilized in the THUNDER piezoelectric patches, and
hence a bigger effect is gained in the fiber direction (Bent
et al., 1995). In Figure 2(a) a sketch of the MFC is seen,
while in Figure 2(b) a single piezoelectric fiber
2
ð Þ is
shown with its isolation on its sides 1
ð Þ and one pair of
IDEs 3
ð Þ on its top and bottom. The enclosed square
marked with black lines is the unit cell of the MFC.
The unit cell is assumed to be governed by two phys-
ical domains, one mechanical and one electrical, which
are coupled by the piezoelectric effect. Figure 3 shows
the MFC unit cell (a) and the two domains (b): on the
left-hand side the mechanical domain is described by
three direct and three shear strain variables, and on the
right-hand side the electrical domain is represented by
three direct electrical field directions.
The piezoelectric constitutive equation (1) is given in
for example Tiersten (1969), where S½ $ is the structural
Nielsen et al.
3
124
Publication P1
==PAGE:141==
compliance, d½ $ is direct piezoelectric constant and e½ $ is
the electric permittivity. The specific configuration of the
piezoelectric fiber patch, for example fiber fraction ratio
and so on, affects the material properties in the constitu-
tive equations, which may be calculated by for example
the uniform field model mentioned in the introduction.
The constitutive equation may also be used for non-
piezoelectric materials, since a non-piezoelectric material
will have zero piezoelectric constant and dielectric per-
mittivity, and hence equation (1a)

The physics behind the constitutive equations can be
explained by the crystal structure of a piezoelectric
material. The crystal lattice of a typical piezoelectric
material like lead zirconate titanate (LZT) is tetragonal
(non-cubic), which below the Curie temperature results
in a spontaneous polarization of the ions within the
material due to their internal position in the lattice.
The relative displacement from the symmetric results in
a dipole moment; see Figure 4. The dipole moments
within the material structure, or even within a material
crystal, are not necessarily aligned upon initial manu-
facturing. An alignment of the dipoles is ensured by
poling the material with a strong electric field, which
ensures alignment of the piezoelectric properties. A
thorough explanation is given by Damjanovic (1999).
Mathematical model
A layered shell containing one or more layers of piezo-
electric fiber patches must be regarded as an electrome-
chanical system in order to take the piezoelectric
coupling effects into account. In the derivation of a
mathematical model, the Lagrange equation

freedom used in the FEM formulation. The Lagrangian
for an electromechanical system is given by the kinetic
energy minus the potential energy

The electrical field created by the IDE
So far, the derivation has been kept general, and hence
no restrictions have been introduced for the shape or
geometry of the electromechanical system. In order to
include the external electric field in the piezoelectric
fiber due to the electrodes (see Figure 2(a)) the electric
field variable
E

electrical field created by an electrical potential differ-
ence between the electrodes is added via superposition
to the existing electrical field within the fibers; hence
the total electrical field is given by

IDEs have dead zones between two equipotential

red in Figure 5. These dead zones introduce a challenge
for selecting the correct distance between two electrode
pairs in the in-plane direction needed for calculating
the electric field due to the direction of the electrical
field as seen in the same figure (Figure 5). Nasser et al.
(2008) give an analytical expression for the effective
electrical field produced by the electric potential differ-
ence between the IDEs taking the dead zones into
account. The correction factor is

There are two important things to note regarding
the electrical field of the IDE. First note: the electrical
field created by the IDE has a different direction on
each side of an electrode finger as illustrated by the
arrows on the electrical field lines seen in Figure 5, but
since the manufacturer uses the IDE for polarization of
the piezoelectric fibers the polarization also has a dif-
ferent direction on each side of an electrode finger, and
hence the electrical field is always aligned with the
polarization. Second note: the third spatial direction of
a piezoelectric material is conventionally aligned with

The eight-node double curved isoparametric element
As the base for the new dynamic electromechanical ele-
ment the static eight-node double curved layered iso-
parametric serendipity element presented by Panda and
Natarajan (1981) is chosen. A schematic can be seen in
the center of Figure 7(a). The physical double curved
layered shell is prescribed by a 2D four-sided shell with
nodes in each corner and on each side. This eight-node
shell element follows the geometrical mid-plane of the
physical shell, with the spatial location of the physical
shell given by

The nodal placement is given in the global coordi-
nate system, while the thickness is given using the third
coordinate direction of the local nodal coordinate sys-
tem (see Figure 7(a)). The transformation matrices from
global to local coordinates are given in the appendix.
In the derivation of the governing equation via the
Lagrange equation the discretization via separation of
variables is done with unspecified shape functions and
nodal degrees of freedom; see equation (9). For the
eight-node element the shape functions are given in iso-
parametric coordinates and listed in the appendix along
with the derivative matrices in the appendix. The nodal
displacement

For multi-layers
For a shell structure with multiple layers the volume
integration in the thickness direction must be done
individually for each layer as proposed by Panda and
Natarajan (1981). This procedure utilizes that the mate-
rial properties are well known at layer level, making the
procedure
straightforward.

each piezoelectric layer is electrically isolated from the
others and that they have their own electrodes.
Therefore they also have their own degree of freedom
with respect to the electrical potential difference. The
assumption is valid for MFC due to the isolation on
the top and bottom (see Figure 1(b)).

Guyan reduction of internal electrical potential
degrees of freedom
The internal electrical field can be generated by a deflec-
tion of the shell or an electrical potential difference over
the IDEs, and since the actual magnitude of this field is
of no interest the degrees of freedom associated with
this can be eliminated via a Guyan reduction presented
by Guyan (1965) and originally in a piezoelectric con-
text by Allik and Hughes (1970). This will result in a
lowering of the total number of variables leading to a
reduction of the computational effort needed, but still
maintain the effects of the internal electrical field due to
the modification of the remaining stiffness matrices and
their corresponding external force vectors. From equa-
tion (17) electrical potential related to the internal elec-
trical field can be isolated. For simplicity the following
equation shows the reduction for one element in a layer

Boundary conditions
The developed element has two types of degrees of free-
dom, that is, displacement
ue
f
g and electrical potential
difference
Def
f
g. The variable related to the electrical
system is taken as the difference between the two elec-
trodes. This is a relative degree of freedom correspond-
ing to one of the electrodes being grounded, and hence
no boundary condition is needed.
The mechanical displacement requires boundary
conditions,
since
rigid
body
motion
is
possible.
Fixation of a mechanical degree of freedom can be
done by setting the corresponding diagonal value in the
mass
u%u ^M
%
&
and stiffness matrices
u%u ^K
%
&
to one and
the associated column and row values as well as the
force vector
u%uF
f
g row to zero. For additional expla-
nation see for example Cook et al. (2002).
Experimental facility
The test facility shown in Figure 9(a) consists of the sin-
gle curved shell mounted in a clamping mechanism. A
sensor bracket gives the possibility of positioning an
eddy current displacement sensor at different locations
in the axial and circumferential directions to measure
the normal deflections of the shell. The smart material
MFC M5628-P1 patch is glued onto the outer curvature
of the shell to avoid any relative movement between the
two layers at the adjacent surfaces. The piezoelectric
fibers in the MFC are aligned in the circumferential
direction and the patch is placed symmetrically along
the centerline in the axial direction and 25.68 from the
clamped end, as illustrated in Figure 9(b). The remain-
ing geometrical constants and material properties
adopted for the electromechanical model are given in
Table 1. Two radial dimensions are given for the shell,
with and without the MFC patch. The originally flat
MFC patch is bent to follow the shell curvature during
montage. The residual forces from the MFC deforma-
tion affect the shell by straightening it, and hence the
shell curvature after montage is 43.0 mm compared to
the original 42.5 mm, as listed in Table 1. It is assumed
the residual stress in the piezoelectric fibers will not
affect the performance of the MFC, in other words, the
MFC will still perform linearly. For all the following
experiments a dSpace 1103 controller board has been
used as the data acquisition unit, while Matlab has been
used for the signal processing.
Numerical and experimental results
Impact test
Experimental modal analysis is initially performed for
three different cases via a hammer impact with a B & K
8200 force transducer and Pulsotronic KJ4-M12MN50-
ANU eddy current displacement sensor. The sampling
frequency is 2 kHz and the three cases are (1) the shell
without the MFC patch; (2) the shell with the MFC
patch and IDE open circuit configuration; and (3) the
shell with the MFC and IDE short-circuited. Figure 10

illustrates a selected frequency response function (FRF)
for the second case with amplitude unit mm/N. The
impact force is applied close to the clamped extremity
near the shell centerline to avoid loading problems such
as overloading and double hit. The displacement sensor
records the normal deflection of the shell and is placed
as seen in Figure 9(a) at the edge of the shell in the axial
direction. Similar FRFs have been obtained for cases 1
and 3 as well but are not shown here.
The large flexibility of the shell imposes a limitation
on the magnitude of the impact force. This is to ensure
the assumption of small vibration amplitudes is not to
violate and to keep the displacements within the range
of the eddy current displacement sensor. The limitation
for the impact force magnitude is reflected in a coher-
ence around 0.9, as shown in Figure 10. This is the
highest obtainable coherence under the given experi-
mental conditions.

The parameters a and b given in Table 3 are calcu-
lated aided by the least squares method, using the
experimental damping factors presented in Tables 4
and 5 with the corresponding experimental natural fre-
quencies shown in Tables 2 and 6.
The natural frequencies depend more on geometry
changes, that is, the curvature radius of the shell, due
to the montage of the MFC patch than on the circuit
configuration of the IDEs. The curvature radius signifi-
cantly affects all natural frequencies, while the shell
thickness has a more pronounced effect on the higher
frequencies.

shell is excited using either random signals (case 4a) or

experimental and theoretical FRFs obtained for case
4a. The unit of the amplitude is mm/V. The theoretical
FRF is calculated using the same excitation amplitude
applied to the IDEs as that in the experiments.
Good agreement between theoretical and experimen-
tal FRF amplitudes is shown for the first five natural
frequencies as seen in Table 6. Discrepancies are smaller
than 4% for these natural frequencies and the ampli-
tudes of the theoretical FRF results closely resemble the
experimentally obtained ones up to approximately 450
Hz as seen in Figure 11. The phase of the theoretical
FRF follows the behavior of the experimental one well,
but some deviations are seen, especially between the
third (69 Hz) and the sixth (471 Hz) natural frequencies.
This is likely due to the simplifying assumption of
Rayleigh structural damping and the a and b para-
meters used, which combined are not able to describe
the more complex behavior of energy dissipation in the
whole frequency range. The coherence function very
close to 1 clearly shows the good quality of the mea-
surement data.

tions, the software ME’scope is used to calculate the
mode shapes. The measured mode shapes are a projec-
tion of deflections onto a vertical plane parallel to the

Comparing Figure 12(a) and (c), the reader can eas-
ily note the similarities between the theoretical and
experimental first bending mode shapes. Theoretical as
well as experimental mode shapes are symmetrical in
relation to the middle plane in the axial direction.
Likewise, the same conclusion can be drawn for the
third, fourth, fifth and sixth mode shapes, shown in
Figure 12(e) to (l). The second bending mode shape can
be clearly identified based on the first nodal line in the
axial direction in Figure 12(e) and (g), while the third
bending mode shape seen in Figure 12(i) and (k) has
two nodal lines in the axial direction. The first and the
second bending–twisting mode shapes are depicted in
Figure 12(f) and (h) and Figure 12(j) and (l), respec-
tively. One nodal line in the circumferential direction is
seen in both cases. For the first bending–twisting mode
shape only one single nodal line is detected in the axial
direction while in the second two nodal lines are
observed.
Poor results in terms of mode shapes can only be
observed for the second one. A relative large difference
is detected when the theoretical (straight) and the
experimental (bent) nodal lines in the circumferential
direction are compared in Figure 12(b) and (d). The
second mode is pure twisting, and since the MFC patch
is mounted symmetrically on the shell with the piezo-
electric fibers following the circumferential direction,
an excitation of the second mode is hard to active. The
vibration amplitude at the second resonance frequency
is low due to this reason. This leads to insignificant
participation of the second mode shape in the total

Limitations of the electromechanical model
The FEM model developed does not take into account
any non-linearity, and despite the good agreement
between the numerical and experimental results shown,
the model has to be used with caution. An experimental
example is given in Figure 13, where the amplitude of
the input signal sent to the MCF actuator influences
the maximum amplitudes of the resonances as well as
the frequencies at which they occur. Generally speak-
ing, the higher the MCF input voltage is, the higher the
resonance peaks and the lower the resonance frequen-
cies are. In Figure 13 the eight different FRFs are dis-
played based on voltage values ranging from 60 to 300
V, that is, from low voltage amplitudes in dark blue to
high voltage amplitudes in light blue.
Piezoelectric materials are well known for their non-
linear properties (see for example Peng and Chen,
2013, or Damjanovic, 1999), but with a voltage level of
300, well below the 1500 voltage specified by the sup-
plier, the piezoelectric material is still in the linear
range. In Figure 14 zoom views are seen of the first and
third resonance peaks. This is a well-known geometric
non-linearity for shells associated with large vibration
amplitudes. Much literature exists on the subject, for
example Selmane and Lakis (1997), who show a change
in the resonance frequencies for a thin shell, at vibra-
tion amplitude levels equivalent to the thickness of the
shell. The vibration amplitude at the first resonance fre-
quency for the highest voltage level is approximately
five times the thickness, in other words, the force gener-
ated by the MFC patch is large enough to excite the
geometric non-linearities of the shell structure.
Conclusions
The electromechanical finite element model developed
based on an eight-node double curved layered shell ele-
ment takes the effects of piezoelectric fibers and IDEs
mounted on the shell into account. The new electrome-
chanical finite element only requires one extra degree of
freedom for each IDE when compared to the purely
mechanical equivalent element, since the internal effects
of the piezoelectric patch are included via Guyan reduc-
tion. Good agreement between experimental and theo-
retical FRFs are shown in a large frequency range from
0.5 to 450 Hz, that is, up to the fifth natural frequency.
Natural frequencies are predicted with high accuracy
and the deviations are smaller than 2% when using
impact testing and 4% when using the MCF as an
actuator. The six first mode shapes obtained experi-
mentally agree very well with the theoretical ones, with
the exception of the second mode shape, a pure twisting
mode, which can hardly be excited by the MFC symme-
trically mounted on the shell. The electromechanical
finite element model developed is able to resemble the
linear dynamic behavior of the system very accurately.
Figure 12. The six first experimental and numerical mode
shapes projected onto a vertical plane parallel to the clamped
edge. The green color in the experimental mode shapes
corresponds to no movement, hence nodal lines are green. The
red color corresponds to maximum movement, which can
either be inward or outward. The vertical direction in the
experimental mode shapes corresponds to the circumferential
direction, and the horizontal direction corresponds to the axial
direction. (a) First experimental mode shape. (b) Second

A
Further experimental evidence shown in the article
leads to the conclusion that the behavior of the electro-
mechanical system depends non-linearly on the ampli-
tude of the input signal sent to the MCF actuator and
the linear model must be used with caution. The higher
the MCF input voltage is, the higher the resonance
peaks and the lower the resonance frequencies are.

Appendix A1
Characterization of Proximity Probe
Experimental work is often associated with calibration of sensors and actuators.
This is important to rely heavily on the measurements from these sensor to estimate
the physical parameters governing the system of interest. In this project the purpose is
to measure the displacement of the vibrating structure, without conﬂicting the system
dynamics. Consequently a proximity probe that measures the relative displacement
contactless between the sensor and the structure is used. The vibrating structure causes
eddy currents in turn to produce an alternating magnetic ﬁeld that induces a current in
the sensors receiver coil Young and Freedman 2012, page 974-975. The ampliﬁer of the
sensor convert this current to a change in voltage that can be related to displacement.
However one must link the measured voltage to a physical parameter to get the output
in the desired physical units. In many cases a faulty calibration can cause confusion
about the system dynamics in terms of defective non linearities, and a downscaling of
system performance around a desired operating point in terms of control. Moreover the
use of modern control theory, here in terms of optimal control, requires knowledge of
the stochastic behaviour of the measurement noise. Finally remarks will be done based
on external inﬂuence causing additional noise in the system. It is thus emphasized the
importance and quality of the characterisation in order to do a fair analysis.
1
2
3
Figure A1.1: Calibration test rig for the proximity sensors:
1 MEGGITT QT442
proximity sensor, 2 calibration drum, 3 micrometer
==PAGE:158==
142
A1 Characterization of Proximity Probe
A1.1
Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
To facilitate the calibration a small set up is used as shown in Figure A1.1. It consist
of a rigid structure where at one end is attached the proximity sensor from Vibrometer
MEGGITT QT442, and in the other end a micrometer screw. The two components is
ﬁxed, such that the micrometer can vary the distance between the calibration drum
and the sensor tip. This distance is controlled by the positioning of the micrometer
screw. Hereby the voltage that is imposed by the relative distance can be related to
a reference displacement that balances the offset. The material used in the drum is
highly dependent on the sensitivity of the sensor. This causes a measurement range
between 1-4 mm, strongly dependent on the electrical resistivity, that deﬁnes the
materials ability to transport electrons. Here a ferromagnetic material will be more
likely to induce eddy currents, whereas a material as aluminium is less likely. Taking
these considerations into account a initial measurement set up is carried out for the
materials listed below
• Inconel (Top foil)
• Aluminium alloy 7075 (Rotor)
The calibration executed with a series of experiments carried out at a duration
time of 60 s. For each measurement the displacement is held constant at a change of
distance ∆d = 0.1mm spanning from 0 mm - the maximum displacement deﬁned by
the maximum output voltage of the sensor. The displacement is deﬁned as di, with the
correlating voltage ui. The duration time of 60s, as well as a sampling frequency of
2000 Hz ensure enough measurements to average noise out of the signal.
Initial the signal is scaled with a gain of a = 1, leaving the SI unit of the signal in
voltage V. The block diagram for the measurements is shown in Figure A1.2.
Finally the governing equations for the gain factor at a given position can be deﬁned
according to evaluation of equation (A1.1)
ai = ui
di
(A1.1)
The procedure is to evaluate if the voltage scales proportional to the displacement
hence making the displacement linear. In this case ai = a, ∀i ∈N Alternatively a
arbitrary function must be ﬁtted to evaluate the displacement as a function of the
induced voltage.

Top Foil Calibration
The drum used for calibrating the top foil is made from polymer to which is attached
a ﬂat piece of the top foil with an adhesive wax. It makes it possible to obtain the voltage
as a function of the displacement, see Figure A1.3(a). By investigating the measured
values of the gain that correlates the reference displacement with the measured voltage,
it is observed that a n’th order function can be used to the ﬁt. However a simple linear
polynomial of type
y = ax + b
predict the displacement quite well. With this simplest possible correlation, it is
assumed that the intersection with the y-axis is zero. Doing this one can estimate the
displacement in the range of y12B1 ∈[0.3 −2.8] mm with the sensitivity gain as seen
in equation (A1.2).

Based on interpretations of the residues graph it is seen that if the displacement
should be predicted with an error less that 5% the range must be between y12B1 ∈
[0.7 −2.9] mm. However the calibration proves that the displacement will never
change sign with the chosen values except from measurements y12B1 ∈[0 −0.1] mm,
eliminating the potential of making a control system unstable.One must however take
this effect of the biased displacement signal into account, even though the vibrations
of the top foil is restrained only to small amplitudes, and the sensor is mounted in the
linear range of the gain factor. In conclusion the block diagram of Figure A1.2, can be
used to measure the displacement of the top foil.
Note on the material: The top foil is manufactured in the material Inconel. Inconal
is a austenite nickel-chromium-based alloys, used for its superior capabilities against
oxidation, corrosion resistance and operability in extreme environment subjected to
pressure and heat as in the case of air bearing Wikipedia 2016.

drum and the rotor had alloys with same weight percentage. Again the output voltage
can be obtained unscaled by use of the block diagram in Figure A1.2. The results of

A1.1 Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
147
Noise Characterisation
For many control purposes it is relevant to characterise the noise level governing
the system. This section outline a procedure for this characterisation of measurement
noise which is the most straightforward to evaluate. It requires a isolated measuring
system, such that the sensor response is governed by a DC-levet with an imposed noise
level from the sensor. For this experiment it must be emphasized that a proper initial
set up is key to success.
Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
The test rig used for the experiment is the calibration rig shown previously in
Figure A1.1. This ensures a ﬁxed reference position between the sensor tip and the
target object. The proceeding steps is to set up the measurement software with the
calibrated sensitivity of the sensor, ensuring physical relation to the measured signal.
The procedure for the noise characterisation is to carry out a measurement series
of length T ≈2 minutes known as a random experiment with the signal y(t). T
is chosen due to convergence with its mean value and variance as t →∞. Please
note that convergence is assumed since the sampling frequency Fs = 2000 Hz gives a
sampling time ∆t = 5 × 10−4 s ≪T, and that the process is stationary hence the mean
value and the variance is time independent. Finally based on the obtained dataset
a partitioning of the measurement can be carried out and illustrated in a histogram,
whereas a theoretical ﬁt to the histogram can be calculated which in this section is
based on a normal Gaussian distribution.
Governing Equations
The logged measurement ﬁle is from this point known as the random variable X,
which domain is the sample space Ωwith continuous or discrete real numbers. In
order to describe the random variable its Probability Density Function (PDF), f (x) and
Cumulative Distribution Function (CDF), F(x) must be obtained. To do this the mean
value µ and the variance σ2 must be evaluated, which is done with the values of the
stochastic variable X denoted by xi, according to
µ = 1
N
N
X
i=i
xi,
,
σ2 =
1
N −1
N
X
i=i
|xi −µ|2
(A1.7)
Here N is a scalar equal to the number of discrete number of observations in the
measurement. If the time dependent variable y12B1(t) = X(t) is plotted, see Figure
A1.8, it is observed that the signal remain approximately within a ±3σ window of its
mean 99.73% of the time. Henceforth random variable can be estimated with gaussian
distribution Hendricks et al. 2008. See Table A1.1 for the probability measures.
The form of the Gaussian distribution is given by the equation (A1.8), which is
known as the probability density function
fX(x) =

2 mm.
It is important to note that the integral of the PDF is equal to 1 indicating the all
observations from the sample space is included.
Z ∞
−∞
fX(x) dx = 1
(A1.9)
It is easy to evaluate fX(x) based on the mean value µ, and standard deviation
σ. However for a fair comparison between the theoretical PDF and the discrete
observations the random variable must be processed to obtain the discrete PDF. The
sample space of the discrete random variable can both be continues and discrete
which can be evaluated if the variable is sorted in descending order, and normalized
with the number of observations. This gives the discrete Cumulative Distribution
Function obtained in Figure A1.9(a). In the ﬁgure the y axis represents the normalized
numbers of occurrence, where 1 represents the complete experimental outcome. It is
observed that the sample space is divided inton subset different from N. Consequently
a histogram containing n blocks can be obtained. Finally a normalization that satisfy
the constraint of equation (A1.9) must be performed according to
fX(x) =

Pr(X = xi)δ(x −xi)
(A1.10)
Here Pr(X = xi) indicates the probability of the random variable X being equal to
xi, while δ is the Dirac delta function. For a correct implementation of noise in control
purposes, simulations must be held up against the discrete sampling, to ensure correct

It can be concluded that the noise from the Vibrometer MEGGITT QT442 sensor can
be characterised by Gaussian noise, due to the good correlation between the discrete
occurrence of the sensor signal and the theoretical estimate of the Gaussian density
function. Moreover it is seen that noise can be well implemented in SIMULINK using a
block diagram equivalent to Figure A1.10.
Finally the level of whiteness must be evaluated for both the simulation and the
discrete random variable. This is done based on a autocorrelation sequence of the
random variable obtained by the MATLAB function xcorr. The results is shown in
Figure A1.11. The autocorrelation function given by CX X is known to carry import-
ant information about the frequency content of the random variable. Generally the
autocorrelation contain information between time instances s and t, from the random
variable. The autocorrelation will hence only depend on the lag between s and t, defed
as τ = s −t. In general two deﬁnitions can be made [Galeazzi 2014].

• If CX X(τ) decreases rapidly with τ then the process changes rapidly with time
• If CX X(τ) decreases slowly with τ then the process changes slowly with time
By inspection of CX X(τ) in Figure A1.11 it is seen that CX X(τ) decreases rapidly with
τ changing from 0-1. When τ > 1 almost zero or little change occur in CX X(τ). This
indicate that very little correlation occur between two sampling instances in sequence
of each other. Moreover it is seen that CX X(0) is large compared to any other values of
τ. This indicating that the noise of the sensor signal and the simulations resembles a
white Gaussian distribution with a constant amplitude spectrum. Moreover the signal
has almost inﬁnite energy due to this rapid change. This conclusion is basic for use
of stochastic design equations for optimal control with Kalman ﬁlters Hendricks et al.
2008.
Noise Disturbances
To evaluate if the measurements is contaminated with systematic noise, an extensive
analysis must be performed. Systematic noise can originate from different sources
which in this case can arise from aliasing, grid frequency disturbance or electromagnetic
radiation imposing electric ﬁelds around the motor coils which can be picked up by
the receiver coil of the proximity probe. The analysis of this section is carried out at a
sampling frequency of 2000 Hz, which is manly due to aliasing problems.
Isolated Conditions of Proximity Probe
During this extensive analysis it has been discovered that measurements with the
displacement sensor; Vibrometer MEGGITT QT442, does not contain grid noise on
the main signal. This is seen in the Fast Fourier Transformation of Figure A1.12(a),
obtained under isolated conditions. It leads to the conclusion that the electrical wiring
in the electrical hardware rack is optimal, and does not pick up noise from power
supply’s and hence the grid. However an extended analysis must be carried out to
facilitate if this is governing under operation with inﬂuence from other electrical
components. Generally a sensor must work unaffected by the surrounding electronics
in the experiments, without picking up grid frequencies or electromagnetic radiation
in the receiver coil. The sensor is intended to operate continuously with inﬂuence of
other components hereunder the piezoelectric patches, and the motor drive, why this
is studied.
==PAGE:167==
A1.1 Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
151
Effect of Piezoelectric Ampliﬁer on the Proximity Probe
It is discovered when the piezoelectric ampliﬁer is turned on, that the proximity
sensor is picking up response from the grid frequency with odd integers of 50 Hz,
see Figure A1.12(b). This is observed when the ampliﬁer is turned on and is not
operating the piezoelectric patch. However as the piezoelectric patch is operated to
excite the top foil, the magnitude of vibrations in the displacement measurement is
dominant compared to the magnitude of the noise signal. Consequently this effect can
be neglected.
Effect of Electromagnetic Radiation on the Proximity Probe
A ﬁnal effect that can cause erroneous measurements and impose noise in the
receiver coil of the proximity probe is electromagnetic radiation from the motor. Due
to the design of the test rig, the displacement sensors is unfortunately placed too close
to the motor. This causes problematic ampliﬁcation of the sensor signal which is seen
as beats in Figure A1.13(a). By investigation of a frequency response analysis it reveal
that the sensor is picking up the drive frequency at ≈0.1 Hz of the motor controlled
by the frequency converter. Moreover it is seen that a aliasing effect is present with a
frequency of approximately 370 Hz, see Figure A1.13(b). It is though important to
note that the imposed noise level given by yemr is present in a beating phenomena in
the order of 1.5 µm opposed on the main noise signal yn.
In order to evaluate if the effect can be neglected for the analysis in the project, the

when the rotor is coupled.

Appendix A2
Bonding Piezoelectric Patch to Top Foil
Proper adhesive between the piezoelectric patch and the top foil must be ensured.
The bonding must be able to withstand dynamic loading, whereby fatigue fracture of
the bonding is avoided. This section covers the procedure of the joining method and
gives a brief overview of uncertainties involved in the process.
(a)
(b)
Figure A2.1: Experimental representation of joining of the piezoelectric actuator for
the top foil. The adhesion is performed by a vacuum bagging technique.
A2.1
Vacuum Bagging Motivation
The purpose of the joining is to impose force from the piezoelectric actuator to the
top foil hence linking the dynamics response of the structure to the electrical actuator.
Such a system is known as a electromechanical system. It is extremely important to
perform an optimal adhesion between the piezoelectric MFC patch and the top foil
to avoid uncertainties in the scope of the project. The concept of optimal adhesion is
emphasized through maximizing the strain level in the top foil when actuation of the
piezoelectric patch is performed. Henceforth a set of conditions must be satisﬁed.
The ﬁrst condition is deﬁned by a complete adhesion of the area between the actuator
==PAGE:172==
156
A2 Bonding Piezoelectric Patch to Top Foil
and the top foil. There should be no air trapped in the joining. Moreover the thickness
of the adhesive glue must be as thin as possible to avoid any strain level to be absorbed
in the connection. In addition the adhesive is a polymer that is neglected in the
mathematical modelling, which in the presence can contribute with additional damping,
and create non-linear coupling of the piezoelectric actuation.
To accommodate these needs the most optimal joining method is a vacuum bagging
technique. It has many advantages that can eliminate or minimize malfunction of the
electromechanical system. This section outline the procedure for the joining which are
listed in paragraph form.
Procedure
1. Initial preparation of the top foil must be carried out. This is done by a slightly
sanding of the surface. Note that a glass-blasting of the surface is too rough
and cause severe deformation of the top foil, and must at all times be avoided.
Degrease and clean the top foil.
2. Place the top foil on the positive part of the mold as seen in Figure A2.1. It is
necessary to apply wax on the mold to prevent adhesion between the top foil and
the mold. The positive mold function as support structure to avoid deformation
of the top foil when vacuum is applied.
3. Degrease the MFC patch, and subsequently cover the top surface with a self
adhesive (non-stick) foil to prevent adhesive from covering the electrodes.
4. Select a proper resin (e.g. 3M Scotch-WeldTM DP 490). Apply the resin such that
it covers the entire bottom surface of the MFC patch. Apply additional resin on
the area of the top foil where the patch should be positioned. Ensure a thin and
uniform amount, excess resin is not of concern. Position the patch on the top foil,
and press the 2 parts together. Place the joined parts on the positive mold on
top of a breather cloth, in the vacuum bag (a non-stick mat). The breath cloth
ensures that the vacuum is applied uniform.
5. Turn on the vacuum pump, and ensure tight connection with the tube, the
vacuum bag and the supporting structure. Prevent the vacuum bag from sharp
edges of the top foil and the mold to avoid puncture.
6. As the air is removed from the chamber, precisely align the MFC patch. Use a
resin squeezing rod and roll over the patch, starting from the center and work
your way to the edges of the MFC. Squeeze as much resin and contaminated air
out between the MFC and the top foil to enhance the joining.
7. When the vacuum is applied satisfactory and the MFC is aligned perfectly, seal it
and cure the epoxy for 24 hours. The approximately pressure in the vacuum bag
should be less than 0.8 bar.
8. Remove the parts, and clean the excess adhesive on the top foil with a razor
blade. Use precaution and avoid damaging the MFC.
9. Continue the same pattern, with as many repetitions as desired.
==PAGE:173==
A2.1 Vacuum Bagging Motivation
157
For more information, se the piezoelectric patch manufacturer instructions from
Smart Materials 2015.
Critical Remarks
Under the manufacturing procedure, one top foil was glass-blasted which caused
severe deformation of the structure. The deformation was manually tried to be com-
pensated for by reshaping the top foil to the speciﬁed tolerances of the curvature.
In general it was hard to make a joining with high repeatability and little variation.
During initiating experiments the MFC patch was joined by use of Loctite super glue.
However it proves to resist dynamic loading very poor, and the cohesiveness of the
joining decreased over time. More over a uniform pressure cannot be applied over the
MFC under the adhesion procedure, hence creating a non uniform joining thickness. It
is therefore strongly discouraged to use.
==PAGE:174==
==PAGE:175==
Appendix A3
Identification of Top Foil Dynamic
Three electromechanical top foils has been manufactured, to construct a complete
active air bearing with piezoelectric actuation. The content of this appendix covers
dynamic identiﬁcation of the three different top foils in order to evaluate the sensitivity
of the system towards the mathematical model. In this way it is encouraged if an
individual adjustment of the mathematical model should be performed to optimize the
model prediction for each top foil. Moreover the impact of the piezoelectric ampliﬁer
is investigated, to see if it has any inﬂuence of the dynamic response.
A3.1
Top Foil System Dependency
The active air bearing system is operated by interaction between three identical
independent top foils. For the simplicity of this study it is assumed, that the air
ﬁlm forces acting on the structure and cause a eccentricity of the rotor is neglected.
The control design of the coherent system is thus highly dependent on the dynamic
predicted by the mathematical model deﬁned for each individual top foil. Based on
the mathematical model damping can be applied in terms of control with electrical
actuation. Though all three top foils are described by the same mathematical model,
discrepancies can occur in the dynamics, of the three different physical system. This
can cause deviation between the model and the physical system that potentially can
degrade the performance and robustness of the application, due to control design
which is based on optimal model prediction. In order better to understand the, system
dependent variables that can cause discrepancies between the top foil dynamic, a
individual study is performed. The control design can hereby be treated with the
necessary precaution, to adjust the model for each top foil.
The variables that can cause change in the top foil dynamic can be linked to geometrical
constraints related to the joining with the piezoelectric patch, the shape of the top foil
and ﬁnally to non-linearities in the piezoelectric properties. Therefore a sensitivity
study is carried out, varying one parameters at the time to link the change in dynamic.
==PAGE:176==
160
A3 Identiﬁcation of Top Foil Dynamic
Experimental Apparatus and Procedure
Excitation with white Gaussian noise, equivalent for all three top foils, is imposed
with the piezoelectric patch (see appendix A4 for the excitation procedure). One top
foil is clamped in the wrench at the time, and the displacement response of the top foil is
attempted to be positioned identical for all three systems. Small variations is discovered
due to tolerance deviation coming from the joining manufacturing procedure, see
appendix A2. The structure of each top foil was adjusted to cope with the maximum
measurement range between the sensor tip and the target. The change was both in the
shell curvature radius, and in the inclination angle of the ﬁxed boundary. The results
of the experiments is obtained in the frequency response function in Figure A3.1.

It is in general discovered that little variation occur between the three top foils in
the range from 0-150 Hz The most signiﬁcant change in this range, is the amplitude
of vibration that is larger for the third compared to the ﬁrst. This can be caused due
to change in the ﬁlm thickness of the adhesive resin used to join the top foil and the
piezoelectric patch. Regarding the natural frequencies no change occur in the ﬁrst
around 28 Hz. However the natural frequency around 120 Hz, seems to change by
approximately 7 %, from one another. Above 150 Hz, both resonances, anti resonances
as well as the general amplitude of vibration changes signiﬁcantly. All though the
response is presented in logarithmic scale it is clear that the response of the three foils
is signiﬁcantly different. It is thus emphasized an adjustment of the mathematical
model for each top foil to predict the dynamic most accurate beneﬁcial in the control
design.
Please note that each foil has an coherent output channel on the ampliﬁer. Top foil
1 on channel 1, top foil 2 on channel 2 and so forth . In order to investigate if the
signiﬁcant change is due to dynamics from the ampliﬁer, top foil 1 is operated by each
individual output channel of the ampliﬁer to see if any change occur. The results is
obtained in Figure A3.2. No noteworthy inﬂuence is observed, hence all top foils can
be operated independently with the three output channels, however precaution must
be given to which channel is operating the top foil with individual tuned mathematical
model.

Appendix A4
Excitation Method of Top Foil
This section elaborates on the signal generating module, which is used by the amp-
liﬁers to excite the structure. The excitation signal can either be governed by a random
excitation with white Gaussian noise, a frequency sweep known as a chirp or ﬁnally
a step change. The ampliﬁers in this project is used to operate the electromagnetic
shakers and the piezoelectric patches, however the main focus in this section has been
on the piezoelectric patches but could be equally applied for the shakers.
In general, the natural frequencies of the ampliﬁer is much higher compared to the
system dynamic. The ampliﬁers is consequently considered free of dynamic.
A4.1
Excitation Module in Simulink
As just mentioned the excitation signal can be varied between three different types.
When the actuators is operated the monitored signal does not contain signiﬁcant noise
from the ampliﬁer, why this factor is neglected. The three types is known as,
1. Step, change in constant DC-level
2. Chirp - Frequency sweep
3. Random excitation - White Gaussian Noise
Common to all types is two main parameters that, which can be varied independent
to meet the needs associated with different experiments. Here the amplitude of the
signal and the bias of the signal in terms of constant DC-level is governed. Due to the
coupling between the piezoelectric patch and the top foil, the dynamic of the system
change according to the settings of these parameters in the excitation signal. This
is caused by geometrical dependency of natural frequencies, and non linearities in
the piezoelectric physical parameters. Based on the linear mathematical model, no
precaution is made accordingly and any pretension or reshaping causes the model to
deviate. This downgrade the performance and robustness of the control that is based
on a optimal model prediction. It is thus of great importance to know the parameters
associated with the signal of excitation to conclude on a more fair basis if operation is
performed outside the adjusted performance level.
Initially the SIMULINK block diagram of the excitation module is presented in Figure
A4.1. The operator change the signal type with the Shaker switch.
