Step Change
The excitation signal, step change, is shown in Figure A4.2. The signal u changes
from u1 to u2 at a desired time instance t1.
Chirp - Frequency Sweep
The excitation signal is a chirp function, as show in Fingure A4.3. It contain a
sinusoidal signal with a varying period such that the signal maps frequencies from
ωmin →ωmax into the signal in the time from tperiod →ton. The amplitude is given by
A = u+ −u−. The signal is restrained with energy in the range of the frequency band,
why this type of signal in most cases is avoided.
Random Excitation - Frequency Sweep
This signal is driven by white Gaussian noise. In theory it contains inﬁnite energy
at all frequencies, why this type of signal is preferably. The time response of the
signal is shown in Figure A4.4(a), normalized with the standard deviation σ and the
mean value µ. These probability measures characterise the excitation signal, presented
by a normal distribution as shown in the normalized probability density function in
Figure A4.4(b). It is important to know the level of amplitude, to operation within the
speciﬁed range of the ampliﬁer. Consequently it is proposed by Hendricks et al. 2008
that the signal of a random variable χ remain within a ±iσ window of its mean value
a certain percentage of the time. Here i indicate a integer between 1 and 3, with the
results shown in equation (A4.1). The results is the integral from
R iσ
−iσ fx(χ).
Pr(|χ(t) −m| ≤σ) = 0.6827
Pr(|χ(t) −m| ≤2σ) = 0.9545
Pr(|χ(t) −m| ≤3σ) = 0.9973
(A4.1)
The notation of the mean value µ and the standard deviation σ is presented with
N (µ,σ). Figure A4.5, represents three different random excitation signals. It is
concluded that mean value µ is equivalent with the bias level on the excitation signal,
while the standard deviation at ±3σ gives the most accurate estimate of the amplitude.

Appendix A6
FFT Module
On-line readings of the frequency content in a recorded signal is provided by the
FFT module presented in the SIMULINK block diagram. It is based on calculations of a
Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT), and can be selected for any given signal

Appendix A8
Time Series Validation - Steady State
Results
This Appendix deal with time series analysis, and validation of the model, due
to steady state response of a constant sinusoidal signal. This is performed both ex-
perimentally as well as theoretically using excitation at different frequencies with an
amplitude of the input signal u = 160 V. The reduced model, is made for implementa-
tion in SIMULINK, and a simple set-up showing the block diagram of the linear time
invariant system (2.31) is shown in Figure A8.1. The SIMULINK model is used with
a ﬁxed step Runge-Kutta solver, at a time step of Ts = 1e −5s. This is selected to
avoid aliasing at frequencies within the range of interest for the mathematical model.
Moreover the reduced model is in continuous time, in terms of control, and includes
the ﬁrst 5 modes with an asymmetry imposed in the force vector.
Figure A8.1: Simple simulink setup for numeric simulation of dynamic response
The results of the time series analysis compared with the experimental results
is shown in Figure A8.2, and A8.3. The analysis of the results is primary to distin-
guish between the mathematical as well as the experimental on possible phase shifts
difference in amplitude and non-linear periodicities.

Figure A8.3: Dynamic steady state response obtained for different frequencies and a
amplitude of the input signal at 160 V
As was found in section 2.3 with the FRF, approximately the same response is
obtained. This is seen when comparing the phase angle of the theoretical and the
experimental results in the interval of 0 - 68 Hz (i.e. the phase shift between two peaks
==PAGE:192==
176
A8 Time Series Validation - Steady State Results
of the displacement). For these frequencies both the amplitude, and the phase angle
correlate extremely well. This is both seen in terms of the amplitude at resonance
around 28.1 Hz as well as for the antiresonance at 52 Hz. Moreover it is seen how the
phase angle between the input signal and the output signal behave. At resonance there
is a 90 ◦phase shift, while for the lower frequencies < 28 Hz a 0 ◦phase shift occur.
Similar interpretation can be done for the frequencies in the range 68-135 Hz. It is seen
that 30 ◦phase shift exist between the two solutions in the FRF exactly as the dynamic
steady state response represents. This indicate that the proportional damping modelled
into the system, is not fully governing the true damping in the system. Considering the
response at 134 Hz, here the amplitude deviates from the mathematical model. Keep
this frequency in mind!. In terms of the higher frequencies both the phase angle and
the magnitude of the response starts to deviate with a larger and larger portion. This
is caused by the mathematical model starts to deviate from the experimental results.
Discrepancies can be seen where the theoretical model has a lower amplitude than
the experimental results or vice versa. This emphasized the necessity of improving
the model, in case the model is not accurate when optimal control design is to be
implemented. This is especially important for spillover effects and stability problems.
However the results is very well replicated by the mathematical model in the interval
of 0-500 Hz using the prescribed amplitude of the input signal, and the dynamic is
captured well. The topic of improving the mathematical model may be reviewed as
the scope of further work and is not addressed further. Finally it is seen that 450 Hz is
close to the maximum comparable results since the experimental sampling frequency
of 2000 Hz is used. However the 2000 Hz sampling frequency is considered approved
for further work.
Returning to the results obtained at 10 Hz and 134 Hz a non-linear response is obtained.
This is seen in the amplitude of vibration that has an non-sinusoidal form. Both
responses are periodic, however at 10 Hz the structure respond with a frequency twice
the frequency of the input signal, while at 134 Hz only 1/2 the input signal. At 134 Hz,
very close to the 3’rd natural frequency its seen that the mode of vibration resembles
the 1 natural frequency. These effects are however only governed at the particular
frequencies, and may be neglected further from this point. It is thought a problem to
be aware of in case spill over effects will be present.
==PAGE:193==
Appendix A9
Initial Top foil Coupling to Rotor -
Results
This appendix outlines initial experimental test that show response characteristics of
the top foil coupled to the rotor system. It contain a perspective toward the piezoelectric
actuation whereas two passive approaches is dealt with. One emphasize the need
of run-out compensation. Another emphasize an elaborate study of the change in
piezoelectric actuation, and its dependency on change in natural frequency. It is
assessed to create a benchmark and motivate an optimization of the mathematical
model to account for the air ﬁlm forces, hereby allowing introduction of damping and
attenuation of the system response by means of active control. The two studies are
found in A9.1 and A9.2 which are based on forced response.
==PAGE:194==
178
A9 Initial Top foil Coupling to Rotor - Results
A9.1
Top Foil Dynamics Coupled to Aero Dynamics
This experiment is carried out with a non-precise positioning of the top foil relative
to the test set up shown in Figure 5.1. The experiments is carried out at different angular
velocities to study the effect of the unbalanced response and residual misalignment of
the rotor center. It result is an excitation of the top foil equivalent to 1X synchronous
2X and 3X subsynchronous vibrations with frequencies equivalent to integers of the
angular velocity.

A9.2
Stiffness Dependence on Shell Curvature
Strong coupling between the rotor and the top foil. The results of the experiments
are too complicated to be validated against numerical calculations. Hence the results in
ﬁgure A9.2 only gives a preliminary investigation of the increased equivalent damping
and stiffness coefﬁcients as the top foil is moved closer to the rotor. The results are
based on a constant angular velocity of 8000 rpm of the rotor.

==PAGE:197==
Simulink Diagrams S1
Numerical and Experimental
Implementation
The following contains the SIMULINK diagram of the standard LQG controller as
well as the diagram of the LQG controller with integral action. The content shows
both the diagram used to obtain the numerical results, as well as the diagram used
for the experimental implementation. Please note that the experimental implementa-
tion work in conjunction with the software ControlDesk from dSpace. It requires
that the main MATLAB program Control_Model.m is executed along with either the
LQR_control.m or LQRi_control.m subprogram. By either of the choice the stand-
ard controller or the one with integral action will be implemented respectively. Please
note that a set of multiple subfunctions and subprograms must be loaded to the current
folder when executing.
==PAGE:198==

==PAGE:201==
Workshop Drawings D1
Workshop Drawings of the Ø85 Rotor
The initial set up of the modular high speed spindle included a rotor with shaft
diameter of Ø50 mm. The scope of this project was however intended to deal with
testing of a Ø85 mm rotor supported by an air foil bearing of associated size. It was
not possible to buy a pre fabricated rotor with the desired diameter, why a custom
made shaft needed to be fabricated.The spindle was consequently retroﬁtted with a
rotor assembled by a standard HSK socket, connected with a conical interface to the
enlarged shaft diameter to reach the desired rotor diameter.
This procedure has been expensive, time demanding, and has been associated with
defective errors of the test rig due to eccentricity between the rotor center and the
spindle center. The eccentricity is attained during fabrication, and it is agreed with the
supplier that the rotor is remade to comply with the tolerances of the drawings. Please
note that the enlarged rotor is made with a interior of stainless steel, and a exterior of
high strength aluminium alloy 7075 to prevent erroneous effects associated with run
out compensation.

==PAGE:205==
Workshop Drawings D2
Safety Shield for High Speed Spindle
To prevent hazardous work with the high speed spindle a safety shield has been
manufactured. This ensures that the operator of the test rig is kept at a safe distance
to the rotating machinery, while preventing fragments from the rotor to be thrown out
at the operator. The design provides easy access to the test rig, in case retroﬁtting or
inspection of modules are to be performed.
Polycarbonate plates has been ﬁtted to a aluminium skeleton, ensuring high transpar-
ency and visibility of the ongoing experiments, while sustaining maximum ability to
absorb impact from fragments preventing the shield from being smashed.

==PAGE:211==
Workshop Drawings D3
Top Foil Test Module
One of the main modules in the project is the multifunctional Top Foil Test Module.
It enables the necessary boundary conditions to be imposed onto the top foil. Here
the supporting structure is considered as being rigid, with natural frequencies much
higher that the natural frequencies of the system. Moreover it allow the operator to
either couple or decouple the top foil dynamic with the rotor and hence the dynamics
of the air ﬁlm forces. This is ensured with the test module being able to move in 2
degree of freedom. One translational, normal to the rotor, and one rotational around
the center line of the rotor. Henceforth the operator can adjust both the the gap size
of the top foil relative to the rotor, and the rate dependent clearance of the top foil
relative to the rotor.

==PAGE:221==
Workshop Drawings D4
Active Piezoelectric Air Bearing Test
Module
The Active Piezoelectric Air Bearing Test Module comply with the new rotor, and
includes several of highly thought-out solutions to accommodate all needs with sensors
and actuators in the system. The purpose of the test rig is to continue validation and
explorative work with the research and development of a full electro mechanical Air
Bearing system. The housing is made from high strength Aluminium alloy 7075, and
has a mass moment of inertia placed strategically to avoid moments to be applied to
the bearing dynamics when ﬁtted onto the module base of the high speed spindle.
The parts assembled to the main housing allow forces to be applied to the bearing by
means of the force applying devices. Moreover it allows installations of shakers on the
structure, while monitoring of physical variables is enabled.

==PAGE:231==
Workshop Drawings D5
Force Applying Device
A retroﬁt of the force applying devices has been performed. The retroﬁt is necessary
when experiments with the Active Piezoelectric Air Bearing and the high speed spindle
is conducted. It consists of a force transducer, permanent magnets and the supporting
main housing initial design by PhD student Bo Bjerregaard Nielsen. The module is
manufactured due to geometrical constraints of the high speed test rig.
